Refuting the Denial of General Possible as a Proposition
Refutation of the claim that the general possible is not an actual proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to defend the status of al-mumkina al-`amma as a genuine proposition using the argument that possibility is a mode of relation and relation implies affirmation.

Core Idea
A proposition does not require actuality; even possibility implies affirmation.

This passage addresses a misconception that the general possible proposition is not a real proposition because it lacks a definite judgment. The author argues that possibility is a mode of the relation between subject and predicate, and the relation itself is rooted in affirmation. Thus, the possible proposition does contain a judgment, albeit a weak one. Understanding this is crucial for grasping the full scope of modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The group says al-mumkina al-`amma is not an actual proposition because it lacks judgment. Recognize that 'actual' here means containing a definite assertion.
2
Extract the author's key premises
Possibility is a quality of the relation (kayfiyyat al-nisba), and the essence of the relation is affirmation (al-thubut). This is the core of the argument.
3
Follow the inference
Since the relation implies affirmation, any mode of relation, including possibility, involves a judgment of affirmation. Therefore, the possible proposition is a genuine proposition.
The author concedes that common understanding associates 'proposition' with actual occurrence, but that does not dismiss its logical status.
4
Apply the principle
If the possible is modal, then the absolute proposition (which is unrestricted) is even more clearly a proposition.
This extends the argument to other modal types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ : ذَهَبَ قَوْمٌ إِلَى أَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَةَ الْعَامَّةَ لَيْسَتْ قَضِيَّةٌ بِالْفِعْلِ؛ لِعَدَمِ اشْتِمَالِهَا عَلَى الْحُكْمِ، فَلَيْسَتْ مُوَجَّهَةٌ، وَذَلِكَ خَطَأٌ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ الْإِمْكَانَ كَيْفِيَّةُ النِّسْبَةِ، وَأَصْلُ النِّسْبَةِ الثُّبُوتُ. نَعَمْ، ذَلِكَ أَضْعَفُ الْمَدَارِكِ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالُوا: الْوُجُوبُ وَالاِمْتِنَاعُ دَالَّةٌ عَلَى وَثَاقَةِ الرَّابِطِ، وَالْإِمْكَانُ عَلَى ضُعْفِهَا، فَالثُّبُوتُ بِطَرِيقِ الْإِمْكَانِ نَوْعٌ مِنَ الثُّبُوتِ مُطْلَقًا. غَايَةُ الْأَمْرِ: الْمُتَبَادِرُ مِنْهُ عِنْدَ الْإِطْلَاقِ هُوَ الْوُقُوعُ عَلَى نَسْجِ الْفِعْلِيَّةِ، وَذَلِكَ لَا يَضُرُّ فِي عُمُومِهِ، كَمَا قَالُوا فِي الْوُجُودِ، وَإِذَا كَانَتِ الْمُمْكِنَةُ مُوَجَّهَةٌ فَالْمُطْلَقَةُ بِالطَّرِيقِ الْأَوْلَى.
The fourth: A group went to the claim that the general possible is not an actual proposition, because it does not contain a judgment, so it is not a modal proposition. That is an error. Do you not see that possibility is a quality of the relation, and the origin of the relation is affirmation? Yes, that is the weakest of perceptions. Hence they said: necessity and impossibility indicate the strength of the link, while possibility indicates its weakness. So affirmation in the way of possibility is a type of affirmation absolutely. The point is: what is immediately understood from it when unrestricted is occurrence in the manner of actuality, but that does not harm its generality, as they said concerning existence. And if the possible is a modal proposition, then the absolute is even more so.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage refutes the view that the general possible proposition (al-mumkina al-`amma) is not a genuine proposition because it lacks a definite judgment. The author argues that possibility is a mode (kayfiyya) of the relation between subject and predicate, and the relation itself implies affirmation (thubut). Thus, even though the possible proposition indicates a weak link, it still affirms something. The author concedes that the common understanding may associate 'proposition' with actual occurrence, but that does not negate its logical status. If the possible is modal, then the absolute (mutlaqa) is even more clearly a proposition. This underscores the principle that modal propositions include even weak affirmations.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
كيفية النسبة
الثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the general possible proposition is still a modal proposition?
A) Because it implies actuality.
B) Because possibility is a quality of the relation, and relation implies affirmation.
C) Because it is equivalent to the absolute proposition.
D) Because it has a strong link between subject and predicate.

Key Takeaway
The general possible proposition is a genuine proposition because possibility is a mode of the relation, and any relation involves affirmation (judgment).

Understanding La-dawam and La-darura
Explains la-dawam and la-darura as indicators of specific modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify la-dawam and la-darura as indicators of general absolute and general possible propositions, and explain their quality and quantity.

Core Idea
La-dawam and la-darura negate permanence and necessity to form modal propositions.

This passage introduces two key terms that modify categorical propositions to express modal nuances. Understanding them helps in analyzing Islamic legal rulings and theological statements where permanence or necessity is negated.

Guided Path
1
Locate the terms
Find the words اللادوام and اللاضرورة in the Arabic passage. Note that they are defined as 'إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة' and 'إلى ممكنة عامة' respectively.
2
Identify the function
Recognize that both terms indicate a removal (رفع) of the relation (النسبة) from the proposition. اللادوام removes permanence, اللاضرورة removes necessity.
3
Analyze quality and quantity
The passage states they differ in quality (كيفية) from what they qualify and agree in quantity (كمية). Determine: la-dawam is negative in quality (negates permanence) but universal in quantity? Actually, 'general absolute' is permissive, not universal. But the passage says they agree in quantity with what they qualify. So check the original proposition's quantity.
In modal logic, these terms modify propositions without changing the subject-predicate structure.
4
Contrast with the base proposition
Compare the modified proposition (with la-dawam or la-darura) to the original unmodified proposition. Understand how the modal nuance changes the judgment.
For example, 'man is an animal' (necessary) vs. 'man is not always an animal' (la-dawam) – the latter is a general absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْخَامِسُ : اللَّادَوَامُ إِشَارَةٌ إِلى مُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ، وَاللَّا ضَرُورَةُ إِلَى مُمْكِنَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُخَالِفَتَيْ الْكَيْفِيَّةِ وَمُوَافِقَتَيْ الْكَمِّيَّةِ لِمَا قُيِّدَ بِهِمَا، لأَنَّهُمَا رَافِعَانِ لِلنِّسْبَةِ، مِنْ غَيْرِ تَفَاوُتٍ، فَالْمُرَكَّبَةُ قَضِيَّةٌ مُتَعَدِّدَةٌ؛ لِأَنَّ الْعِبْرَةَ في وَحْدَتِهَا وَتَعَدُّدُهَا بِوَحْدَةِ الْحُكْمِ وَتَعَدُّدِهِ، وَتَعَدُّدُهُ إِمَّا بِاخْتِلَافِهِ كَيْفًا أَوْ مَوْضُوعاً أَوْ مَحْمُولًا، لَا رَابِعَ لَهَا.
The fifth: 'La-dawam' is an indication of a general absolute, and 'la-darura' of a general possible, both differing in quality and agreeing in quantity with what they are qualified by, because both remove the relation without distinction. Thus the compound proposition is a multiple proposition, because the criterion of its unity or multiplicity is the unity or multiplicity of the judgment, and its multiplicity is either by difference in mode, or subject, or predicate; there is no fourth way.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage explains the terms la-dawam (non-permanence) and la-darura (non-necessity) as referring to specific modal propositions. La-dawam indicates the general absolute (mutlaqa `amma), which affirms the predicate for some time but not always. La-darura indicates the general possible (mumkina `amma), which affirms possibility. Both are negative in quality (i.e., they negate permanence or necessity) but agree in quantity with the original they modify. The author then defines a compound proposition (murakkaba) as one that contains multiple judgments; its unity or multiplicity depends on the unity or multiplicity of the judgment. Multiplicity arises from a difference in mode, subject, or predicate—no other possibilities. For instance, in usul al-fiqh, a ruling that is necessary (wajib) and at the same time possible is a compound judgment.

Key Terms
اللادوام
اللاضرورة
المركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'la-dawam' indicate?
A) A general absolute proposition
B) A general possible proposition
C) A necessary proposition
D) A universal negative proposition

Key Takeaway
La-dawam and la-darura are modal indicators that respectively negate permanence and necessity, producing general absolute and general possible propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the proposition 'Zakat is not always obligatory on the poor' uses la-dawam or la-darura, and explain what modal nuance it adds.

Compound Propositions: Criteria for Multiplicity
Defines compound proposition and its criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a proposition is compound (murakkaba) by checking if it involves multiple judgments differing in mode, subject, or predicate.

Core Idea
A compound proposition has multiple judgments; multiplicity arises only from mode, subject, or predicate.

The passage defines compound propositions as those containing multiple judgments. This is a critical distinction in logical analysis, especially when evaluating complex statements in Islamic legal theory and theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the passage's statement: 'المركبة قضية متعددة' – the compound proposition is a multiple proposition. The unity of the proposition depends on the unity of the judgment.
2
Identify the three sources of multiplicity
The passage lists three ways a judgment can be multiple: difference in mode (كيفا), subject (موضوعا), or predicate (محمولا). Note that these are exhaustive – 'لا رابع لها' (no fourth).
Mode refers to modality like necessity, possibility, etc.
3
Apply to an example
Take a proposition like 'Salat is wajib and fasting is wajib'. This has two subjects (salat, fasting) and one predicate (wajib) – it is compound because of multiple subjects. Verify using the criteria.
4
Distinguish from simple conjunction
A compound proposition is not merely a conjunction; it must involve multiple judgments. A simple conjunction with a single judgment is not compound. For example, 'Zaid is standing and Zaid is sitting' has two judgments (different predicates) – compound.
The criterion is the judgment, not the words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْخَامِسُ : اللَّادَوَامُ إِشَارَةٌ إِلى مُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ، وَاللَّا ضَرُورَةُ إِلَى مُمْكِنَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُخَالِفَتَيْ الْكَيْفِيَّةِ وَمُوَافِقَتَيْ الْكَمِّيَّةِ لِمَا قُيِّدَ بِهِمَا، لأَنَّهُمَا رَافِعَانِ لِلنِّسْبَةِ، مِنْ غَيْرِ تَفَاوُتٍ، فَالْمُرَكَّبَةُ قَضِيَّةٌ مُتَعَدِّدَةٌ؛ لِأَنَّ الْعِبْرَةَ في وَحْدَتِهَا وَتَعَدُّدُهَا بِوَحْدَةِ الْحُكْمِ وَتَعَدُّدِهِ، وَتَعَدُّدُهُ إِمَّا بِاخْتِلَافِهِ كَيْفًا أَوْ مَوْضُوعاً أَوْ مَحْمُولًا، لَا رَابِعَ لَهَا.
The fifth: 'La-dawam' is an indication of a general absolute, and 'la-darura' of a general possible, both differing in quality and agreeing in quantity with what they are qualified by, because both remove the relation without distinction. Thus the compound proposition is a multiple proposition, because the criterion of its unity or multiplicity is the unity or multiplicity of the judgment, and its multiplicity is either by difference in mode, or subject, or predicate; there is no fourth way.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage explains the terms la-dawam (non-permanence) and la-darura (non-necessity) as referring to specific modal propositions. La-dawam indicates the general absolute (mutlaqa `amma), which affirms the predicate for some time but not always. La-darura indicates the general possible (mumkina `amma), which affirms possibility. Both are negative in quality (i.e., they negate permanence or necessity) but agree in quantity with the original they modify. The author then defines a compound proposition (murakkaba) as one that contains multiple judgments; its unity or multiplicity depends on the unity or multiplicity of the judgment. Multiplicity arises from a difference in mode, subject, or predicate—no other possibilities. For instance, in usul al-fiqh, a ruling that is necessary (wajib) and at the same time possible is a compound judgment.

Key Terms
اللادوام
اللاضرورة
المركبة

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A compound proposition is identified by multiple judgments, which can arise from differences in mode, subject, or predicate, and no other possibilities.

Hierarchy of Modal Propositions in Islamic Logic
Author lists the relative generality and specificity among various modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rank modal propositions from most general to most specific according to the traditional hierarchy.

Core Idea
The general possible is the most inclusive; the absolute necessary is the most restricted simple.

This passage provides a compact summary of how different modal propositions relate in generality. Memorizing this hierarchy helps you quickly classify any proposition and understand its logical force. The author uses induction (istiqra') to derive these relationships from the nature of the modals.

Guided Path
1
Identify simple vs compound modals
Note that some modal propositions are simple (like necessary and permanent) and some are compound (like specific possible, which combines possibility and negation of necessity).
Simple modals have one modal condition; compound ones have two.
2
Find the most general proposition
The text says 'الممكنة العامة أعم القضايا' – the general possible is the most general of all propositions.
It covers any possibility, including what is necessary or permanent.
3
Find the most specific simple
The absolute necessary (الضرورية المطلقة) is the most specific among simple propositions (أخص البسائط).
Necessity is the strongest modal condition.
4
Find the most specific compound
The specific conditional (المشروطة الخاصة) is the most specific among compound propositions (أخص المركبات).
It adds a restrictive condition beyond the basic modality.
5
Trace the intermediate relations
The text also states that absolute necessary is more specific than absolute permanent. Use this to map all relations between the six modals mentioned.
Derive: general possible > general absolute > absolute permanent > absolute necessary (among simples), and specific possible > specific conditional (among compounds).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالُوا: إِنَّ الضَّرُورِيَّةَ الْمُطْلَقَةَ أَخَصُّ مُطْلَقًا مِنَ الدَّائِمَةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ لَا يُسْتَصْعَبُ عَلَيْكَ اسْتِخْرَاجُ النَّسَبِ بَيْنَ الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ الْمَذْكُورَةِ، وَلَوِ اسْتَقْرَيْتَ عَلِمْتَ أَنَّ الْمُمْكِنَةَ الْعَامَّةَ أَعَمُّ الْقَضَايَا، وَالْمُمْكِنَةُ الْخَاصَّةُ أَعَمُّ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ. أَوْ عَدَمُ وُقُوعِ جَانِبِ الأَصْلِ، وَالْمُطْلَقَةُ الْعَامَّةُ أَعَمُّ الْفِعْلِيَّاتِ، وَالضَّرُوريَّةُ الْمُطْلَقَةُ أَخَصُّ الْبَسَائِطِ، وَالْمَشْرُوطَةُ الْخَاصَّةُ أَخَصُّ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ، عَلَى وَجْهِ فَضْلٍ
Hence they said: The absolute necessary is absolutely more specific than the absolute permanent. Then it is not difficult for you to derive the relations between the mentioned modals. If you investigate, you will know that the general possible is the most general of propositions, and the specific possible is the most general of compounds. Or non-occurrence of the side of the original. And the general absolute is the most general of actualities, and the absolute necessary is the most specific of simples, and the specific conditional is the most specific of compounds, in an excellent manner.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage summarizes the hierarchy of modal propositions. The absolute necessary (al-daruriyya al-mutlaqa) is more specific than the absolute permanent (al-da'ima al-mutlaqa). Through induction (istiqra'), one finds that the general possible (al-mumkina al-`amma) is the most general of all propositions, covering all possibilities. The specific possible (al-mumkina al-khassa) is the most general among compound propositions. The general absolute (al-mutlaqa al-`amma) is the most general among actuality propositions. The absolute necessary is the most specific among simple propositions, while the specific conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) is the most specific among compounds. This classification helps in understanding logical relationships among modals.

Key Terms
الضرورية المطلقة
الدائمة المطلقة
الممكنة الخاصة
المطلقة العامة
المشروطة الخاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Absolute necessary.
B) General possible.
C) General absolute.
D) Specific conditional.

Key Takeaway
The general possible is the most inclusive modal proposition, while the absolute necessary is the narrowest among simple propositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Types of Conditional Propositions: Muttasila and Munfasila
Author defines conditional propositions and their types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify any conditional proposition as either muttasila or munfasila and specify its subtype (lāzimiyya, ittifāqiyya, etc.).

Core Idea
Conditionals split into connected (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or), with subtypes based on necessity and exclusivity.

Conditional propositions are the backbone of hypothetical reasoning in Islamic logic. This lesson classifies them into two main families, each with distinct logical properties that determine how they function in arguments.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish muttasila vs munfasila
Look for whether the proposition expresses a conditional relation (if-then) or an exclusive relation (either-or). The text uses 'upon the assumption of another' for muttasila and 'mutual exclusion' for munfasila.
2
Identify the subtype of muttasila
Determine if the connection between antecedent and consequent is necessary (lāzimiyya), accidental (ittifāqiyya), or absolute (mutlaqa).
Necessary: the consequent always follows; accidental: they happen to co-occur; absolute: no claim about necessity.
3
Identify the subtype of munfasila
Check if the disjunction is true exclusive (haqiqiyya), only prevents both true (mani'at al-jam'), or only prevents both false (mani'at al-khulu).
Haqiqiyya: exactly one true; mani'at al-jam': at least one false; mani'at al-khulu: at least one true.
4
Apply to a new example
Take a real conditional from a source and classify it using the above distinctions.
E.g., 'If it is a human, it is mortal' is muttasila lāzimiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشَّرْطِيَّةُ إِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِنُبُوتِ نِسْبَةٍ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ أُخْرَى لُزُومًا، أَوِ اتِّفَاقًا، أَوْ إطلاقًا فَمُتَصِلَةٌ لُزُومِيَّةٌ، أَوِ اتِّفَاقِيَّةٌ، أَوْ مُطْلَقَةٌ. وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِتَنَافِي النِّسْبَتَيْنِ صِدْقًا وَكَذِبًا مَعا، أَوْ صِدْقًا فَقَطْ، أَوْ كَذِبًا فَقَطْ عِنَادًا، أو اتفاقا، أَوْ إطلاقا فَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ حَقِيقِيَّةٌ، أَوْ مَانِعَةُ الْجَمْعِ، أَوْ مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُو، عِنَادِيَّةٌ، أَوِ اتِّفَاقِيَّةُ، أَوْ مُطْلَقَةٌ. وَرُبَّمَا يُعْتَبَرُ فِي مَانِعَتَيْ الْجَمْعِ وَالْخُلُو التَّنَافِي فِي الصِّدْقِ أَوْ فِي الْكَذِبِ مُطلَقًا، وَبِهَذَا الْمَعْنَى يَكُونَانِ أَعَمَّ. وَهَذِهِ كُلُّهَا حَقَائِقُ الْمُوجَبَاتِ، أَمَّا سَوَالِبُهَا فَرَفْعُ إِيجَابَاتِهَا، فَالصَّالِبَةُ اللُّزُومِيَّةُ مَا يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِسَلْبِ اللُّزُومِ، لَا بِلُزُومِ السَّلْبِ، وَعَلَى هَذَا فَقِسْ. ثُمَّ الْحُكْمُ فِيهَا إِنْ كَانَ عَلَى تَقْدِيرٍ مُعَيَّنٍ فَمَخْصُوصَةٌ، وَإِلَّا،
The conditional proposition: if it is judged in it with the affirmation of a relation upon the assumption of another, whether necessarily, accidentally, or absolutely, then it is a connected conditional (mutasila) that is lāzimiyya (necessary connection), ittifāqiyya (accidental), or mutlaqa (absolute). And if it is judged in it with the mutual exclusion of the two relations in both truth and falsity together, or in truth only, or in falsity only, whether adversatively, accidentally, or absolutely, then it is a disjunctive conditional (munfasila) that is haqiqiyya (real), or mani'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction), or mani'at al-khulu (preventing emptiness), 'inadiyya (adversative), ittifaqiyya (accidental), or mutlaqa (absolute). And perhaps in mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khulu the mutual exclusion is considered in truth or in falsity absolutely, and in this sense they are more general. All these are the positive ones; as for their negatives, they are the raising of their affirmations. Thus the negative necessary-connected (salibat lāzimiyya) is one in which the negation of the connection is judged, not the connection of negation. And on this, analogize. Then the judgment in them, if it is upon a specific assumption, then it is specific, and if not...
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of conditional propositions, a core topic in logic. It first defines the muttasila (connected conditional), which is an if-then statement. If the connection between antecedent and consequent is necessary, it is lāzimiyya (e.g., 'If it is a human, then it is an animal' — necessary). If the connection is merely accidental (ittifāqiyya), e.g., 'If it rained, the river rose' (could be by coincidence). If absolute (mutlaqa) without specifying the type of connection. Next, the munfasila (disjunctive conditional) is an either-or statement. If it excludes both truth and falsity of both parts together (i.e., exactly one true), it is haqiqiyya (real disjunction). If it only excludes the conjunction of truth (both true together), it is mani'at al-jam' (e.g., 'Either it is day or night' — cannot both be true, but both could be false? Actually day/night example is haqiqiyya; but for mani'at al-jam', think: 'Either I am in Cairo or Alexandria' — I could be elsewhere, so only prevents both being true). If it only excludes the conjunction of falsity (both false together), it is mani'at al-khulu (i.e., at least one must be true, e.g., 'Either it is water or it is not water' — one must be true). The author adds that in mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khulu, the exclusion can be absolute, making them more general. Then the passage explains how to form negatives of these: the negative of a lāzimiyya is the negation of necessity, not the necessity of negation (i.e., 'It is not necessary that if A then B' vs. 'It is necessary that if A then not B'). Finally, the judgment can be general (if no specific assumption) or specific (makhsusa) if based on a particular assumption.

Key Terms
الشرطية المتصلة (al-shartiyya al-muttasila): A conditional proposition that asserts a relation between antecedent and consequent (if-then), with subtypes based on the nature of connection. [Note: Standard term; no shift.]
الشرطية المنفصلة (al-shartiyya al-munfasila): A conditional proposition that asserts an exclusive relation (either-or) between two propositions. [Note: Standard.]
مانعة الجمع (mani'at al-jam'): A disjunctive that prevents both parts from being true together (but allows both false). [Note: Standard; sometimes called 'man'at al-jam'.]
مانعة الخلو (mani'at al-khulu): A disjunctive that prevents both parts from being false together (i.e., at least one true). [Note: Standard.]
الحقيقية (al-haqiqiyya): A real disjunctive that prevents both truth and falsity together (exclusive disjunction). [Note: Standard in logic; the term 'haqiqiyya' here distinguishes it from other types.]
اللزومية (al-lāzimiyya): A connected conditional with a necessary relation between antecedent and consequent. [Note: Standard.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of disjunctive conditional prevents both parts from being true together but allows both to be false?
A) Haqiqiyya.
B) Mani'at al-jam'.
C) Mani'at al-khulu.
D) 'Inadiyya.

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are divided into connected and disjunctive types, each with subtypes that determine their truth conditions and logical behavior.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Classification of Conditional Propositions into Conjunctive and Disjunctive
Author defines conditional propositions and enumerates their types and negations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify conditional propositions as conjunctive or disjunctive and identify their subtypes (necessary, coincidental, absolute) as well as negate them correctly.

Core Idea
Conditionals are either conjunctive (if-then) or disjunctive (either-or), each with three modes: necessary, coincidental, and absolute.

The source passage provides a systematic classification of conditional propositions, a foundational tool in Islamic logic. You will learn to recognize the two primary types – conjunctive and disjunctive – and their finer divisions. This classification is crucial for constructing sound arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Define the conditional proposition
The passage begins by stating that a conditional judges a relation 'upon another estimation' – meaning the truth of one component depends on another. Note the terms 'luzum' (necessity), 'ittifaq' (coincidence), and 'itlaq' (absoluteness).
2
Distinguish conjunctive from disjunctive
If the judgment is about the existence of a relation, it is conjunctive (mutasila). If it is about mutual exclusion (tanafi) of the two relations, it is disjunctive (munfasila).
3
Classify conjunctive conditionals
The conjunctive is further divided into luzumiyya (necessary connection), ittifaqiyya (coincidental), and mutlaqa (absolute, without qualification).
4
Classify disjunctive conditionals
The disjunctive is divided based on the type of exclusion: haqiqiyya (real, excludes both truth and falsehood), man'at al-jam' (excludes coexistence in truth), and man'at al-khuluw (excludes both falsehood). Each also has modes of inadiyya (opposition), ittifaqiyya, and mutlaqa.
5
Understand negatives
The negative of a conditional is simply the negation of its affirmative judgment. For example, the negative of a necessary conditional negates the necessity, not the necessity of negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشَّرْطِيَّةُ إِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِنُبُوتِ نِسْبَةٍ عَلَى تَقْدِيرِ أُخْرَى لُزُومًا ، أَوْ اتِّفَاقًا، أَوْ إطلاقًا فَمُتَصِلَةٌ لُزُومِيَّةٌ، أَوِ اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ، أَوْ مُطْلَقَةٌ. وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِتَنَافِي النِّسْبَتَيْنِ صِدْقًا وَكَذِبًا مَعا ، أَوْ صِدْقًا فَقَطْ ، أَوْ كَذِبًا فَقَطْ عِنَادًا، أو اتفاقا ، أَوْ إطلاقا فَمُنْفَصِلَةٌ حَقِيقِيَّةٌ، أَوْ مَانِعَةُ الْجَمْعِ ، أَوْ مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُو، عِنَادِيَّةٌ ، أَوِ اتَّفَاقِيَّةُ، أَوْ مُطْلَقَةٌ. وَرُبَّمَا يُعْتَبَرُ فِي مَانِعَتَيْ الْجَمْعِ وَالْخُلُو التَّنَافِي فِي الصِّدْقِ أَوْ فِي الْكَذِبِ مُطلَقًا، وَبِهَذَا الْمَعْنَى يَكُونَانِ أَعَمَّ. وَهَذِهِ كُلُّهَا حَقَائِقُ الْمُوجَبَاتِ، أَمَّا سَوَالِبُهَا فَرَفْعُ إِيجَابَاتِهَا ، فَالصَّالِبَةُ اللُّزُومِيَّةُ مَا يُحْكَمُ فِيهَا بِسَلْبِ اللُّزُومِ، لَا بِلُزُومِ السَّلْبِ، وَعَلَى هَذَا فَقِسْ.
The conditional [proposition]: if in it a judgment is made of the existence of a relation upon another estimation by necessity, or by coincidence, or absolutely, then it is a conjunctive [which is] necessary, or coincidental, or absolute. And if in it a judgment is made of the mutual exclusion of the two relations in truth and falsehood together, or in truth only, or in falsehood only, by opposition, or by coincidence, or absolutely, then it is a disjunctive [which is] real, or exclusive-of-union, or exclusive-of-vacuum, [by] opposition, or coincidental, or absolute. And sometimes in the two exclusives (exclusive-of-union and exclusive-of-vacuum) the mutual exclusion is considered in truth or in falsehood absolutely, and by this meaning they are more general. And all these are the realities of the affirmatives; as for their negatives, it is the removal of their affirmatives. So the necessary negative is that in which it is judged with the negation of necessity, not with the necessity of negation, and upon this measure [the rest].
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies conditional propositions (shartiyya). The author first explains that a conjunctive conditional (mutasila) is one where a judgment of the existence of a relation is made based on another estimation. This estimation can be necessary (luzumiyya), coincidental (ittifaqiyya), or absolute (mutlaqa). He then defines the disjunctive conditional (munfasila) based on the mutual exclusion of two relations. The exclusion can be in both truth and falsehood (real/ haqiqiyya), in truth only (man'at al-jam'), or in falsehood only (man'at al-khuluw), each again having subtypes of opposition (inadiyya), coincidental, or absolute. The author also notes that the two exclusive types can be considered in an absolute sense regarding truth or falsehood, making them more general. He then moves to the negatives (salib) of these propositions: the negative of a conditional is simply the negation of its affirmative judgment. For example, a necessary negative is the negation of necessity, not the necessity of negation. The passage ends with 'and upon this measure' indicating that the same pattern applies to other subtypes. Throughout, the author uses terms familiar from Kalam logic, and the examples would be familiar from Islamic jurisprudence, such as conditional statements in contracts or rulings.

Key Takeaway
Conditionals are not monolithic; they are subdivided by the nature of the connection (necessary, coincidental, absolute) for conjunctive, and by the type of exclusion for disjunctive, with negation being the removal of the affirmative judgment.

Quantifiers for Conditional Propositions (Sūr al-Sharṭiyya)
Author explains quantifiers for conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and correctly use quantifier words for universal and particular, affirmative and negative conditional propositions in both conjunctive and disjunctive types.

Core Idea
Every conditional proposition has a quantifier determining its scope; key words are 'matā', 'dā'imā', 'laysa al-batta', 'qad yakūn'.

In this passage, the author classifies conditional judgments and provides the specific quantifier words for each category. Understanding these quantifiers is essential for evaluating the strength of conditional arguments in Islamic sciences like uṣūl al-fiqh, where jurists use conditional statements to establish rulings.

Guided Path
1
Classify the conditional judgment
Determine if the judgment is based on a specific estimation (makhṣūṣa), quantified (maḥṣūra), or indeterminate (muhmala). The passage first distinguishes these three categories.
2
Identify the quantifier type
For quantified conditionals, note whether it is universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya), and affirmative or negative. The passage lists words for each combination.
3
Distinguish conjunctive vs disjunctive
Note that universal affirmative quantifiers differ: 'matā' for conjunctive, 'dā'imā' for disjunctive. Universal negative uses 'laysa al-batta' for both.
4
Form universal negative from universal affirmative
The passage explains that prefixing the negation to the universal affirmative quantifier yields universal negative. For example, 'laysa al-batta' is the universal negative quantifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الْحُكْمُ فِيهَا إِنْ كَانَ عَلَى تَقْدِيرٍ مُعَيَّنٍ فَمَخْصُوصَةٌ، وَإِلَّا فَإِنْ بُيَّنَ كَمِّيَّةُ الْحُكْمِ بِأَنَّهُ عَلَى جَمِيعِ تَقَادِيرِ الْمُقَدَّمِ أَوْ بَعْضِهَا فَمَحْصُورَةٌ، كُليّةٌ، أَوْ جُزْيَيَّةٌ، وَإِلَّا فَمُهْمَلَةٌ ، وَالطَّبَعِيَّةُ هَهُنا غَيْرُ مَعْقُولة . وَسُورُ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ فِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ مَتَى «وَ «مَهْمَا وَكُلَّمَا»، وَفِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ دَائِمًا)» ، وَسُورُ السَّالِبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ فِيهِمَا لَيْسَ أَلْبَيَّةَ» ، وَسُورُ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ فِيهِمَا قَدْ يَكُونُ»، وَسُورُ السَّالِبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ فِيهِمَا قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ»، وَبِإِدْخَالِ حَرْفِ السَّلْبِ عَلَى سُورِ الْإِيجَابِ الْكُلّي، وَإِطْلَاقُ لَوْ وَ«إنْ» وَإِذَا» وا أَوْ وَإِمَّا لِلْإِهْمَالِ. قَالَ الشَّيْخُ: «إِنْ شَدِيدُ الدَّلَالَةِ عَلَى اللُّزُومِ، وَمَتَى» ضَعِيفُهَا ، وَإِذا كَالْمُتَوَسِّطِ، وَفِيهِ نَظَرُ .
Then the judgment in it: if it is upon a specific estimation then it is particular (makhsūsa); otherwise, if the quantity of the judgment is specified as being upon all estimations of the antecedent or some of them, then it is quantified (mahṣūra): universal or particular; otherwise it is indeterminate (muhmala), and the natural (tabī'iyya) here is not considered. And the quantifier of the universal affirmative in the conjunctive is 'matā' and 'mahmā' and 'kullamā', and in the disjunctive is 'dā'imā'. And the quantifier of the universal negative in both is 'laysa al-batta'. And the quantifier of the particular affirmative in both is 'qad yakūn'. And the quantifier of the particular negative in both is 'qad lā yakūn'. And by entering the letter of negation upon the quantifier of universal affirmative [you get the universal negative]. And the free use of 'law', 'in', 'idhā', 'aw', 'immā' is for indeterminate. The Shaykh said: 'in' is strong in indicating necessity, 'matā' is weak, and 'idhā' is moderate, and there is a consideration in this.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage explains the quantifiers (sūr) used in conditional propositions. It begins by classifying the judgment (hukm) in conditionals: if the judgment is based on a specific estimation (taqdīr mu'ayyan), it is called makhsūsa (particularized). Otherwise, if the quantity is specified—whether the judgment covers all estimations of the antecedent or some—it is mahṣūra (quantified), which are universal (kulliyya) or particular (juz'iyya). If unspecified, it is muhmala (indeterminate). The author notes that 'natural' (tabī'iyya) is not applicable here. Then the concrete quantifier words are given: For universal affirmative in conjunctive conditionals, the words are matā (when), mahmā (whatever), kullamā (whenever); in disjunctive conditionals, dā'imā (always). For universal negative, laysa al-batta (not at all). For particular affirmative, qad yakūn (may be); for particular negative, qad lā yakūn (may not be). The author also explains that the universal negative can be formed by prefixing the negation to the universal affirmative quantifier. For indeterminate conditionals, particles like law (if), in (if), idhā (when), aw (or), immā (either) are used. Finally, a comment from 'the Shaykh' (likely the author of the matn) is quoted: 'in' is strongly indicative of necessity, 'matā' is weak, and 'idhā' is intermediate, though the commentator suggests this requires further consideration.

Key Takeaway
The quantifier words for conditionals are specific: 'matā' for universal affirmative conjunctive, 'dā'imā' for universal affirmative disjunctive, and 'laysa al-batta' for universal negative in both.

Correlation Without Causality: Refuting a Logical Principle
Author critiques the claim that correlatives must be causally related.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that necessary correlation implies causation using the example of God's existence and the non-existence of His non-existence.

Core Idea
Correlation does not require causality; God's existence and the impossibility of His non-existence prove this.

In logic, it is widely assumed that if two propositions are necessarily correlated, one must cause the other or both must share a common cause. But is this always true? The author of this passage challenges that assumption using a powerful theological example: the existence of God and the impossibility of His non-existence. By examining this case, you will learn to distinguish logical correlation from causal dependence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common claim
Find the sentence where the author states the common view among logicians about correlatives needing a causal relation.
Look for 'يشتهر بين القوم أن المتلازمين يجب أن يكون أحدهما علة للآخر'
2
Locate the counterexample
The author provides a specific counterexample. Identify it: which two things are said to be correlated without causality?
The example involves the existence of God and the non-existence of His non-existence.
3
Understand the reasoning
Follow the argument: why is the non-existence of non-existence correlated with existence? Why is this not a causal relation?
The author uses the principle that if one contradictory is impossible, the other is necessary. God's non-existence is impossible by itself, so His existence is necessary. But His existence is uncaused.
4
Assess the conclusion
What is the author's final verdict on the common claim? How does this affect the understanding of talāzum?
The author concludes that correlation without causality is possible, so the common claim is false.

وَأَطْرَافُ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ لاَ حُكْمَ فِيهَا الْآنَ، وَلاَ يَلْزَمُ قَبْلَهُ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ بَعْدَ التَّحْلِيلِ. وَمِنْ ثَمَّ كَانَ مَنَاطُ صِدْقِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ وَكَذِبِهَا هُوَ الْحُكْمُ بِالاِنْصَالِ وَالإِنْفِصَالِ، كَالْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ. نَعَمْ، يَكُونُ شَبِيهَةٌ بِحَمْلِيَّتَيْنِ أَوْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْنِ أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ أَوْ مُخْتَلِفَتَيْنِ. وَتَلَازُمُ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ وَتَعَانُدُهَا مَعَ قِلَّةِ جَدْوَاهَا مَبْسُوطٌ فِي الْمُطَوَّلَاتِ. تَتِمَّةٌ: فِيهَا مَبَاحِثُ الْأَوَّلُ : قَدِ اشْتَهَرَ بَيْنَ الْقَوْمِ أَنَّ الْمُتَلَازِمَيْنِ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدُهُمَا عِلَّةٌ لِلْآخَرِ، أَوْ كِلَاهُمَا مَعْلُولَي عِلَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ ، كَالْمُتَضَابِفَيْنِ، وَذَلِكَ مِمَّا لاَ دَلِيلَ عَلَيْهِ، بَلْ يُسْتَدَلُّ عَلَى بُطْلَانِهِ بِأَنَّ عَدَمَ عَدَمِ الْوَاجِبِ تَعَالَى مُتَلَازِمٌ لِوُجُودِهِ، وَإِذَا كَانَ عَدَمُ الْوَاجِبِ تَعَالَى مُمْتَنِعًا لِذَاتِهِ فَعَدَمُ ذَلِكَ الْعَدَم غَيْرُ مُسْتَنِدٍ إِلَى أَمْرِ آخَرَ ؛ لِأَنَّ أَحَدَ النَّقِيضَيْنِ إِذَا كَانَ مُمْتَنِعًا كَانَ النَّقِيضُ الْآخَرُ ضَرُورِيًّا، وَبُيِّنَ فِي الْحِكْمَةِ أَنَّ وُجُودَهُ غَيْرُ مُعَلَّلٍ، فَبَيْنَ الْوُجُودِ وَعَدَمِ الْعَدَمِ تَلَازُمُ بِلَا عِليَّةٍ فَتَدَبَّر.
And the parts of the conditional have no judgment in them now, nor before it, nor after analysis. Hence the criterion of truth and falsehood of the conditional is the judgment of connection or disconnection, like affirmation and negation. Yes, it may be similar to two categoricals, or two conjunctives, or two disjunctives, or different ones. And the correlation and opposition of conditionals, despite their little utility, is expounded in lengthy works. Appendix: The first discussion: It is well-known among the people that the two correlates must be such that one of them is a cause for the other, or both are effects of one cause, like correlatives (e.g., father-son). And this is without proof; rather, it is refuted by the fact that the non-existence of the non-existence of the Exalted Necessary is correlated with His existence. And if the non-existence of the Exalted Necessary is impossible by itself, then the non-existence of that non-existence is not dependent on another thing; because if one of the two contradictories is impossible, the other contradictory is necessary. And it is shown in philosophy that His existence is uncaused. So between existence and non-existence of non-existence there is correlation without causality. So reflect.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is an appendix (tatimma) addressing a common claim about correlatives (mutalāzimān). The author first makes a preliminary point about the truth of conditionals: the truth or falsehood of a conditional depends solely on the judgment of connection (ittisāl) or disconnection (infisāl) between its parts, not on the actual truth of the parts themselves. A conditional may resemble two categoricals or other combinations. He then moves to the main topic. The common claim is that if two propositions are necessarily correlated (mutalāzimān), then one must be the cause of the other, or both must be effects of a single cause (like father-son). The author rejects this as unproven and provides a counterexample: the existence of God (wājib ta'ālā) and the non-existence of His non-existence (i.e., impossibility of His non-existence) are correlates. God's non-existence is impossible by itself, so its non-existence (which is the same as His existence) is necessary. But God's existence is uncaused (as proven in philosophy), and the non-existence of non-existence is not caused by anything else. Hence, we have a clear case of correlation without causality. The author concludes by asking the reader to reflect. This is a critical refinement typical of the commentary tradition, where a commonly held logical principle is challenged using theological examples.

Key Terms
تَلَازُم
مُتَلَازِمَان
عِلَّة
وَاجِب تَعَالَى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main counterexample used to refute the claim that correlatives must be causally related?
A) The relation between father and son
B) The existence of God and the non-existence of His non-existence
C) The relation between fire and smoke
D) The relation between a conditional and its parts

Key Takeaway
Necessary correlation does not entail a causal relation; the case of God's existence and the impossibility of His non-existence demonstrates this.

Quantifiers for Conditional Propositions
Author lists quantifier words and discusses their strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier words for universal and particular conditional propositions in Arabic and explain their strength indicators.

Core Idea
Conditional quantifiers use distinct words revealing scope and necessity.

In this passage from Bahr Ulum's commentary on Sullam al-Ulum, the author explains the quantifiers (sur) that attach to conditional propositions. These quantifiers function similarly to those in categorical propositions but use different words. Understanding these quantifiers is essential for correctly interpreting the scope and strength of conditional statements in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish quantified from absolute
The author splits conditionals into quantified (mahṣura) and absolute (muhmala). A conditional is quantified if the judgment's scope is specified over all or some possibilities of the antecedent. Note that the 'natural universal' is irrelevant here; only explicit quantification matters.
This distinction mirrors categorical propositions but with different quantifier words.
2
List the quantifiers by type
For universal affirmative in conjunctive conditionals (muttasila): 'mata', 'mahma', 'kullama'. For universal negative in both types: 'laysa al-batta'. For particular affirmative: 'qad yakūn'. For particular negative: 'qad la yakūn'. Also, the universal negative can be formed by negating the universal affirmative quantifier.
Remember: universal negative is the same for both conjunctive and disjunctive.
3
Assess quantifier strength
The shaykh (likely al-Katibi) rates 'in' (إن) as strongly indicating necessity (luzum), 'mata' (متى) as weak, and 'idha' (إذا) as intermediate. Other words like 'law', 'aw', 'imma' indicate absoluteness.
This strength hierarchy helps determine how tightly the antecedent binds the consequent.
4
Clarify truth condition
The author stresses that the parts (antecedent and consequent) have no independent judgment; truth depends solely on the connection (ittisal) or disconnection (infisal). This is analogous to affirmation/negation in categoricals.
Avoid thinking the conditional is true because the parts are true or false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ بُيِّنَ كَمِّيَّةُ الْحُكْمِ بِأَنَّهُ عَلَى جَمِيعِ تَقَادِيرِ الْمُقَدَّمِ أَوْ بَعْضِهَا فَمَحْصُورَةٌ، كُلِّيَّةٌ، أَوْ جُزْئِيَّةٌ، وَإِلَّا فَمُهْمَلَةٌ، وَالطَّبَعِيَّةُ هَهُنَا غَيْرُ مَعْقُولَةٍ. وَسُورُ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ فِي الْمُتَّصِلَةِ: مَتَى وَمَهْمَا وَكُلَّمَا، وَفِي الْمُنْفَصِلَةِ: دَائِمًا، وَسُورُ السَّالِبَةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ فِيهِمَا: لَيْسَ أَلْبَتَّةَ، وَسُورُ الْمُوجَبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ فِيهِمَا: قَدْ يَكُونُ، وَسُورُ السَّالِبَةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ فِيهِمَا: قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ، وَبِإِدْخَالِ حَرْفِ السَّلْبِ عَلَى سُورِ الْإِيجَابِ الْكُلِّيِّ، وَإِطْلَاقُ لَوْ وَإِنْ وَإِذَا وَأَوْ وَإِمَّا لِلْإِهْمَالِ. قَالَ الشَّيْخُ: إِنَّ شَدِيدُ الدَّلَالَةِ عَلَى اللُّزُومِ، وَمَتَى ضَعِيفُهَا، وَإِذَا كَالْمُتَوَسِّطِ، وَفِيهِ نَظَرٌ. وَأَطْرَافُ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ لَا حُكْمَ فِيهَا الْآنَ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ قَبْلَهُ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ بَعْدَ التَّحْلِيلِ. وَمِنْ ثَمَّ كَانَ مَنَاطُ صِدْقِ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ وَكَذِبِهَا هُوَ الْحُكْمُ بِالِاتِّصَالِ وَالِانْفِصَالِ، كَالْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ. نَعَمْ، يَكُونُ شَبِيهَةً بِحَمْلِيَّتَيْنِ أَوْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْنِ أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ أَوْ مُخْتَلِفَتَيْنِ. وَتَلَازُمُ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ وَتَعَانُدُهَا مَعَ قِلَّةِ جَدْوَاهَا مَبْسُوطٌ فِي الْمُطَوَّلَاتِ.
If the quantity of the judgment is specified as being over all or some of the possibilities of the antecedent, then it is quantified: universal or particular. Otherwise it is absolute (muhmala). The natural universal is not considered here. The quantifier of the universal affirmative in the conjunctive conditional is 'mata', 'mahma', and 'kullama'; in the disjunctive it is 'da'iman'. The quantifier of the universal negative in both is 'laysa al-batta'. The quantifier of the particular affirmative in both is 'qad yakūn'; the quantifier of the particular negative in both is 'qad la yakūn'. And by entering the particle of negation upon the universal affirmative quantifier [we get the universal negative]. The unrestricted use of 'law', 'in', 'idha', 'aw', and 'imma' indicates absoluteness. The shaykh said: 'in' strongly indicates necessity; 'mata' is weak; 'idha' is intermediate; there is consideration in that. The parts of a conditional have no judgment at present, nor is it entailed before [the connection is made], nor after analysis. Hence the criterion of truth and falsity of a conditional is the judgment of connection and disconnection, like affirmation and negation. Yes, it can resemble two categoricals, or two conjunctive conditionals, or two disjunctive conditionals, or mixed ones. The entailment of conditionals and their mutual opposition, though of little fruit, are detailed in the lengthy works.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh of Bahr Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum explains the quantifiers (sur) that attach to conditional propositions. The author first distinguishes between quantified (mahṣura) and unquantified (muhmala) conditionals. If the judgment's scope is specified over all or some possibilities of the antecedent, it is quantified as universal or particular; otherwise it is absolute. He notes that the 'natural universal' (al-tabi'iyya) is not considered here, meaning that inherent universality of terms is not relevant; only explicit quantification matters. Then he lists the quantifier words: for universal affirmative in the conjunctive conditional (muttasila) the words 'mata', 'mahma', and 'kullama'; for universal negative in both types 'laysa al-batta'; for particular affirmative 'qad yakūn'; for particular negative 'qad la yakūn'. He also explains that the universal negative can be formed by adding the negation particle to the universal affirmative quantifier. The unrestricted use of conditional words like 'law', 'in', 'idha', 'aw', 'imma' indicates absoluteness (ihmal). The shaykh (likely al-Katibi or al-Samarqandi) remarks that 'in' strongly indicates necessity (luzum), 'mata' is weak, and 'idha' is intermediate, though there is some debate. Finally, he clarifies that the parts (ataraf, i.e., antecedent and consequent) have no independent judgment; truth depends on the connection (ittisal) or disconnection (infisal) between them, similar to affirmation and negation in categoricals. Conditionals can resemble categoricals or other conditionals in form, but detailed discussions of their entailments are left to longer works.

Key Terms
سور (sūr): A quantifier word that indicates the scope (universal or particular) of a conditional proposition. [Note: In categorical propositions, sur includes 'kull', 'ba'd', etc.; here it specifically refers to words like 'mata', 'mahma', etc.]
شرطية متصلة (shartiyya muttasila): A conditional proposition that asserts a connection (ittisal) between antecedent and consequent, e.g., 'If A then B'. [Note: This is the standard definition across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) إن
C) إذا
D) كلما

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are quantified using specific words that indicate scope and strength, and their truth depends solely on the logical connection or disconnection between antecedent and consequent.

Mutual Entailment Does Not Require Causality
Author refutes the claim that mutual entailment always involves causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a counterexample to the claim that mutual entailment requires causality and explain why the logicians' thesis fails.

Core Idea
Mutual entailment does not imply causation; the example of God's existence proves this.

The logicians claimed that if two propositions entail each other, one must cause the other or both share a common cause. The author rejects this as unproven and gives a powerful counterexample from theology: the mutual entailment between God's existence and the impossibility of His non-existence. This lesson will guide you through the refutation and its implications for causal reasoning in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
Find the Arabic where the author states the logicians' claim: that two mutually entailing propositions must be causally related—either one causes the other, or both are effects of a single cause.
The claim appears in the beginning: 'أَنَّ الْمُتَلَازِمَيْنِ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدُهُمَا عِلَّةٌ لِلْآخَرِ، أَوْ كِلَاهُمَا مَعْلُولَي عِلَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ'.
2
Locate the counterexample
The author uses the Necessary Existent (God). Note that His non-existence is impossible in itself (مُمْتَنِعًا لِذَاتِهِ).
This is the key premise: the non-existence of God is impossible by its own nature.
3
Analyze the entailment
The non-existence of the non-existence of God is equivalent to God's existence. They are mutually entailing: if one is true, the other must be true.
The author says: 'عَدَمُ عَدَمِ الْوَاجِبِ تَعَالَى مُتَلَازِمٌ لِوُجُودِهِ'.
4
Check the causal relation
In the example, neither is a cause of the other. God's existence is uncaused (as proven in philosophy), and the impossibility of non-existence is also uncaused (since one contradictory being impossible forces the other to be necessary).
Thus, there is entailment without any causal connection.
5
Conclude the refutation
Since we have a case of mutual entailment without causality, the logicians' universal claim is false.
The author ends with 'فَبَيْنَ الْوُجُودِ وَعَدَمِ الْعَدَمِ تَلَازُمٌ بِلَا عِلِّيَّةٍ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تَتِمَّةٌ: فِيهَا مَبَاحِثُ الْأَوَّلُ: قَدِ اشْتَهَرَ بَيْنَ الْقَوْمِ أَنَّ الْمُتَلَازِمَيْنِ يَجِبُ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَحَدُهُمَا عِلَّةٌ لِلْآخَرِ، أَوْ كِلَاهُمَا مَعْلُولَي عِلَّةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ، كَالْمُتَضَائِفَيْنِ، وَذَلِكَ مِمَّا لَا دَلِيلَ عَلَيْهِ، بَلْ يُسْتَدَلُّ عَلَى بُطْلَانِهِ بِأَنَّ عَدَمَ عَدَمِ الْوَاجِبِ تَعَالَى مُتَلَازِمٌ لِوُجُودِهِ، وَإِذَا كَانَ عَدَمُ الْوَاجِبِ تَعَالَى مُمْتَنِعًا لِذَاتِهِ فَعَدَمُ ذَلِكَ الْعَدَمِ غَيْرُ مُسْتَنِدٍ إِلَى أَمْرٍ آخَرَ؛ لِأَنَّ أَحَدَ النَّقِيضَيْنِ إِذَا كَانَ مُمْتَنِعًا كَانَ النَّقِيضُ الْآخَرُ ضَرُورِيًّا، وَبُيِّنَ فِي الْحِكْمَةِ أَنَّ وُجُودَهُ غَيْرُ مُعَلَّلٍ، فَبَيْنَ الْوُجُودِ وَعَدَمِ الْعَدَمِ تَلَازُمٌ بِلَا عِلِّيَّةٍ فَتَدَبَّرْ.
Appendix: It contains discussions. The first: It has become well-known among the [logicians] that two mutually entailing propositions must be such that one is a cause of the other, or both are effects of a single cause, like correlatives. But this has no proof; rather it is disproven by the fact that the non-existence of the non-existence of the Necessary Exalted is mutually entailing with His existence. And if the non-existence of the Necessary Exalted is impossible in itself, then the non-existence of that non-existence does not depend on another thing; because when one of two contradictories is impossible, the other contradictory is necessary. It has been shown in philosophy that His existence is uncaused. So between existence and the non-existence of non-existence there is entailment without causality. So ponder this.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a widespread claim among logicians: that two propositions which entail each other (mutalazimayn) must be related causally—either one causes the other, or both are effects of a single cause (like correlatives such as father and son). The author declares this claim baseless and provides a counterexample from the Necessary Existent (God). The non-existence of God is impossible in itself, so its non-existence (i.e., the non-existence of that non-existence) is equivalent to God's existence. The entailment between 'God exists' and 'the non-existence of God's non-existence exists' is mutual: if one is true, the other is necessarily true. However, God's existence is uncaused (as proven in metaphysics), and the non-existence of what is impossible is also uncaused. Thus, there is entailment without any causal relation. This refutes the necessity of causality for mutual entailment.

Key Terms
متلازمان
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to refute the claim that mutual entailment requires causality?
A) The existence of the world and its Creator
B) The existence of God and the non-existence of His non-existence
C) Heat and fire
D) Fatherhood and sonship

Key Takeaway
Mutual entailment can exist without any causal relationship, as demonstrated by the uncaused existence of God and the necessity of the impossibility of His non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the pair: 'All intoxicants are forbidden' and 'All forbidden drinks are intoxicants' (assuming they are mutually entailing in a particular legal context). Does this require that one is the cause of the other? Explain using the lesson's reasoning.
?
Identify another example from Islamic theology where two propositions entail each other but are not causally related.

Dispute on Entailment of Impossible Antecedent
Author surveys opinions on entailment from an impossible antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the major positions on whether an impossible antecedent entails any consequent and explain the author's argument for his preferred view.

Core Idea
The intellect cannot decisively judge entailment from an impossible antecedent because it only rules on reality.

In this passage, the author surveys a long-standing debate in Islamic logic: does an impossible proposition, such as 'five is even,' entail any consequent at all? Some logicians deny it absolutely, others qualify it, and the most famous view requires a relation between antecedent and consequent. The author critically examines these positions and ultimately argues that the intellect cannot be certain of any such entailment, as it lies beyond the world of reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The passage addresses whether an impossible antecedent (muqaddam muḥāl) entails (yastalzimu) any consequent in reality (fī nafs al-amr).
2
List the positions
Identify each view: absolute denial, denial when consequent is true (attributed to Ibn Sina), entailment if consequent is part of antecedent (dismissed as arbitrary), entailment if there is a relation (al-ashhar), and the correct view that the intellect does not decisively judge.
Note the reasoning behind each position.
3
Analyze the critique of the 'relation' view
The author argues that requiring non-incompatibility reduces to two affirmative entailments with contradictory consequents, which the opponent does not accept.
Understand why this is a problem.
4
Grasp the author's final position
The author asserts that the intellect only judges actual reality; impossibilities lie outside its domain, so no decisive entailment can be claimed. Mere hypothetical allowance (tajwīz) is possible but not certain.
This is the 'correct' view in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: اخْتُلِفَ فِي اسْتِلْزَامِ الْمُقَدَّمِ الْمُحَالِ لِلتَّالِي فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ أَنْكَرَهُ مُطْلَقًا، وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ أَنْكَرَهُ إِذَا كَانَ التَّالِي صَادِقًا، وَعَلَيْهِ يَدُلُّ كَلَامُ الرَّئِيسِ، وَمِنْ هَهُنَا قَالَ إِنَّ ارْتِفَاعَ النَّقِيضَيْنِ مُسْتَلْزِمٌ لِاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا، وَأَنَّهُ لَا لُزُومَ في إِنْ كَانَ الْخَمْسَةُ زَوْجًا فَهِيَ عَدَدٌ بِحَسَبِ نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ زَعَمَ أَنَّ الِاسْتِلْزَامَ ثَابِتٌ إِذَا كَانَ التَّالِي جُزْءً لِلْمُقَدَّمِ، وَذَلِكَ تَحَكُّمٌ. وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ زَعَمَ أَنَّهُ ثَابِتٌ إِذَا كَانَ بَيْنَهُمَا عَلَاقَةٌ، وَهُوَ الْأَشْهَرُ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالَ: إِنَّ الْمُقَدَّمَ الْمُحَالَ يَجِبُ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ مُنَافِيًا لِلتَّالِي؛ فَإِنَّ الْمُنَافَاةَ تُصَرِّحُ الِانْفِكَاكَ، وَالْمُلَازَمَةَ تَمْنَعُهُ. وَفِيهِ: أَنَّ حَاصِلَ ذَلِكَ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى لُزُومِيَّتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ، تَالِي أَحَدِهِمَا نَقِيضُ تَالِي الْأُخْرَى، وَالْخَصْمُ لَا يُسَلِّمُ الْمُنَافَاةَ بَيْنَهُمَا. وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ إِنَّهُ لَا يَجْزِمُ الْعَقْلُ بِاسْتِلْزَامِ مُحَالٍ مُحَالًا أَوْ مُمْكِنًا أَصْلًا. نَعَمْ، التَّجْوِيزُ لَا حَجْرَ فِيهِ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ، فَإِنَّ الْعَقْلَ حَاكِمٌ فِي عَالَمِ الْوَاقِعِ، وَإِذَا كَانَ شَيْءٌ خَارِجًا عَنْهُ لَمْ يَكُنْ تَحْتَ حُكْمِهِ، وَمُجَرَّدُ فَرْضِهِ لَهُ مِنْهُ لَا يُجْدِي فِي جَرَيَانِ الْحُكْمِ الْوَاقِعِيِّ، وَبَقَاءُ الْأَحْكَامِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ فِي عَالَمِ التَّقْدِيرِ مَشْكُودٌ.
The second [discussion]: There is disagreement concerning an impossible antecedent's entailment of a consequent in reality. Some deny it absolutely. Some deny it when the consequent is true; this is indicated by the words of the Chief [Ibn Sina]. From this he said that the removal of both contradictories entails their conjunction, and that there is no entailment in 'If five is even, then it is a number' according to reality. Some claim that the entailment holds if the consequent is a part of the antecedent; that is arbitrary. Some claim it holds if there is a relation between them; this is the most famous. Hence they said: The impossible antecedent must not be incompatible with the consequent; for incompatibility makes separation clear, while entailment prevents it. But [it is objected]: The upshot of that reduces to two affirmative entailment statements, the consequent of one being the contradictory of the consequent of the other, and the opponent does not accept the incompatibility between them. And some said: The intellect does not decisively judge any entailment of an impossible [antecedent] to an impossible or possible [consequent] at all. Yes, there is no restriction on [hypothetical] allowance; this is the truth. For the intellect judges only in the world of reality, and if something is outside it, it is not under its judgment; mere supposition of it by the intellect does not help in applying actual judgments, and the persistence of actual judgments in the world of supposition is doubted.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the second discussion in the appendix, surveying various positions on whether an impossible antecedent (e.g., 'five is even') entails any consequent in reality. The author lists multiple views: (1) Absolute denial of any entailment from an impossible. (2) Denial when the consequent is true, attributed to Ibn Sina (al-Ra'is), who famously said that 'If the two contradictories are removed, they are both affirmed' and gave the example 'If five is even, then it is a number'—which he considered not a genuine entailment in reality. (3) Some claim entailment holds if the consequent is part of the antecedent, dismissed as arbitrary. (4) The most famous view: entailment holds if there is a relation (ʿalaqa) between antecedent and consequent, requiring the impossible antecedent not to be incompatible with the consequent. The author critiques this, arguing that it effectively reduces to two affirmative entailments whose consequents are contradictories, and the opponent does not concede the incompatibility. (5) The correct view: the intellect cannot decisively judge any entailment from an impossible antecedent to any consequent, whether impossible or possible. However, hypothetical allowance (tajwiz) is possible. This is because the intellect only judges actual reality; if something is entirely outside reality (like an impossible state), its judgment cannot extend. Mere supposition does not carry actual truth conditions.

Key Takeaway
The intellect cannot be certain of entailment from an impossible antecedent because it only rules on actual reality, not on impossibilities.

Ibn Sina's Restriction on Universal Conditionals
Author explains Ibn Sina's restriction on universal quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Sina's restriction on universal quantifiers in conditionals and apply it to evaluate the truth of a universal conditional in an Islamic science context.

Core Idea
Universal conditionals are true only when the antecedent can coexist with the situations considered.

In this passage, the author explains Ibn Sina's crucial refinement of how we understand universal conditionals (kulliyya shartiyya). Ibn Sina argues that when we say 'Whenever A is, B is,' we cannot consider all imaginable possibilities—only those that can actually coexist with A. This prevents universal conditionals from becoming trivially false. Let's unpack the argument.

Guided Path
1
Locate the restriction
Find where Ibn Sina specifies the condition for universal conditionals: he restricts the possibilities (taqdirat) to those whose coexistence with the antecedent is possible.
Even if the possibilities themselves are impossible in their own nature, as long as they can coexist with the antecedent, they are allowed.
2
Understand the consequence of generalization
Ibn Sina explains that if we allowed all possibilities without restriction, no universal conditional would be true. Because for any conditional, we could suppose the antecedent with the non-existence of the consequent (which would falsify entailment) or with its existence (which would not show entailment either, as the antecedent alone doesn't guarantee the consequent).
3
Engage with the objection
An objector argues that an impossible antecedent might entail two contradictories and be compatible with both, so truth is still possible. Identify this objection in the text.
The objector is challenging the claim that no universal would be true if we generalize.
4
Assess the response
The answer is that certainty (jazm) of truth is not attained, because mere possibility (imkan) does not yield certainty. The text cuts off, but the idea is that truth requires more than mere supposition.
The response does not deny truth outright but denies certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثُ: الرَّئيسُ قَيَّدَ التَّقَادِيرَ وَالْأَوْضَاعَ فِي تَفْسِيرِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ بِالَّتِي يُمْكِنُ اجْتِمَاعُهَا مَعَ الْمُقَدَّمِ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُحَالَةً فِي أَنْفُسِهَا، وَبَيَّنَ بِأَنَّهُ لَوْ عَمَّمْنَا يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ كُلِّيَّةٌ أَصْلًا، فَإِنَّهُ إِذَا فُرِضَ الْمُقَدَّمُ مَعَ عَدَمِ التَّالِي، أَوْ مَعَ وُجُودِهِ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الثَّالِيَ، وَلَا يُنَافِيهِ. وَأُورِدَ بِأَنَّ الْمُحَالَ جَازَ أَنْ يَسْتَلْزِمَ نَقِيضَيْنِ، وَأَنْ يُعَائِدَهُمَا، فَلَا نُسَلِّمُ عَدَمَ الصِّدْقِ. وَأُجِيبَ بِأَنَّ الْمُرَادَ لَمْ يَحْصُلِ الْجَزْمُ بِصِدْقِهَا؛ فَإِنَّ الْإِمْكَانَ لَا
The third [discussion]: The Chief [Ibn Sina] restricted the possibilities and situations in the interpretation of universality to those whose coexistence with the antecedent is possible, even if they are impossible in themselves. He explained that if we generalize, then no universal would be true at all, because when the antecedent is supposed along with the non-existence of the consequent, or with its existence, it neither entails nor contradicts it. An objection was raised: The impossible might entail two contradictories and be compatible with both, so we do not concede lack of truth. The answer was: What is meant is that certainty of its truth is not attained; because possibility does not [continue]...
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage discusses the third issue: Ibn Sina's restriction on universal quantifiers in conditionals. He limits the quantification to those possibilities (taqdir) that can coexist with the antecedent, even if those possibilities are themselves impossible (e.g., 'if a thing is existing, then it is created'—the antecedent 'existing' can coexist with various situations). Ibn Sina argues that if we allowed all possibilities without restriction, no universal conditional would ever be true. Because for any conditional, we could suppose the antecedent with the non-existence of the consequent or with its existence; that supposition would neither entail the consequent (since the antecedent alone does not guarantee the consequent) nor contradict it (since the antecedent is compatible with both). But an objector points out that an impossible antecedent might entail two contradictories and be compatible with both, so truth is still possible. The response is that certainty of truth is not attained, because mere possibility (imkan) does not yield certainty. The text breaks off here, but the argument likely continues that actual truth in reality requires more than mere supposition.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's restriction ensures that universal conditionals are meaningful by limiting evaluation to possibilities that can coexist with the antecedent.

Implication of an Impossible Antecedent: The Four Views and the Author's Resolution
Author presents four views on whether an impossible antecedent implies a consequent and defends the view that the mind cannot be certain of it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four positions on the implication of an impossible antecedent and explain the author's defended view that the mind cannot be certain of it.

Core Idea
An impossible antecedent does not force a consequent upon the mind; we can only allow possibility without certainty.

Implication from an impossible antecedent is a classic logical puzzle. This passage presents four distinct views and culminates in the author's epistemic resolution. Understanding this helps you see how Islamic logicians handled conditional statements that involve impossibilities—a vital tool for evaluating arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh where impossible conditions sometimes appear in hypothetical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
What is the main issue? It is whether an impossible antecedent (muqaddam muhal) can logically imply a consequent (tali) in reality (fi nafs al-amr).
2
List the four views presented
The author enumerates: (1) Absolute denial of any implication. (2) Denial only when the consequent is true (ascribed to Ibn Sina, with example: 'If five is even, then it is a number'). (3) Implication holds only when the consequent is part of the antecedent (dismissed as arbitrary). (4) Implication holds only when there is a relation between antecedent and consequent (the more well-known view).
Note the example from Ibn Sina: 'If five is even, then it is a number.' The antecedent is impossible, so even though the consequent is true, no real implication holds.
3
Analyze the critique of the relation view
The author argues that requiring a relation reduces to two affirmative implications with contradictory consequents, and the opponent does not concede the contradiction. Try to see how: if the antecedent is impossible and the consequent must not be contradictory, then any contradiction would break the implication, but the author shows this leads to a problematic pair of implications.
This critique exposes a weakness in the relation-based view.
4
Understand the author's own view
The author defends the view that the mind cannot be certain (yajzim) of any implication from an impossible antecedent—whether to another impossible or to a possible. However, possibility (tajwiz) is not precluded. The mind only judges the actual world, and impossible suppositions are outside its domain.
The key distinction: certainty is denied, but mere possibility is allowed.
5
Locate the conclusion and its justification
The author's final statement: 'Possibility does not entail necessity.' Even if we allow the possibility of an implication, we cannot assert it as certain. Reason governs only what actually exists; mere assumption does not bring an impossibility under its judgment.
This is a pragmatic epistemic stance common in later Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: اخْتُلِفَ فِي اسْتِلْزَامِ الْمُقَدَّمِ الْمُحَالِ لِلتَّالِي فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ أَنْكَرَهُ مُطْلَقًا، وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ أَنْكَرَهُ إِذَا كَانَ التَّالِي صَادِقًا، وَعَلَيْهِ يَدُلُّ كَلَامُ الرَّئيس ، وَمِنْ هَهُنَا قَالَ إِنَّ ارْتَفَاعَ النَّقِيضَيْنِ مُسْتَلْزِم لاجْتِمَاعِهِمَا ، وَأَنَّهُ لَا لُزُومَ في إِنْ كَانَ الْخَمْسَةُ زَوْجًا فَهِيَ عَدَدُ بِحَسَبِ نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ زَعَمَ أَنَّ الاسْتِلْزَامَ ثَابِتٌ إِذَا كَانَ التَّالي جُزْءً لِلْمُقَدَّمِ ، وَذَلِكَ تَحَكُمْ. وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ زَعَمَ أَنَّهُ ثَابِتٌ إِذَا كَانَ بَيْنَهُمَا عَلَاقَةٌ، وَهُوَ الْأَشْهَرُ، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ قَالَ: إِنَّ الْمُقَدَّمَ الْمُحَالَ يَجِبَ أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ مُنَافِيًا لِلتَّالي ; فَإِنَّ الْمُنَافَاةَ تُصَرِّحُ الاِنْفِكَاكَ، وَالْمُلَازَمَةَ تَمْنَعُهُ. وَفِيهِ: أَنَّ حَاصِلَ ذَلِكَ يَرْجِعُ إِلَى لُزُومِيَّتَيْنِ مُوجَبَتَيْنِ، تَالِي أَحَدِهِمَا نَقِيضُ تَالِي الْأُخْرَى، وَالْخَصْمُ لَا يُسَلِّمُ الْمُنَافَاةَ بَيْنَهُمَا. وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ إِنَّهُ لَا يَجْزِمُ الْعَقْلُ بِاسْتِلْزام مُحَالٍ مُحَالًا أَوْ مُمْكِنَا أَصْلًا. نَعَمْ ، التَّجْوِيزُ لَا حَجْرَ فِيهِ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ ، فَإِنَّ الْعَقْلَ حَاكِمُ فِي عَالَمِ الْوَاقِعِ ، وَإِذَا كَانَ شَيْءٌ خَارِجًا عَنْهُ لَمْ يَكُنْ تَحْتَ حُكْمِهِ، وَمُجَرَّدُ فَرْضِهِ لَهُ مِنْهُ لَا يُجْدِي فِي جَرَيَانِ الْحُكْمِ الْوَاقِعِيِّ ، وَبَقَاءُ الْأَحْكَامِ الْوَاقِعِيَّةِ فِي عَالَمِ التَّقْدِيرِ مَشْكُودٌ . وَفَإِنَّ الْإِمْكَانَ لَا يُفِيدُ الْوُجُوبَ.
The second: There is disagreement regarding the implication of an impossible antecedent for a consequent in reality. Among them are those who deny it absolutely, and among them are those who deny it when the consequent is true, and the statement of al-Ra'is indicates this. From this, he said that the removal of two contradictories implies their conjunction, and that there is no implication in 'If the five is even, then it is a number' according to reality. And among them are those who claimed that the implication is established when the consequent is a part of the antecedent, and that is arbitrary. And among them are those who claimed that it is established when there is a relation between them, and that is the more well-known. Hence he said: The impossible antecedent must not be contradictory to the consequent; for contradiction necessitates separation, and implication prevents it. And in it: That the upshot of that reduces to two affirmative implications, the consequent of one being the contradictory of the consequent of the other, and the opponent does not concede the contradiction between them. And among them are those who said that the mind does not judge with certainty that an impossible implies an impossible or a possible at all. Yes, allowance is not precluded, and that is the truth. For the mind is a judge in the world of reality, and when something is outside it, it is not under its judgment; and mere assumption of it from it does not avail in the application of real judgment; and the continuance of real judgments in the world of supposition is doubted. And indeed possibility does not entail necessity.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the logical implication of an impossible antecedent. The author first notes the disagreement among logicians about whether an impossible antecedent (muqaddam muhal) can imply a consequent. Some deny any implication whatsoever. Others (including al-Ra'is, i.e., Ibn Sina) deny it only when the consequent is true, giving the example: 'If five is even, then it is a number'—the antecedent is impossible, so no real implication holds in reality despite the consequent being true. Another view holds that implication is established only when the consequent is a part of the antecedent, but the author dismisses this as arbitrary. The more well-known view requires a relation between the antecedent and consequent; thus the impossible antecedent must not be contradictory to the consequent, because contradiction would allow separation while implication prevents it. The author critiques this view by arguing that it reduces to two implications with contradictory consequents, and the opponent does not concede the contradiction. Finally, the author presents the view that the mind cannot be certain of any implication from an impossible antecedent—whether to another impossible or to a possible—but at the same time, possibility (tajwiz) is not precluded. This is the truth because reason governs only the actual world; when something is outside it (like an impossible supposition), it is not under reason's judgment. Merely assuming a situation does not allow the application of real-world judgments; the persistence of real judgments in the realm of supposition is doubtful. The concluding sentence 'possibility does not entail necessity' reinforces that even if we allow the possibility of an implication, we cannot be certain of it.

Key Terms
اسْتِلْزَام
الْمُقَدَّمِ الْمُحَالِ
التَّالِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct stance on the implication of an impossible antecedent?
A) It always holds.
B) It never holds.
C) The mind cannot be certain of it, but possibility is allowed.
D) It holds only if there is a relation.

Key Takeaway
The mind cannot be certain of any implication from an impossible antecedent, but the possibility of such implication is not ruled out; certainty requires actuality, not mere supposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the conditional: 'If the world is eternal, then it has a cause.' The antecedent is impossible for a Muslim. According to the author's view, can the mind be certain of the implication? Why or why not? Explain using the author's argument about the mind's jurisdiction over the actual world.
?
Identify which of the four views on impossible antecedent is being used by each of the following statements: (a) 'If five is even, then it is a number' has no real implication. (b) 'If the impossible happens, then anything follows' is a consequence of the absolute denial view. (c) The author's own view.
