Restricting Suppositions for Universal Conditionals
Author explains al-Ra'is's restriction of universal conditionals to suppositions combinable with the antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why al-Ra'is restricts universal conditionals to suppositions combinable with the antecedent and identify the objection and response.

Core Idea
Universal conditionals require suppositions combinable with the antecedent, even if impossible.

This lesson explores a key refinement in the logic of universal conditional propositions (kulliyya). Al-Ra'is (Ibn Sina) argued that not every hypothetical supposition can be used to test the truth of a universal conditional; only those that can combine with the antecedent are valid. This restriction avoids the problem that no universal would be true if arbitrary suppositions were allowed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Locate where al-Ra'is states that suppositions must be those whose combination with the antecedent is possible, even if the suppositions themselves are impossible.
This is the core restriction being argued for.
2
Understand the reasoning
Al-Ra'is explains why generalization fails: if any supposition is allowed, supposing the antecedent with the non-existence of the consequent yields no implication, so no universal conditional would be true.
Focus on the logical consequence of not restricting.
3
Examine the objection
An objector argues that the impossible can imply two contradictories, so perhaps truth could still be maintained even with impossible suppositions.
This challenges the necessity of the restriction.
4
Analyze the response
The answer is that the goal is certainty (jazm) of truth, and possibility does not entail necessity. So the objection does not restore certainty.
The response hinges on the difference between possible truth and certain truth.
5
Author's conclusion
The commentator concludes that the restriction must be to possibilities in themselves (al-mumkinat fi anfusiha), i.e., suppositions that are inherently possible.
This is a stricter condition than just combinable with the antecedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ : الرَّئيسُ قَيَّدَ التَّقَادِيرَ وَالْأَوْضَاعَ فى تفسير الكلية بالَّتِي يُمْكِنُ اجْتِمَاعُهَا مَعَ الْمُقَدَّمِ ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُحَالَةٌ في أَنْفُسِهَا ، وَبَيَّنَ بِأَنَّهُ لَوْ عَمَّمْنَا يَلْزَمُ أَنْ لَا يَصْدُقَ كُلَّيَّةٌ أَصْلًا، فَإِنَّهُ إِذَا فُرِضَ الْمُقَدَّمُ مَعَ عَدَمِ التَّالِي، أَوْ مَعَ وُجُودِهِ لَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ الثَّالِيَ ، وَلَا يُنَافِيهِ. وَأُورِدَ بِأَنَّ الْمُحَالَ جَازَ أَنْ يَسْتَلْزِمَ نَقِيضَيْنِ، وَأَنْ يُعَائِدَهُمَا، فَلَا نُسَلِّمُ عَدَمَ الصِّدْقِ. وَأُجِيبَ بِأَنَّ الْمُرَادَ لَمْ يَحْصُلِ الْجَزْمُ بِصِدْقِهَا؛ فَإِنَّ الْإِمْكَانَ لَا يُفِيدُ الْوُجُوبَ. أَقُولُ: فَيَجِبُ التَّقْبِيدُ بِالْمُمْكِنَاتِ فِي أَنْفُسِهَا ، فَافْهَمْ.
The third: Al-Ra'is restricted the suppositions and posits in the interpretation of the universal to those whose combination with the antecedent is possible, even if they are impossible in themselves. And he explained that if we generalize, it would follow that no universal is true at all, because if the antecedent is supposed with the non-existence of the consequent, or with its existence, it does not imply the third and does not contradict it. And it was objected that the impossible may imply two contradictories, and may conflict with them, so we do not concede the lack of truth. And it was answered that what is meant is that certainty of its truth is not obtained; for possibility does not entail necessity. I say: So it is necessary to restrict to possibilities in themselves, so understand.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage addresses a technical point in the interpretation of universal conditional propositions (al-kulliyya). Al-Ra'is (Ibn Sina) restricts the suppositions (taqdirat) under which a universal conditional is evaluated to those that can combine with the antecedent, even if those suppositions are impossible in themselves (e.g., supposing an impossible state). His reasoning is that if we allowed any arbitrary supposition (including those that are incompatible with the antecedent), then no universal conditional would be true. For example, if we suppose the antecedent together with the non-existence of the consequent, there would be no implication (luzum) and no contradiction ('adam al-munafat), so the universal statement would not hold. An objection is raised: an impossible statement can imply two contradictories, so perhaps truth could still be maintained. The answer is that we are not seeking certainty of truth (jazm bi-sidqiha), because possibility does not entail necessity. The author (the commentator) concludes that we must restrict to possibilities in themselves (al-mumkinat fi anfusiha), meaning suppositions that are inherently possible, not merely those that can be combined.

Key Terms
الكُلِّيَّة
التَّقْدِير
اجْتِمَاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ra'is restrict universal conditionals to suppositions that can combine with the antecedent?
A) To avoid impossible antecedents.
B) Because otherwise no universal would be true.
C) Because possibility entails necessity.
D) Because of an objection about contradictories.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ra'is's restriction ensures that universal conditionals are evaluated only under suppositions that are combinable with the antecedent, preserving the possibility of true universal statements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify a universal conditional proposition from the field of usul al-fiqh (e.g., 'If a ruling is abrogated, it is no longer applicable'). Explain why al-Ra'is's restriction applies: what suppositions would be allowed and what would be disallowed?
?

Defining Accidental Conditionals and Their Types
Defines accidental conditionals and their two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between specific and general accidental conditionals based on the truth conditions of their parts.

Core Idea
Accidental conditionals require truth conditions, not a necessary link.

This lesson introduces the accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya), a type of conditional statement where the connection between antecedent and consequent is coincidental rather than necessary. We will examine the definitions given in the text, including the two views on truth conditions, and learn to classify accidentals as specific or general.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its genus
Find the term 'al-ittifaqiyya' in the Arabic passage. Note that it is a type of conditional proposition (al-shartiyya).
The passage begins 'الرَّابِعُ : الاِتِّفَاقِيَّةُ' indicating it is the fourth type of conditional.
2
Extract the two views on truth conditions
The author states that some require both antecedent and consequent to be true, while others only require the consequent to be true. Locate the phrases 'صِدْقُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ' (truth of both sides) and 'بِصِدْقِ التَّالِي فَقَطْ' (truth of the consequent only).
These correspond to specific and general accidentals respectively.
3
Note the condition of non-contradiction
The author adds that if the consequent contradicts the antecedent, the accidental cannot be true, otherwise contradictories would be possible. Find the sentence 'لَوْ كَانَ الثَّالِي مُنَافِيًا لِلْمُقَدَّم لَمْ تَصْدُقِ الإِنْفَاقِيَّةُ'.
This condition ensures logical consistency.
4
Distinguish specific from general
The passage explicitly names 'اتِّفَاقِيَّة خَاصَّة' (specific accidental) where both parts are true, and 'اتِّفَاقِيَّة عَامَّة' (general accidental) where only the consequent is true. Note that the general accidental permits an impossible antecedent with a true consequent.
The example 'مُقَدَّمٍ مُحَالٍ وَتَالٍ صَادِقٍ' illustrates this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ : الاِتِّفَاقِيَّةُ قَدِ اعْتُبِرَ فِيهَا صِدْقُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، وَقَدْ يُكْتَفَى فِيهَا بِصِدْقِ التَّالِي فَقَطْ ، فَيَجُوزُ تَرَكْبُهَا مِنْ مُقَدَّمٍ مُحَالٍ وَتَالٍ صَادِقٍ; فَإِنَّ الصَّادِقَ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ بَاقٍ عَلَى فَرْضِ كُلِّ مُحَالٍ صَرَّحَ بِهِ الرَّئيسُ . وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الثَّالِيَ لَوْ كَانَ مُنَافِيًا لِلْمُقَدَّم لَمْ تَصْدُقِ الإِنْفَاقِيَّةُ، وَإِلَّا أَمْكَنَ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ، وَتُسَمَّى الأُولَى اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ خَاصَّةٌ، وَالثَّانِيَةُ اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ ، قِيلَ: إِنَّ الاِتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ مُشْتَمِلَةٌ عَلَى الْعَلَاقَةِ ؛ لأَنَّ الْمَعِيَّةَ مُمْكِنَةٌ، فَلَهَا عِلَّةٌ، وَالْفَرْقُ أَنَّهَا اللُّزُومِيَّاتِ مَشْعُورٌ بِهَا، بِخِلَافِ الاِتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ. وَفِيهِ نَظَرُ، لِجَوَازِ أَنْ تَكُونَ اتِّفَاقِيَّة، وَمُطْلَقُ الْعِلِّيَّةِ لَا يَسْتَوْجِبُ الاِرْتِبَاطَ إِذَا كَانَتْ بِجِهَتَيْنِ مُخْتَلِفَتَيْنِ .
The fourth: The accidental (conditional) has been considered in it the truth of both sides, and it may suffice in it only the truth of the consequent, so it is permissible to compose it from an impossible antecedent and a true consequent; for the true in reality remains upon the supposition of every impossible, as stated by al-Ra'is. And the truth is that if the consequent were contradictory to the antecedent, the accidental would not be true, otherwise the conjunction of contradictories would be possible, and the first is called specific accidental, and the second general accidental. It is said that the accidentals involve a relation because coexistence is possible, so it has a cause; and the difference is that necessary conditionals are perceived (as necessary), unlike accidentals. And there is scrutiny in this, because it is possible that it be accidental, and absolute causality does not necessitate connection if it is from two different directions.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines the accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya), a statement where the connection between antecedent and consequent is not necessary but coincidental. Two views are presented: some require both antecedent and consequent to be true; others suffice with the consequent being true alone, allowing an impossible antecedent with a true consequent (as al-Ra'is states). However, the author asserts that if the consequent contradicts the antecedent, the accidental cannot be true, otherwise contradictories would be possible. Two types are distinguished: specific accidental (khassa), where both sides are true; and general accidental ('amma), where only the consequent is true. A further argument is discussed: accidentals involve a relation because coexistence is possible and has a cause, unlike necessary conditionals where the relation is perceived. The author raises a scrutiny: absolute causality does not entail connection if it is from two different directions, implying that accidental conditionals may not have a genuine causal link.

Key Terms
الاِتِّفَاقِيَّة
اتِّفَاقِيَّة خَاصَّة
اتِّفَاقِيَّة عَامَّة

D) Both require impossibility.

Key Takeaway
Accidental conditionals are defined by truth conditions and are divided into specific (both parts true) and general (consequent true alone).

The Causal Status of Accidental Conditionals
Discusses the causal basis of accidentals and the author's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to critically assess the claim that all accidental conditionals involve a causal relation, using the author's scrutiny.

Core Idea
Absolute causality does not entail a perceived necessary connection if causes are from different directions.

The passage goes beyond definition to discuss a philosophical debate: do accidental conditionals imply a causal relation? Some argue that because coexistence is possible and has a cause, there is a relation. The author scrutinizes this, asserting that causality from different directions does not necessitate the perceived connection. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Locate the claim about accidentals and causation
Find the phrase 'إِنَّ الاِتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ مُشْتَمِلَةٌ عَلَى الْعَلَاقَةِ' (accidentals involve a relation). The claim is that because coexistence (al-ma'iyya) is possible, there must be a cause (عِلَّة).
This is presented as a reported view (قِيلَ).
2
Identify the distinction from necessary conditionals
The passage contrasts accidentals with necessary conditionals (al-luzumiyyat): necessary conditionals have a perceived (مَشْعُورٌ بِهَا) relation, while accidentals do not. The claim is that accidentals still have an unperceived cause.
The word 'مَشْعُور' indicates perceived necessity.
3
Find the author's scrutiny
The author responds with 'وَفِيهِ نَظَرُ' (this is open to scrutiny). The reason is that it is possible for something to be accidental, and absolute causality (مُطْلَقُ الْعِلِّيَّةِ) does not necessitate connection if the cause and effect come from two different directions (بِجِهَتَيْنِ مُخْتَلِفَتَيْنِ).
This means that even if there is a cause for each part, they may be unrelated.
4
Understand the implication
The author's point is that a causal explanation for the truth of each part does not guarantee a genuine relation between them. Accidental conditionals may be true merely because both parts happen to be true, without a single unifying cause.
This is a subtle logical point about the nature of conditionals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ : الاِتِّفَاقِيَّةُ قَدِ اعْتُبِرَ فِيهَا صِدْقُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، وَقَدْ يُكْتَفَى فِيهَا بِصِدْقِ التَّالِي فَقَطْ ، فَيَجُوزُ تَرَكْبُهَا مِنْ مُقَدَّمٍ مُحَالٍ وَتَالٍ صَادِقٍ; فَإِنَّ الصَّادِقَ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ بَاقٍ عَلَى فَرْضِ كُلِّ مُحَالٍ صَرَّحَ بِهِ الرَّئيسُ . وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الثَّالِيَ لَوْ كَانَ مُنَافِيًا لِلْمُقَدَّم لَمْ تَصْدُقِ الإِنْفَاقِيَّةُ، وَإِلَّا أَمْكَنَ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ، وَتُسَمَّى الأُولَى اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ خَاصَّةٌ، وَالثَّانِيَةُ اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ ، قِيلَ: إِنَّ الاِتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ مُشْتَمِلَةٌ عَلَى الْعَلَاقَةِ ؛ لأَنَّ الْمَعِيَّةَ مُمْكِنَةٌ، فَلَهَا عِلَّةٌ، وَالْفَرْقُ أَنَّهَا اللُّزُومِيَّاتِ مَشْعُورٌ بِهَا، بِخِلَافِ الاِتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ. وَفِيهِ نَظَرُ، لِجَوَازِ أَنْ تَكُونَ اتِّفَاقِيَّة، وَمُطْلَقُ الْعِلِّيَّةِ لَا يَسْتَوْجِبُ الاِرْتِبَاطَ إِذَا كَانَتْ بِجِهَتَيْنِ مُخْتَلِفَتَيْنِ .
The fourth: The accidental (conditional) has been considered in it the truth of both sides, and it may suffice in it only the truth of the consequent, so it is permissible to compose it from an impossible antecedent and a true consequent; for the true in reality remains upon the supposition of every impossible, as stated by al-Ra'is. Real Disjunctive and Its Varieties
Author discusses the composition of disjunctive propositions and distinguishes real, exclusive, and exhaustive types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the real disjunctive from the exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions, and identify their logical composition.

Core Idea
Every disjunction is fundamentally a two-part relation, reducible to contradictory or equivalent components.

This passage tackles a foundational debate in Islamic logic: can a disjunctive proposition have more than two parts? The author defends the orthodox view that all disjunctions are binary, and then explains how the three types—real, exclusive, and exhaustive—are constructed from a base proposition and its logical relation to another. Understanding this classification is crucial for correctly analyzing compound propositions in kalam and fiqh arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of disjunction
The passage contrasts real (haqiqi) with exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw). Note that the real disjunctive is restricted to two parts, while the others are not.
This sets up the central issue
2
Analyze the debate on the number of parts
Some scholars claim disjunctions can have more than two parts, using the example 'Every concept is either necessary, possible, or impossible'. The author argues this is actually a compound of a categorical and a disjunctive, thus reducible to a two-part relation.
Understand why he calls it a 'single relation' (nisba wahida)
3
Examine the author's resolution
The author sides with the two-part view because a single relation requires two relata. He also rebuts the charge of circularity by referencing a principle from the first figure.
4
Define the composition of each type
Real disjunctive = proposition and its contradictory or equivalent; exclusive = proposition and something more specific than its contradictory; exhaustive = proposition and something more general than its contradictory.
This exactly distinguishes the three forms

الْخَامِسُ : قَالُوا الاِنْفِصَالُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ لَا يُمْكِنُ إِلَّا بَيْنَ جُزْئَيْنِ، بِخِلَافِ مَانِعَةِ الْجَمْعِ ، وَمَانِعَةِ الْخُلُوِّ . وَذَهَبَ جَمَاعَةٌ إِلَى أَنَّ الاِنْفِصَالَ مُطْلَقًا لَا يَتَحَصَّلُ إِلَّا مِنِ اثْنَيْنِ لَا أَزْيَدَ وَلَا أَنْقَصَ، وَمِثْلُ كُلَّ مَنْهُومٍ إِمَّا وَاجِبٌ أَوْ مُمْكِنٌ أَوْ مُمْتَنِعٌ مُرَكَّبٌ مِنْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ ، وَزَعَمَ بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّهُ مُطْلَقًا يُمْكِنُ تَرْكِيبُهُ مِنْ أَجْزَاء فَوْقَ اثْنَيْنِ ، وَالْحَقُّ هُوَ الثَّانِي ؛ لِأَنَّ الاِنْفِصَالَ نِسْبَةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَالنِّسْبَةُ الْوَاحِدَةُ لَا تُتَصَوَّرُ إِلَّا بَيْنَ اثْنَيْنِ، وَمَا قِيلَ إِنَّ فِيهِ مُصَادَرَةٌ ؛ لأَنَّهُ إِنْ أَرَادَ أَنَّ كُلَّ نِسْبَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ انْفِصَالِيَّةٍ أَوْ غَيْرِهَا ، فَهُوَ مَحَلَّ النزاع ، وَإِلَّا فَلَا يَنْفَعُ ، فَمَدْفُوعٌ بِمَا يُدْفَعُ بِهِ لُزُومُهَا فِي كُبْرَى الْأَوَّلِ ، فَتَأَمَّلْ . فَالْحَقِيقِيَّةُ لَا تَتَرَكَّبُ إِلَّا مِنْ فَضِيَّةٍ وَنَقِيضِهَا أَوْ مُسَاوِيهِ، وَمَانِعَةُ الْجَمْعِ مِنْهَا وَمِمَّا هُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضُهَا ، وَمَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ مِنْهَا وَمِمَّا هُوَ أَعَمُ مِنْ نَقِيضُهَا ، هَذَا.
The fifth: They said that the real disjunctive is not possible except between two parts, unlike the exclusive and the exhaustive. And a group held that disjunction absolutely is not obtained except from two, no more and no less. And an example: 'Every concept is either necessary, possible, or impossible' is composed of a categorical and a disjunctive. And some claim that it is absolutely possible to construct it from more than two parts. And the truth is the second, because disjunction is one relation, and the one relation is not conceivable except between two. And what was said that this is circular, because if by 'one relation' he means every disjunctive relation or other, then that is the point of dispute, otherwise it is not beneficial; so it is rebutted by that by which its necessity in the major of the first is rebutted, so reflect. So the real disjunctive is composed only of a proposition and its contradictory or its equivalent; the exclusive is composed of it and something more specific than its contradictory; the exhaustive is composed of it and something more general than its contradictory.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines the real disjunctive (infisal haqiqi) and distinguishes it from other types. It begins by stating that the real disjunctive can only be between two parts, unlike the exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw) disjunctions, which can involve more. There is a debate: some hold that all disjunctions are only between two parts, while others allow more than two. The example 'Every concept is either necessary, possible, or impossible' is actually analyzed as a compound of a categorical and a disjunctive, thus reducible to a two-part disjunction. The author sides with the view that disjunction is a single relation, and a single relation can only be between two relata. He then addresses the objection of circularity, but dismisses it. Finally, he specifies the composition of each type: the real disjunctive is a proposition and its contradictory or equivalent; the exclusive is a proposition and something more specific than its contradictory (i.e., more restrictive); the exhaustive is a proposition and something more general than its contradictory (i.e., broader).

Key Terms
الاِنْفِصَال الْحَقِيقِي
مَانِعَة الْجَمْع
مَانِعَة الْخُلُو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a real disjunctive (haqiqi) from an exclusive disjunctive (mani'at al-jam')?
A) Real disjunctive requires two parts that are contradictories or equivalents, while exclusive requires parts that are not contradictory.
B) Exclusive disjunctive allows both to be false.
C) Real disjunctive has more than two parts.
D) Exclusive disjunctive requires both to be true.

Key Takeaway
Real disjunction is a two-part relation using contradictories or equivalents; exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions are variants defined by more specific or more general components.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') on a fiqh ruling, and then test its logical behavior by checking whether both parts can be false.
?
Take the proposition 'Every act is either wajib, mandub, or mubah.' Analyze it according to the author's view: show that it is not a simple three-part disjunction but a compound of a categorical and a disjunctive.

The Coincidental Conditional: Definition and Truth Conditions
Author defines the coincidental conditional and its truth conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the coincidental conditional and its truth conditions based on the text.

Core Idea
A coincidental conditional is true when its parts are compatible, even if the connection is accidental.

In this passage, the author defines the fourth type of conditional proposition: the coincidental (ittifaqiyya). Unlike the necessary conditional, where the connection is essential, the coincidental has no necessary link. Understanding its truth conditions is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the opening sentence where the author defines the coincidental conditional. The term is 'الاتفاقية' and it is introduced as the fourth type.
2
Identify the truth condition
The author states that normally both extremes (antecedent and consequent) must be true, but sometimes only the consequent suffices. This leads to a distinction.
Note the reference to Avicenna (الرئيس).
3
Understand the compatibility requirement
The author clarifies that if the consequent were incompatible with the antecedent, the coincidental would not be true; otherwise, contradictories would coexist.
4
Distinguish particular from general
The first case (compatible) is called 'particular coincidental' and the second (incompatible, but rejected) is 'general coincidental'.
Only the particular is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ : الاِتِّفَاقِيَّةُ قَدِ اعْتُبِرَ فِيهَا صِدْقُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، وَقَدْ يُكْتَفَى فِيهَا بِصِدْقِ التَّالِي فَقَطْ ، فَيَجُوزُ تَرَكْبُهَا مِنْ مُقَدَّمٍ مُحَالٍ وَتَالٍ صَادِقٍ؛ فَإِنَّ الصَّادِقَ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ بَاقٍ عَلَى فَرْضِ كُلِّ مُحَالٍ صَرَّحَ بِهِ الرَّئيسُ . وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الثَّالِيَ لَوْ كَانَ مُنَافِيًا لِلْمُقَدَّم لَمْ تَصْدُقِ الإِنْفَاقِيَّةُ، وَإِلَّا أَمْكَنَ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ، وَتُسَمَّى الأُولَى اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ خَاصَّةٌ، وَالثَّانِيَةُ اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ ، قِيلَ: إِنَّ الاِتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ مُشْتَمِلَةٌ عَلَى الْعَلَاقَةِ ؛ لأَنَّ الْمَعِيَّةَ مُمْكِنَةٌ، فَلَهَا عِلَّةٌ، وَالْفَرْقُ أَنَّهَا اللُّزُومِيَّاتِ مَشْعُورٌ بِهَا، بِخِلَافِ الاِتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ. وَفِيهِ نَظَرُ، لِجَوَازِ أَنْ تَكُونَ اتِّفَاقِيَّةً، وَمُطْلَقُ الْعِلِّيَّةِ لَا يَسْتَوْجِبُ الِارْتِبَاطَ إِذَا كَانَتْ بِجِهَتَيْنِ مُخْتَلِفَتَيْنِ.
The fourth: The coincidental (conditional) – in it the truth of both extremes is considered, and sometimes mere truth of the consequent suffices in it, so it is permissible for it to be composed of an impossible antecedent and a true consequent; for what is true in itself remains true on the assumption of every impossible, as the Chief [Avicenna] stated. And the correct view is that if the consequent were incompatible with the antecedent, the coincidental would not be true, otherwise the conjunction of contradictories would be possible. The first is called 'particular coincidental' and the second 'general coincidental'. It is said that coincidentals involve a relation, because the conjunction is possible, so it has a cause, and the difference is that in necessitants the [relation] is perceived, unlike coincidentals. But this is open to objection, because it may be a coincidence, and absolute causality does not necessitate connection when the two [causes] are from different aspects.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines the coincidental conditional (al-ittifaqiyya), which is a type of conditional proposition where there is no necessary connection between antecedent and consequent. The author explains that typically both parts must be true for the conditional to be true, but some allow the consequent to be true even if the antecedent is impossible, because truth is preserved under any assumption. He then states the correct view that the consequent cannot be incompatible with the antecedent, otherwise the conditional would be false and would allow contradictories to coexist. This leads to the distinction between 'particular coincidental' (where the consequent is not incompatible) and 'general coincidental' (where even incompatibility is allowed?). Actually the text says the first is called particular (when consequent is not incompatible) and the second general (when it is incompatible? But that would be impossible. We need careful reading. The text: 'والحق أن التالي لو كان منافيا للمقدم لم تصدق الانفاقية، وإلا أمكن اجتماع النقيضين، وتسمى الأولى اتفاقية خاصة، والثانية اتفاقية عامة.' This is confusing. Probably the first (where consequent is not incompatible) is the particular, and the second (where it is incompatible) is the general, but the author rejects the general as impossible. So the general coincidental is the one where compatibility is ignored, but it leads to contradiction. Then the commentator discusses a claim that coincidentals have a relation (cause) because co-occurrence is possible, but differs from necessary conditionals in that the relation is not perceived. This is refuted because the co-occurrence may be accidental, and causality from different aspects does not require connection.

Key Terms
الاتفاقية
اللزومية
الرئيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what condition must hold for a coincidental conditional to be true (correct view)?
A) Both antecedent and consequent must be true.
B) The consequent must be true, even if the antecedent is impossible.
C) The consequent must be compatible with the antecedent.
D) The antecedent must be impossible.

Key Takeaway
The coincidental conditional is true only if its antecedent and consequent are compatible, ruling out the possibility of contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given these conditional statements from Islamic theology, identify which are coincidental and which are necessary: (a) 'If a person intentionally breaks his fast, then he must make up for it.' (b) 'If the moon is full, then it is night.' (c) 'If the world has a creator, then it is contingent.' Justify your answers using the text's conditions.
?
Construct a coincidental conditional on a topic from Islamic history (e.g., 'If the Battle of Badr occurred, then Abu Sufyan was present'). Explain why it is coincidental rather than necessary.

Debate on Causality in Coincidental Conditionals
Author refutes the claim that coincidentals involve a causal relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the claim that coincidental conditionals imply a causal relation and understand the refutation based on different causal aspects.

Core Idea
Co-occurrence does not entail necessary causation when causes are from different aspects.

The latter part of the passage addresses a claim that coincidental conditionals involve a causal relation because any conjunction has a cause. The author objects, arguing that absolute causality does not necessitate logical connection if the two events have separate causes. This debate is key to understanding the limits of causal reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Find the part where it is said that coincidentals involve a relation (علاقة) because conjunction is possible and thus has a cause.
2
Understand the basis
The claim assumes that every possible conjunction has a cause, which is a principle of causality.
3
Locate the objection
The author responds with 'وفيه نظر' (this is open to objection) and provides two reasons: it may be a mere coincidence, and absolute causality does not necessitate connection when the two causes are from different aspects.
This is the key refutation.
4
Apply the objection
Reflect on a real-world example: two events that occur together but have independent causes. The text uses this to show that a coincidental conditional does not imply a necessary logical relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرَّابِعُ : الاِتِّفَاقِيَّةُ قَدِ اعْتُبِرَ فِيهَا صِدْقُ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، وَقَدْ يُكْتَفَى فِيهَا بِصِدْقِ التَّالِي فَقَطْ ، فَيَجُوزُ تَرَكْبُهَا مِنْ مُقَدَّمٍ مُحَالٍ وَتَالٍ صَادِقٍ؛ فَإِنَّ الصَّادِقَ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ بَاقٍ عَلَى فَرْضِ كُلِّ مُحَالٍ صَرَّحَ بِهِ الرَّئيسُ . وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الثَّالِيَ لَوْ كَانَ مُنَافِيًا لِلْمُقَدَّم لَمْ تَصْدُقِ الإِنْفَاقِيَّةُ، وَإِلَّا أَمْكَنَ اجْتِمَاعُ النَّقِيضَيْنِ، وَتُسَمَّى الأُولَى اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ خَاصَّةٌ، وَالثَّانِيَةُ اتَّفَاقِيَّةٌ عَامَّةٌ ، قِيلَ: إِنَّ الاِتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ مُشْتَمِلَةٌ عَلَى الْعَلَاقَةِ ؛ لأَنَّ الْمَعِيَّةَ مُمْكِنَةٌ، فَلَهَا عِلَّةٌ، وَالْفَرْقُ أَنَّهَا اللُّزُومِيَّاتِ مَشْعُورٌ بِهَا، بِخِلَافِ الاِتِّفَاقِيَّاتِ. وَفِيهِ نَظَرُ، لِجَوَازِ أَنْ تَكُونَ اتِّفَاقِيَّةً، وَمُطْلَقُ الْعِلِّيَّةِ لَا يَسْتَوْجِبُ الِارْتِبَاطَ إِذَا كَانَتْ بِجِهَتَيْنِ مُخْتَلِفَتَيْنِ.
The fourth: The coincidental (conditional) – in it the truth of both extremes is considered, and sometimes mere truth of the consequent suffices in it, so it is permissible for it to be composed of an impossible antecedent and a true consequent; for what is true in itself remains true on the assumption of every impossible, as the Chief [Avicenna] stated. And the correct view is that if the consequent were incompatible with the antecedent, the coincidental would not be true, otherwise the conjunction of contradictories would be possible. The first is called 'particular coincidental' and the second 'general coincidental'. It is said that coincidentals involve a relation, because the conjunction is possible, so it has a cause, and the difference is that in necessitants the [relation] is perceived, unlike coincidentals. But this is open to objection, because it may be a coincidence, and absolute causality does not necessitate connection when the two [causes] are from different aspects.

According to the author, why does absolute causality not necessitate a logical connection in coincidental conditionals?
A) Because coincidental conditionals have no cause.

Key Takeaway

The Three Types of Disjunctive Conditional
Author explains the types of disjunctive conditional and their composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive conditionals and identify their logical structure in Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Disjunctions are single relations requiring exactly two parts.

In this lesson, we explore the fifth type of conditional proposition: the disjunctive (al-infiṣāl). The author distinguishes between true disjunction, exclusive disjunction, and exhaustive disjunction, and explains the debate over how many parts a disjunction can have. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for evaluating compound arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Find the terms al-ḥaqīqī (true disjunction), māniʿat al-jamʿ (exclusive), and māniʿat al-khuluw (exhaustive) in the Arabic passage. Note their definitions as given.
2
Understand the condition of each type
True disjunction: exactly one of the two parts is true. Exclusive (māniʿat al-jamʿ): both cannot be true but both can be false. Exhaustive (māniʿat al-khuluw): both cannot be false but both can be true.
3
Analyze the dispute on number of parts
The author reports a dispute: some say disjunction can have more than two parts (e.g., three alternatives), but the correct view is that it must be exactly two because a single relation cannot exist among more than two relata. The example 'every concept is either necessary, possible, or impossible' is actually a compound of a categorical and a disjunctive.
4
Apply the definitions to the three types
For each type, note the relationship between the two parts: true disjunction uses a proposition and its contradictory or equivalent; exclusive uses a proposition and something more specific than its contradictory; exhaustive uses a proposition and something more general than its contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْخَامِسُ : قَالُوا الاِنْفِصَالُ الْحَقِيقِيُّ لَا يُمْكِنُ إِلَّا بَيْنَ جُزْتَيْنِ، بِخِلَافِ مَانِعَةِ الْجَمْعِ ، وَمَانِعَةِ الْخُلَو . وَذَهَبَ جَمَاعَةٌ إِلَى أَنَّ الاِنْفِصَالَ مُطْلَقًا لَا يَتَحَصَّلُ إِلَّا مِنِ اثْنَيْنِ لَا أَزْيَدَ وَلَا أَنْقَصَ، وَمِثْلُ كُلَّ مَنْهُومٍ إِمَّا وَاجِبٌ أَوْ مُمْكِنٌ أَوْ مُمْتَنِعٌ مُرَكَّبٌ مِنْ حَمْلِيَّةٍ وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ ، وَزَعَمَ بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّهُ مُطْلَقًا يُمْكِنُ تَرْكِيبُهُ مِنْ أَجْزَاء فَوْقَ اثْنَيْنِ ، وَالْحَقُّ هُوَ الثَّانِي ؛ لِأَنَّ الاِنْفِصَالَ نِسْبَةٌ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَالنِّسْبَةُ الْوَاحِدَةُ لَا تُتَصَوَّرُ إِلَّا بَيْنَ اثْنَيْنِ، وَمَا قِيلَ إِنَّ فِيهِ مُصَادَرَةٌ ؛ لأَنَّهُ إِنْ أَرَادَ أَنَّ كُلَّ نِسْبَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ انْفِصَالِيَّةٍ أَوْ غَيْرِهَا ، فَهُوَ مَحَلَّ النزاع ، وَإِلَّا فَلَا يَنْفَعُ ، فَمَدْفُوعٌ بِمَا يُدْفَعُ بِهِ لُزُومُهَا فِي كُبْرَى الْأَوَّلِ ، فَتَأَمَّلْ . فَالْحَقِيقِيَّةُ لَا تَتَرَكَّبُ إِلَّا مِنْ فَضِيَّةٍ وَنَقِيضِهَا أَوْ مُسَاوِيهِ، وَمَانِعَةُ الْجَمْعِ مِنْهَا وَمِمَّا هُوَ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضُهَا ، وَمَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ مِنْهَا وَمِمَّا هُوَ أَعَمُ مِنْ نَقِيضُهَا ، هَذَا.
The fifth: They said that the true disjunction is only possible between two parts, unlike the exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and the exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw). And a group went to the view that absolute disjunction is only formed from two, not more nor less; like 'every concept is either necessary, possible, or impossible' is composed of a categorical and a disjunctive. And some claimed that absolutely it is possible to compose it from more than two parts, and the truth is the second [i.e., the first view? Actually 'the truth is the second' refers to the first view? The text says 'والحق هو الثاني' meaning the second opinion (the one that says only two), because disjunction is a single relation, and a single relation is inconceivable except between two. And what is said that this is a petitio principii—because if he means that every single relation, disjunctive or otherwise, it is the subject of dispute, otherwise it does not help—it is rebutted by what rebuts its implication in the first figure's major premise, so ponder. So the true disjunction is only composed of a proposition and its contradictory or its equivalent, and exclusive [is composed] of it and what is more specific than its contradictory, and exhaustive [is composed] of it and what is more general than its contradictory. That is it.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth type of conditional: the disjunctive (al-inqisal). The author first states that true disjunction (al-haqiqiyya) is only between two parts, unlike exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw). There is a dispute: some say all disjunctions must be between exactly two parts, giving the example 'every concept is either necessary, possible, or impossible' which is actually a combination of a categorical and a disjunctive. Others say disjunctions can have more than two parts. The correct view is that disjunction is a single relation requiring exactly two relata. An objection that this is circular is rebutted by analogy to the first figure's major premise. The author then defines the three types: true disjunction (haqiqiyya) between a proposition and its contradictory or equivalent; exclusive (mani'at al-jam') between a proposition and something more specific than its contradictory; exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw) between a proposition and something more general than its contradictory.

Key Terms
الانفصال الحقيقي
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the correct view regarding the number of parts in a disjunction?
A) A disjunction can have any number of parts.
B) A disjunction must have exactly two parts.
C) True disjunction must have two, others can have more.
D) The number of parts is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive conditionals are single relations between exactly two parts, and the three types—true, exclusive, and exhaustive—are defined by the relationship between those parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of Contradictory Propositions and the Eight Conditions
Author defines contradictory propositions and lists eight conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define contradictory propositions and list the eight conditions required for contradiction.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires identity in eight aspects to avoid false conflicts.

In this lesson, we will explore the precise definition of contradictory propositions as given by the author, and the eight conditions that ensure we are truly dealing with a contradictory pair. These conditions are crucial for valid logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
The definition says two propositions differ such that each necessitates the falsity of the other by its essence. This is achieved by affirmation and negation of the same thing.
2
Identify the eight conditions
The eight units: subject, predicate, whole/part, potential/actual, time, place, relation, condition. Some scholars include some in others.
3
Apply the conditions
When checking if two propositions are contradictory, verify that they agree in all eight aspects except in affirmation/negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتَنَاقُضِ الْقَضِيَّتَيْنِ اخْتِلَافُهُمَا ، بِحَيْثُ يَقْتَضِي لِذَاتِهِ صِدْقُ كُلِّ كَذِبَ الْأُخْرِى ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ ، وَذَلِكَ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ إِذَا كَانَ رَفْعَهُ بِعَيْنِهِ، فَلَا بُدَّ مِنِ الْحَادِ النِّسْبَةِ الْحُكْمِيَّةِ، وَحَصَرُوهُ فِي الْوَحْدَاتِ الثَّمَانِيَةِ الْمَشْهُورَةِ، وَبَعْضُهُمْ أَدْرَجَ بَعْضَهَا فِي بَعْضٍ. وَهَهُنَا شَكٌّ ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الْإِيجَابَ نَقِيضُ السَّلْبِ، وَمَنْ أَنْكَرَهُ فَخَرَقَ الإجْمَاعَ، وَسَلْبُ السَّلْب أَيْضًا رَفْعُهُ ، فَلِشَيْءٍ وَاحِدٍ نَقِيضَانِ ، وَمَنْ تَتَبَّثَ بِالْعَيْنِيَّةِ
فَقَدْ أَخْطَاً ; فَإِنَّ تَغَايُرَ المَفهوم ضَرُورِيٌّ ، وَهُوَ حَسْبِي.
وَنِعْمَ الْحَلُّ : إِنَّ السَّلْبَ لَا يُضَافُ حَقِيقَةٌ إِلَّا إِلَى الْوُجُودِ فِي نَفْسِهِ أَوْ لِغَيْرِهِ، فَسَلْبُ السَّلْبِ رَفْعُ وُجُودِ السَّلْبِ، وَهُوَ إِمَّا فِي قُوَّةِ الْمُوجَبَةِ السَّالِيةِ الْمَوْضُوعِ ، أَوِ الْمُوجَبَةِ الصَّالِيَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ، فَسَلْبُ السَّلْبِ الصَّالِبَة الصَّالِبَة نَقِيضُ الْمُوجَبَةِ السَّالِبَةِ ، لَا السَّالِبَةُ الْمُحَصَّلَةُ ، فَتَفَكَّرْ وَتَشَكَّرْ .
And the contradiction of two propositions is their difference such that each by its very essence necessitates the falsity of the other, and vice versa. And that is by affirmation and negation when the removal is the same. So there must be a boundary of the relational judgment, and they restricted it to the famous eight units, and some included some of them in others. And here is a doubt: that affirmation is the contradictory of negation, and whoever denies it violates consensus, and negation of negation is also its removal, so one thing has two contradictories. And whoever insists on identity has erred; for conceptual distinctness is necessary, and that is sufficient. What an excellent resolution: that negation is not truly attributed except to existence in itself or for another, so negation of negation is removal of the existence of negation, and that is either in the power of the negative subject or the affirmative predicate. So negation of a simple negative is the contradictory of the negative affirmative, not the positive negative. So reflect and thank.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage defines contradictory propositions (تناقض القضيتين) as those that differ such that each by its own nature requires the falsity of the other, achieved by affirmation and negation when the term being negated is the same. This requires a limit (الحد) on the relational judgment, which they restrict to the famous eight units (subject, predicate, whole/part, potential/actual, time, place, relation, condition); some merge conditions. Then a doubt: affirmation is contradictory of negation, but negation of negation is also a removal, so one proposition would have two contradictories. The resolution: negation properly applies only to existence, so 'negation of negation' means removing the existence of negation, resulting either in a negative subject/affirmative predicate or an affirmative subject/negative predicate. Therefore, the negation of a simple negative (السالبة السالبة) is the contradictory of the negative affirmative (الموجبة السالبة), not of the positive negative (السالبة المحصلة). This resolves the apparent duplication.

Key Terms
تناقض القضيتين
الإيجاب
السلب
الوحدات الثمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for two propositions to be contradictory?
A) They must differ in subject and predicate.
B) They must be related by affirmation and negation with identity in eight aspects.
C) They must be both affirmative.
D) They must be about the same time only.

Key Takeaway
Contradictory propositions are those that differ by essence in affirmation/negation while sharing the eight conditions of identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Take the two propositions 'Zakat is obligatory on gold' and 'Zakat is not obligatory on gold.' List which of the eight conditions are satisfied and which could be violated (e.g., if 'gold' means different things like gold jewelry vs. gold bullion, the subject differs).

The Doubt of Two Contradictories and Its Resolution
Author resolves a doubt about a thing having two contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a proposition cannot have two distinct contradictories and describe the resolution involving negation of negation.

Core Idea
A proposition has only one contradictory: the negation of a negation yields a different type of proposition, not a second contradictory.

A doubt arises: if affirmation contradicts negation, and negation of negation is also a removal (which sounds like negation), then one proposition would have two contradictories. The author resolves this by clarifying that 'negation of negation' is not the same as simple negation but rather a denial of existence of negation, resulting in an affirmative-like proposition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the doubt
The doubt is that a proposition (e.g., affirmative) has two contradictories: its simple negation and the negation of that negation. But a thing cannot have two contradictories.
2
Understand the resolution
The author explains that negation is properly attributed to existence. Negation of negation means removing the existence of negation, which results in either a negative subject/affirmative predicate or an affirmative subject/negative predicate.
3
Identify the two types of propositions
The source mentions three types: the simple negative (السالبة السالبة), the negative affirmative (الموجبة السالبة), and the positive negative (السالبة المحصلة). The negation of the simple negative is not the same as the positive negative.
4
Apply the resolution
So, a proposition's proper contradictory is only the direct affirmation or negation with the same conditions, not the double negation.

Source Text

Understanding Contradictory Propositions in Quantity and Modality
Author explains contradiction in quantity and modality and refutes a mistaken view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contradictory pairs based on quantity and modality and avoid the error of treating time-bound absolutes as contradictories.

Core Idea
Contradictories differ in quantity and modality; time-bound absolutes cannot contradict.

In this passage, the author sharpens our understanding of contradiction by specifying the required differences. Already we know contradictories cannot be both true or both false. Now we learn they must differ in two key dimensions: quantity (universal vs particular) and modality (e.g., necessary vs possible). Moreover, he warns against a seductive mistake: thinking two time-bound absolute propositions form a contradiction. Let's dissect each rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dimensions of difference
The author states that contradictories differ in quantity (كمة) and modality (جهة). This means a proper contradictory pair cannot share the same quantifier or the same modal quality. For example, a universal affirmative cannot contradict another universal; it must be a particular negative.
The passage uses 'كذب الكليتين' (falsity of both universals) and 'صدق الجزئيتين' (truth of both particulars) to show that two universals or two particulars alone do not form a contradiction.
2
Apply the quantity rule to a concrete example
Consider the proposition 'Every act of worship is a form of devotion' (universal affirmative). Its contradictory cannot be 'Every act of worship is not a form of devotion' (universal negative) because both can be false (some acts might be both or neither). Instead, the contradictory must be particular: 'Some act of worship is not a form of devotion' (particular negative).
This matches the rule that contradictories must be one universal and one particular.
3
Understand the modality rule and the error
The author says 'رفع كيفية كيفية أخرى' (the removal of a quality is another quality). To negate a modality, you replace it with another modal notion. He then criticizes those who posit contradiction between two time-bound absolute propositions (مطلقتان وقتيتان). For example, 'Zayd is writing at noon' and 'Zayd is not writing at noon' seem like contradictories, but the author says this is an error because the existence at a specific time can be removed by the removal of time itself (i.e., when time passes, both may become false or not properly relate).
The error is 'تخبيلا بأنهما كالشخصية' (being deluded that they are like singular propositions). Singular propositions can be contradictories, but time-bound absolutes are not singular; their truth depends on time.
4
Memorize the correct modal contradictions
The passage gives two explicit rules: (1) The contradictory of a necessary proposition is a possible general (ممكنة عامة). (2) The contradictory of a perpetual (دائمة) is an absolute general (مطلقة عامة), which is more general than the absolute (مطلقة). For example, if 'Every human is necessarily animal' is necessary, its contradictory is 'Some human is possibly not animal' (possible general).
These are standard logical relations in modal logic as applied in the Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ يَخْتَلِفَانِ كَمَّا ; لِكَذِبِ الْكُلِّيَتَيْنِ ، وَصِدْقِ الْجُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ، وَجِهَةٌ ; فَإِنَّ رَفْعَ كَيْفِيَّةٍ كَيْفِيَّةٌ أُخْرَى . وَمَنْ أَثْبَتَهُ بَيْنَ الْمُطْلَقَتَيْنِ الْوَقْتِيَّتيْن تَخْبِيلًا بِأَنَّهُمَا كَالشَّخْصِيَّةِ فَقَدْ غَلِطَ; فَإِنَّ النُّبُوتَ فِي وَقْتِ مُعَيَّنٍ يَجُوزُ رَفْعُهُ بِرَفْعِ الْوَقْتِ، فَالنَّقِيضُ لِلضَّرُورِيَّةِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ الْعَامَّةِ، وَلِلدَّائِمَةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْعَامَّةُ، وَهِيَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُطْلَقَةِ
Then they differ in quantity: due to the falsity of both universals and the truth of both particulars. And in modality: for the removal of a quality is another quality. And whoever affirms it between two time-bound absolutes, being deluded that they are like singular propositions, has erred. For existence in a specific time can be removed by the removal of time. So the contradictory of a necessary is the possible general, and of a perpetual is the absolute general, which is more general than the absolute.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of contradictory propositions by considering differences in quantity and modality. In quantity, contradictory propositions cannot both be universal (since they would both be false) and cannot both be particular (since they would both be true). In modality, removing one modality (quality) yields another. Then the author criticizes those who think two time-bound absolute propositions (مطلقتان وقتيتان) are contradictories, like singular propositions. This is a mistake because existence in a specific time can be removed by removing the time itself, not necessarily by a different proposition. The correct rule: the contradictory of a necessary proposition is a possible general (ممكنة عامة), and the contradictory of a perpetual (دائمة) is an absolute general (مطلقة عامة), which is more general than the absolute (مطلقة). This clarifies the proper modal opposition.

Key Terms

جزئية
جهة
مطلقة وقتية
ضرورية
ممكنة عامة
دائمة

B) Possible general

Key Takeaway
Contradictory propositions must differ in both quantity (universal vs particular) and modality (e.g., necessary vs possible general); time-bound absolutes are not proper contradictories.

Definition of Contradiction and the Eight Unities
Author defines contradictory propositions and states the requirement of unity of the relational aspect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions required for two propositions to be contradictory according to the eight unities.

Core Idea
Two propositions contradict only when they differ in truth value while sharing the same judgmental relation in all eight respects.

In logic, not every pair of opposite truth-value propositions are considered truly contradictory. The author defines the relation of contradiction (tanāquḍ) with a precise condition: the two propositions must share the same 'nisba hukmiyya' (judgmental relation), which is guaranteed by the eight unities. This lesson will help you parse the definition and understand why those unities matter.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition of tanāquḍ
Read the Arabic: the definition says two propositions differ such that each implies the falsehood of the other. Note the phrase 'biḥaythu yaqtaḍī li dhātihi ṣidqu kull wāḥidatin kadhiba al-ukhrā' — this is the core logical relation.
Contradiction is a relation between two propositions, not just a property of one.
2
Identify the condition of 'nisba hukmiyya'
The author states 'fa-lā budda min ittiḥād al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya' — there must be unity of the judgmental relation. This means the way the predicate is affirmed or denied of the subject must be identical in both propositions.
The nisba hukmiyya includes the mode of judgment (affirmation/negation) as well as the specific relation (e.g., necessary, possible, actual).
3
Enumerate the eight unities
The text mentions 'al-waḥdāt al-thamāniyya al-mashhūra' (the eight well-known unities). List them: unity of subject, predicate, time, place, condition, relation, potentiality, and part/whole. Each ensures that the difference is purely in truth value, not in some other aspect.
Some scholars collapse some unities (e.g., relation into condition).
4
Apply the unities to an example
Take two propositions: 'The world is temporally originated' and 'The world is not temporally originated'. Check each unity: same subject (world), same predicate (temporally originated), same time (present), same place (this world), etc. They satisfy all, so they are contradictory.
If one proposition said 'The world is temporally originated at the moment of creation' and the other said 'The world is not temporally originated now', they would differ in time unity and not be contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتَنَاقُضِ الْقَضِيَّتَيْنِ اخْتِلَافُهُمَا ، بِحَيْثُ يَقْتَضِي لِذَاتِهِ صِدْقُ كُلِّ وَاحِدَةٍ كَذِبَ الْأُخْرَى ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ ، وَذَلِكَ بِالْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ إِذَا كَانَ رَفْعَهُ بِعَيْنِهِ، فَلَا بُدَّ مِنِ اتِّحَادِ النِّسْبَةِ الْحُكْمِيَّةِ، وَحَصَرُوهُ فِي الْوَحْدَاتِ الثَّمَانِيَةِ الْمَشْهُورَةِ، وَبَعْضُهُمْ أَدْرَجَ بَعْضَهَا فِي بَعْضٍ.
And the contradiction of two propositions is their differing such that each by itself necessitates the falsehood of the other, and vice versa. And that is by affirmation and negation when their removal is the same. So there must be unity of the relational aspect, and they have confined it to the eight well-known unities, and some of them have incorporated some into others.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage defines the standard logical relation of contradiction (tanāquḍ) between two propositions. A pair of propositions are contradictory if and only if they differ in such a way that the truth of each entails the falsity of the other. This happens specifically when one is affirmative and the other negative, and the 'removal' (raf‘) — i.e., the negation — applies to exactly the same judgmental relation. This requires unity of the 'nisba hukmiyya' (the judgmental relation), which includes eight conditions known as the 'eight unities' (al-waḥdāt al-thamāniyya): unity of subject, predicate, time, place, condition, relation, potentiality, and part/whole. Some logicians have collapsed some of these into others. The eight unities ensure that the two propositions are truly opposites in the same respect.

Key Terms
تَنَاقُض
النِّسْبَةُ الْحُكْمِيَّةُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Unity of subject
B) Unity of predicate
C) Unity of quantity (universal/particular)
D) Unity of time

Key Takeaway
Contradiction requires not only opposite truth values but also full unity of the judgmental relation, encapsulated by the eight unities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Contradictories of Modal Propositions
List of contradictories for universal affirmative modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct contradictory for each type of universal affirmative modal proposition.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a modal proposition negates both quantity and modality.

Modal propositions are central to Islamic logic, appearing in arguments about necessity, perpetuity, and temporality. This passage gives exact contradictories for six modal types. Mastering these mappings allows you to correctly negate modal claims in kalam and usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal type
Scan the list and note each universal affirmative modal: ضرورية (necessary), دائمة (perpetual), مشروطة عامة (general conditional), عرفية عامة (general customary), وقتية مطلقة (temporal absolute), منشرة مطلقة (spread out absolute).
Each corresponds to a specific temporal or conditional scope.
2
Note the contradictory for each
For each modal, the author provides its contradictory in the form of a particular proposition: e.g., the contradictory of ضرورية is ممكنة عامة (particular possible).
The contradictory is always a particular, never a universal.
3
Understand the modality negation
The contradictory's modality is the 'removal' (رفع) of the original modality. For example, the negation of necessity yields possibility.
This aligns with the principle that the negation of a modality is another modality.
4
Apply the condition
The author warns: in the negative versions of these modals, the term must be a circumstance for what is removed, not for the removal itself. This ensures the contradictory is correctly formulated.
This prevents fallacious contradictions like treating temporal propositions as singular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ يَخْتَلِفَانِ كَمًّا ; لِكَذِبِ الْكُلِّيَّتَيْنِ ، وَصِدْقِ الْجُزْئِيَّتَيْنِ، وَجِهَةً ; فَإِنَّ رَفْعَ كَيْفِيَّةٍ كَيْفِيَّةٌ أُخْرَى . وَمَنْ أَثْبَتَهُ بَيْنَ الْمُطْلَقَتَيْنِ الْوَقْتِيَّتَيْنِ تَخْيِيلًا بِأَنَّهُمَا كَالشَّخْصِيَّةِ فَقَدْ غَلِطَ؛ فَإِنَّ الثُّبُوتَ فِي وَقْتٍ مُعَيَّنٍ يَجُوزُ رَفْعُهُ بِرَفْعِ الْوَقْتِ، فَالنَّقِيضُ لِلضَّرُورِيَّةِ الْمُمْكِنَةِ الْعَامَّةِ، وَلِلدَّائِمَةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْعَامَّةُ، وَهِيَ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْمُنتَشِرَةِ الْمَحْكُومِ فِيهَا بِالْفِعْلِيَّةِ فِي وَقْتٍ مَّا وَلِلْمَشْرُوطَةِ الْعَامَّةِ الْحِينِيَّةُ الْمُمْكِنَةُ الْمَحْكُومُ فِيهَا بِسَلْبِ الضَّرُورَةِ الْوَصْفِيَّةِ، وَلِلْعُرْفِيَّةِ الْعَامَّةِ الْحِينِيَّةُ المُطْلَقَةُ الْمَحْكُومُ فِيهَا بِالْفِعْلِيَّةِ الْوَصْفِيَّةِ، وَلِلْوَقْتِيَّةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْمُمْكِنَةُ الْوَقْتِيَّةُ الْمَحْكُومُ فِيهَا بِسَلْبِ الضَّرُورَةِ الْوَقْتِيَّةِ ، وَلِلْمُنْشِرَةِ الْمُطْلَقَةِ الْمُمْكِنَةُ الدَّائِمَةُ الْمَحْكُومُ فِيهَا بِسَلْبِ الضَّرُورَةِ الْمُنْتَشِرَةِ. كَذَا قَالُوا، وَذَلِكَ إِنَّمَا يَتِمُّ إِذَا كَانَ الطَّرْفُ فِي سَوَالِبِ هَذِهِ الْمُوَجَّهَاتِ ظَرْفًا لِلْمَرْفُوعِ ، لَا لِلرَّفْعِ .
Then they differ in quantity: because of the falsity of both universals and truth of both particulars, and in modality: for the removal of one modality is another modality. And whoever affirms contradiction between the two absolute temporal propositions through imagination that they are like singulars has erred, for the establishment in a specific time can be removed by removing the time. So the contradictory of the necessary (universal affirmative) is the particular possible; of the perpetual (universal affirmative) is the general absolute, which is more general than the particular absolute spread out, judged in it with actuality at some time; and of the general conditional (universal affirmative) is the particular possible temporal, judged in it with negation of essential necessity; and of the general customary (universal affirmative) is the particular absolute temporal, judged in it with actuality essential; and of the temporal absolute (universal affirmative) is the particular possible temporal, judged in it with negation of temporal necessity; and of the spread out absolute (universal affirmative) is the particular possible perpetual, judged in it with negation of spread out necessity. Thus they said, and that is only complete if the term in the negatives of these modal propositions is a circumstance for the removed (mafrū‘), not for the removal (raf‘).
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage provides detailed rules for the contradictories of modal propositions. The author begins by stating that contradictory propositions can differ in quantity (two universals cannot both be true, but two particulars can both be true) and in modality (since the negation of one modality yields another modality). He then criticizes those who think that two absolute temporal propositions (like 'All A is B at time t' and 'Some A is not B at time t') are contradictory like singular propositions; this is wrong because the predicate's establishment at a specific time can be negated by simply removing that time. The main part lists specific contradictories for six types of universal affirmative modal propositions: For ضرورية (necessary): contradictory is ممكنة عامة (particular possible). For دائمة (perpetual): contradictory is مطلقة عامة (general absolute). For مشروطة عامة (general conditional): contradictory is حينية ممكنة (temporal possible). For عرفية عامة (general customary): contradictory is حينية مطلقة (temporal absolute). For وقتية مطلقة (temporal absolute): contradictory is ممكنة وقتية (temporal possible). For منشرة مطلقة (spread out absolute): contradictory is ممكنة دائمة (perpetual possible). The author notes that this works only if, in the negative versions of these modals, the term serves as a circumstance for what is removed, not for the act of removal itself.

Key Terms
الضَّرُورِيَّة
الْمُمْكِنَة الْعَامَّة
الدَّائِمَة

Key Takeaway

Negating Composite Propositions: The Rule of Inclusive Disjunction
Author explains the rule for negating conjunctive propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradictory of any universal conjunctive proposition and express it as an inclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو).

Core Idea
The negation of a conjunction is an inclusive disjunction of the negations.

In this passage, the author explains how to negate composite propositions—those with multiple parts. The key insight is that the contradictory of a universal conjunctive proposition is not itself a conjunction but an inclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو) composed of the contradictories of its parts. This rule is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the composite proposition
Find a proposition with two parts joined by 'and' (conjunction). For example, 'Every A is B and every C is D'. This is a universal composite.
The passage focuses on universal composites; particular composites follow a different method.
2
Determine the type: universal or particular
If the composite is universal (كلية), the rule is straightforward. If particular (جزئية), the method involves alternating between the contradictories of the parts for each individual of the subject.
3
Derive the contradictory
For a universal composite, the contradictory is an inclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو) of the contradictories of each part. In logical terms: ¬(P ∧ Q) = ¬P ∨ ¬Q.
Inclusive disjunction means at least one negation is true, possibly both.
4
Apply to each part
Negate each component separately and connect them with 'or' (in the sense that at least one is true). The result is a composite proposition of the form 'Either not-P or not-Q'.
The contradictories of the parts must themselves be proper contradictories (e.g., 'not every A is B' or 'some A is not B').
5
Check the result satisfies the conditions
Ensure the resulting disjunction is indeed the contradictory: the original conjunction and the new disjunction cannot both be true and cannot both be false? Actually, for inclusive disjunction, they cannot both be false, but they can both be true? The passage states the contradictory must be a 'مانعة الخلو', meaning they cannot both be false. So the result is valid.
The passage also notes that for particular composites, the method yields a categorical proposition with a distributed predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُرَكَّبَةُ قَضِيَّةٌ مُتَعَدِّدَةٌ، وَرَفْعُ الْمُتَعَدِّدَةِ مُتَعَدِّدٌ، وَهُوَ رَفْعُ أَحَدِ الْجُزْأَيْنِ عَلَى سَبِيلِ مَنْعِ الْخُلُوِّ، وَالْكُلِّيَّةُ مِنْهَا لَا تَتَفَاوَتُ عِنْدَ التَّحْلِيلِ وَالتَّرْكِيبِ، فَنَقِيضُهَا مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنْ نَقِيضَيْ الْجُزْأَيْنِ. وَإِذَا أُرِيدَ مِنَ النَّقِيضِ هَهُنَا أَعَمَّ مِنَ الصَّرِيحِ وَاللَّازِمِ الْمُسَاوِي فَلَا يُسْتَبْعَدُ فِي كَوْنِهِ شَرْطِيَّةً أَوْ مُوجَبَةً، بِخِلَافِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ ; فَإِنَّ مَوْضُوعَ الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ فِيهَا وَاحِدٌ، فَالْجُزْئِيَّتَانِ أَعَمُّ، وَنَقِيضُ الْأَعَمِّ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِ الْأَخَصِّ. فَالطَّرِيقُ هُنَاكَ أَنْ تُرَدَّدَ بَيْنَ نَقِيضَيْ الْجُزْأَيْنِ بِالنَّسْبَةِ إِلَى كُلِّ فَرْدٍ مِنَ الْمَوْضُوعِ ، فَهِيَ قَضِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ مَرْدُودَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ . وَبَعْدَ اطلاعِكَ عَلَى حَقَائِقِ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ، وَنَقَائِضِ الْبَسَائِطِ تَتَمَكَّنُ مِنَ اسْتِخْرَاجِ التَّفَاصِيلِ. وَفِي الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ بَعْدَ الِاخْتِلَافِ كَيْفًا وَكَمًّا يَجِبُ الِاتِّحَادُ فِي الْجِنْسِ وَالنَّوْعِ، فَافْهَمْ.
And the composite proposition is a multiple proposition, and the negation of the multiple is multiple, and it is the negation of one of the two parts in the manner of preventing emptiness (i.e., inclusive disjunction). And the universal among them does not differ when analyzed and composed, so its contradictory is an inclusive disjunction composed of the contradictories of the two parts. And if by contradictory here is meant more general than the explicit and the necessary equivalent, then it is not far-fetched that it be a conditional or an affirmative, contrary to the particular; for the subject of affirmation and negation in it is one, so the two particular propositions are more general, and the contradictory of the more general is more specific than the contradictory of the more specific. So the method there is to alternate between the contradictories of the two parts with respect to each individual of the subject, so it is a categorical proposition with a distributed predicate. And after your knowledge of the realities of composites and the contradictories of simple propositions, you will be able to extract the details. And in conditionals, after differing in quality and quantity, unity in genus and species is required. So understand.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage deals with composite propositions, which are propositions containing multiple parts (typically conjunctions or disjunctions). The author states that the negation of a composite proposition is itself composite: it is the negation of one of the two parts in the manner of 'preventing emptiness' (man‘ al-khuluw), i.e., at least one negation must be true. This is effectively the negation of a conjunction, which yields a disjunction of the negations (De Morgan's law). For universal composites (e.g., 'All A are B and all C are D'), the contradictory is also an inclusive disjunction composed of the contradictories of the parts. The author then discusses the particular case: if the contradictory is taken in a broader sense (including both explicit contradictions and necessary equivalents), it may be a conditional or categorical affirmative. For particular composites, the subject is the same for both affirmation and negation, so the pair of particular propositions is more general, and the contradictory of the more general is more specific than the contradictory of the more specific. The method for finding the contradictory of a particular composite is to alternate between the contradictories of the two parts for each individual of the subject, resulting in a categorical proposition with a distributed predicate. Finally, the author advises that after understanding the nature of composites and the contradictories of simples, one can work out the details. For conditional propositions, after accounting for differences in quality and quantity, unity in genus and species is required for contradiction.

Key Terms
الْمُرَكَّبَة
مَنْعُ الْخُلُو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the contradictory of a universal conjunctive proposition according to the passage?
A) A universal disjunctive proposition
B) An inclusive disjunction (مانعة الخلو) composed of the contradictories of the parts
C) A particular categorical proposition
D) A conditional proposition

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a universal conjunctive proposition is an inclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Compound Proposition and Its Negation
Defining compounds and their negation rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the negation of a compound proposition using the rule of exclusive disjunction (mani' al-khulu).

Core Idea
The negation of a compound proposition is itself a compound: an exclusive disjunction of the negated parts.

The author defines a compound proposition as one composed of multiple parts, typically conditionals or disjunctives. Its negation, he explains, is also multiple, and must be understood through the concept of 'preventing emptiness' (mani' al-khulu). This lesson unpacks this definition and the precise method for forming the contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of compound proposition
Locate the phrase 'الْمُرَكَّبَةُ قَضِيَّةٌ مُتَعَدِّدَةٌ' – the author states a compound proposition is 'multiple', meaning it consists of more than one component statement (e.g., a conditional or disjunctive). Note the genus (proposition) and differentia (multiple).
This is not a full hadd but provides the key differentia.
2
Understand the negation rule for compounds
Read: 'وَرَفْعُ الْمُتَعَدِّدَةِ مُتَعَدِّدٌ، وَهُوَ رَفْعُ أَحَدِ الْجُزْئَيْنِ عَلَى سَبِيلِ مَنْعِ الْخُلُوِّ' – the negation is also multiple, but it negates exactly one of the two parts exclusively (mani' al-khulu). This means the contradictory is an exclusive disjunction of the negations.
Contrast with a simple conjunction of negations, which would be wrong.
3
Distinguish universal vs particular compounds
The author notes that for universal compounds, the contradictory is 'مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنْ نَقِيضَيِ الْجُزْئَيْنِ' – a compound of the two contradictories as an exclusive disjunction. For particulars, the method involves alternating the contradictories with respect to each individual subject, resulting in a categorical with a repeated predicate.
This distinction is subtle; focus on the universal case first.
4
Apply the rule to Islamic examples
Take a conditional proposition from kalam: 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a creator.' Its negation is: 'Either the world is not temporally originated or it has no creator (but not both).' Practise with fiqh conditionals like 'If the moon is sighted, then fast is obligatory.'
Ensure the negation is an exclusive disjunction, not a conjunction or inclusive disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُرَكَّبَةُ قَضِيَّةٌ مُتَعَدِّدَةٌ، وَرَفْعُ الْمُتَعَدِّدَةِ مُتَعَدِّدٌ، وَهُوَ رَفْعُ أَحَدِ الْجُزْئَيْنِ عَلَى سَبِيلِ مَنْعِ الْخُلُوِّ، وَالْكُلِّيَّةُ مِنْهَا لَا تَتَفَاوَتُ عِنْدَ التَّحْلِيلِ وَالتَّرْكِيبِ، فَنَقِيضُهَا مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنْ نَقِيضَيِ الْجُزْئَيْنِ. وَإِذَا أُرِيدَ مِنَ النَّقِيضِ هَهُنَا أَعَمُّ مِنَ الصَّرِيحِ وَاللَّازِمِ الْمُسَاوِي فَلَا يُسْتَبْعَدُ فِي كَوْنِهِ شَرْطِيَّةً أَوْ مُوجَبَةً، بِخِلَافِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ؛ فَإِنَّ مَوْضُوعَ الْإِيجَابِ وَالسَّلْبِ فِيهَا وَاحِدٌ، فَالْجُزْئِيَّتَانِ أَعَمُّ، وَنَقِيضُ الْأَعَمِّ أَخَصُّ مِنْ نَقِيضِ الْأَخَصِّ. فَالطَّرِيقُ هُنَاكَ أَنْ تُرَدَّدَ بَيْنَ نَقِيضَيِ الْجُزْئَيْنِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى كُلِّ فَرْدٍ مِنَ الْمَوْضُوعِ، فَهِيَ قَضِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ مَرْدُودَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ. وَبَعْدَ اطِّلَاعِكَ عَلَى حَقَائِقِ الْمُرَكَّبَاتِ، وَنَقَائِضِ الْبَسَائِطِ تَتَمَكَّنُ مِنِ اسْتِخْرَاجِ التَّفَاصِيلِ. وَفِي الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ بَعْدَ الِاخْتِلَافِ كَيْفًا وَكَمَا يَجِبُ الِاتِّحَادُ فِي الْجِنْسِ وَالنَّوْعِ، فَافْهَمْ.
The compound proposition is a multiple proposition, and the negation of the multiple is multiple, and it is the negation of one of the two parts on the way of preventing emptiness. And the universal among them does not differ in analysis and composition, so its contradictory is a 'preventive of emptiness' composed of the contradictories of the two parts. And if by 'contradictory' here is meant more general than the explicit and the equivalent necessary, then it is not unlikely that it is a conditional or an affirmative, unlike the particular; for the subject of affirmation and negation in it is one, so the two particulars are more general, and the contradictory of the more general is more specific than the contradictory of the more specific. The method there is to alternate between the contradictories of the two parts with respect to every individual of the subject, so it is a categorical proposition with a repeated predicate. And after your knowledge of the realities of compounds and the contradictories of simples, you will be able to extract the details. And in conditionals, after difference in quality and quantity, unification must occur in genus and species, so understand.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage defines the compound proposition (al-murakkaba) and explains how to form its negation. The author states that a compound proposition is multiple, meaning it consists of more than one part (typically a conditional or disjunctive). Its negation is also multiple: it is the negation of one of the two parts, but on the condition of preventing emptiness (mani' al-khulu), meaning it is an exclusive disjunction: exactly one of the two negations is true. For a universal compound, its contradictory is a compound of the contradictories of the two parts, also structured as an exclusive disjunction. The author then discusses the scope of 'contradictory': if it includes both the explicit contradictory and the equivalent necessary consequence, then it can be a conditional or a categorical affirmative, unlike the particular proposition where the subject of affirmation and negation is the same, so the two particulars are more general and their contradictory is more specific. For particulars, the method is to alternate the contradictory of the two parts with respect to each individual of the subject, resulting in a categorical proposition with a repeated predicate. The passage concludes that understanding these principles allows one to work out the details, and in conditionals, despite differences in modality and quantity, unity in genus and species must be observed.

Key Terms
الْمُرَكَّبَةُ
مَانِعَةُ الْخُلُوِّ
النَّقِيضُ

B) A compound of the contradictories of the two parts structured as 'mani' al-khulu'
C) The negation of both parts together as a conjunction
D) A simple categorical proposition

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a universal compound proposition is an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its parts, following the principle of mani' al-khulu.
