Self-Evident Premises (Awwaliyat)
Defines awwaliyat (self-evident) as the first type of certain premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify awwaliyat premises and distinguish them from other certain premises by checking whether the judgment occurs immediately upon conceiving the terms.

Core Idea
Self-evident truths are immediately known without any reasoning.

The first and most fundamental type of certain premises are the awwaliyat. These are judgments that the mind makes spontaneously as soon as it understands the terms involved. In this lesson, we will examine their definition, their degrees of clarity, and their role as the bedrock of demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the author's definition: awwaliyat are propositions that the mind judges immediately upon merely conceiving the two extremes (subject and predicate). The judgment is automatic, not requiring any external evidence or syllogism.
The extremes here mean the subject and predicate terms.
2
Identify the degree of clarity
The author notes that awwaliyat vary in clarity and obscurity. For example, 'the whole is greater than the part' is extremely clear, while others may be less obvious. He mentions the clearest example is 'the knowledge of knowledge itself' (self-awareness).
This variation does not affect their self-evident nature; they are still certain.
3
Test with an Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'The existence of a thing cannot be simultaneously affirmed and denied.' Upon understanding the terms, the mind immediately accepts it. This is an awwali used in Islamic theology to establish the principle of non-contradiction.
4
Distinguish from fitriyat
Awwaliyat require no mental process at all, whereas fitriyat (innate premises) require a syllogism, but the middle term is always present. Practice distinguishing: 'Two is half of four' is fitri because it needs the concept of division, even if obvious.
This distinction is crucial for classifying premises correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأُصُولُهَا الْأَوَّلِيَّاتُ ، وَهِيَ مَا يَجْزِمُ الْعَقْلُ فِيهَا بِمُجَرَّدِ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ ، نَظْرِيًّا أَوْ بَدِيهِيَّا، وَتَتَفَاوَتُ جَلَاء وَخَفَاءً ، وَبَدَاهَهُ الْبَدِيهِي كَعِلْم الْعِلْمِ مِنْهَا ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ . وَالْفِطَرِيَّاتُ، وَهِيَ مَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ لَا تَغِيبُ عَنِ الذَّهْنِ، وَتُسَمَّى قَضَايَا قِيَاسَاتُهَا مَعَهَا ، وَالْمُشَاهَدَاتُ إِمَّا بِحِسٍ ظَاهِرٍ، وَهِيَ الْحِسِّيَّاتُ ، أَوْ بِحِسٍ بَاطِنٍ، وَهِيَ الْوِجْدَانِيَّاتُ ، وَمِنْهَا الْوَهْمِيَّاتُ فِي الْمَحْسُوسَاتِ، وَمِنْهَا مَا تَجِدُهُ بِنُفُوسِنَا، لَا بِآلَاتِنَا وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْحِسَّ لَا يُفِيدُ إِلَّا حُكْمًا جُزْئِيًّا، وَالْمُنْكِرُونَ لِإِفَادَتِهِ صُمٌ وَعُمْي. وَالْحَدَسِيَّاتُ، وَهِيَ سُنُوحُ الْمَبَادِي الْمُرَتَّبَةِ دَفْعَةً، وَلَا يَجِبُ الْمُشَاهَدَةُ ، فَضْلًا عَنْ تَكْرَارِهَا، كَمَا قِيلَ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَطَالِبَ الْعَقْلِيَّةَ قَدْ تَكُونُ حَدَسِيَّةٌ. وَالتَّجَرُبِيَّاتُ ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَكْرَارِ فِعْلٍ ; حَتَّى يَحْصُلَ الْجَزْمُ. وَقَدْ نَازَعَ بَعْضُهُمْ فِي كَوْنِهَا مِنَ الْيَقِينِيَّاتِ ، كَالْحَدَسِيَّاتِ . وَالْمُتَوَاتِرَاتُ، وَهُوَ إِخْبَارُ جَمَاعَةٍ يُحِيلُ الْعَقْلُ تَوَاطُهُمْ عَلَى الْكَذِبِ، وَتَعْيِينُ الْعَدَدِ لَيْسَ بِشَرْطٍ، بَلِ الضَّابِطَةُ مَبْلَغٌ يُفِيدُ الْيَقِينَ. نَعَمْ، لَا بُدَّ فِيهِ مِنَ الانْتِهَاءِ إِلَى الْحِسَ، وَمُسَاوَاةِ الطَّرَفِ الْوَسَطَ . وَهَذِهِ الثَّلَاثُ الْأَخِيرَةُ لَا تَنْتَهِضُ حُجَّةٌ عَلَى الْغَيْرِ إِلَّا بَعْدَ الْمُشَارَكَةِ. وَحَصَرَ الْمَقَاطِعَ بَعْضُهُمْ فِي الْبَدِيهِيَّاتِ وَالْمُشَاهَدَاتِ ، وَلَهُ وَجْهٌ مَّا .
And their foundations are the self-evident propositions (awwaliyat), which are those that the mind judges immediately upon merely conceiving the two extremes, whether theoretical or intuitive, and they vary in clarity and obscurity. The most obvious of the intuitive is like the knowledge of knowledge itself, and that is the truth. And the innate ones (fitriyat) are those that need a medium that does not disappear from the mind; they are called propositions whose syllogisms are with them. And the observed ones (mushahadat) are either by an external sense, which are the sensory ones (hissiyat), or by an internal sense, which are the introspective ones (wijdaniyat). Among them are the estimative ones (wahmiyat) in the domain of sensibles, and among them are what you find in our souls, not by our instruments. The truth is that sense yields only a particular judgment; those who deny its efficacy are deaf and blind. And the intuitive ones (hadsiiyat) are the occurrence of ordered principles all at once; observation is not necessary, let alone repetition, as it is said; for intellectual demands may be intuitive. And the empirical ones (tajrubiyat) require repetition of an action until certainty is achieved. Some have disputed that they are among the certainties, like the intuitive ones. And the mutawatir ones (mutawatirat) are reports of a group whose collusion on falsehood is rationally impossible; specification of a number is not a condition; rather, the criterion is a magnitude that yields certainty. Indeed, it must end in sense, and the two extreme ends must be equal to the middle. And these last three do not constitute a proof against others except after shared experience. And some have restricted the types to the self-evident and the observed, and that has some validity.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the types of certain premises (usul al-yaqiniyyat) that serve as foundations for demonstration. The author enumerates them sequentially: First, awwaliyat (self-evident propositions): judgments the mind makes immediately upon understanding the subject and predicate, like the principle of non-contradiction or 'the whole is greater than the part'. These vary in clarity; the clearest is 'the knowledge of knowledge itself' (perhaps self-awareness). Second, fitriyat (innate propositions): these require a middle term, but that middle is so obvious it is always present in the mind, like 'two is half of four'—the syllogism is implicitly there. Third, mushahadat (observational propositions) are divided into hissiyat (external senses, e.g., 'the sun is shining') and wijdaniyat (internal senses, e.g., 'I am hungry'). Wahmiyat (estimative judgments) are included within sensibles, like judgments about the motion of a point. The author asserts that sense yields only particular judgments, and he harshly criticizes those who deny its utility. Fourth, hadsiyat (intuitive propositions): principles that occur to the mind suddenly, without repeated observation, like Archimedes' discovery. Fifth, tajrubiyat (empirical propositions): require repeated experiments to reach certainty, e.g., 'fire burns'. Some dispute their certainty. Sixth, mutawatirat (widespread reports): reports from a group too large to conspire on falsehood, no fixed number but a magnitude that gives certainty; they must trace back to sense and have equality of ends. The author notes that these last three (hadsiiyat, tajrubiyat, mutawatirat) do not serve as proof for others unless the other shares the experience. Finally, some scholars restrict certain premises to only the self-evident and observed, and this view has some face. This classification is crucial for understanding which premises yield certainty in demonstration.

Key Terms
أوليات
بديهيات

D) They need a syllogism that is always present in the mind.

Key Takeaway
Awwaliyat are certain premises known immediately by the mind without any reasoning; they are the foundations of all demonstration.

Innate Premises (Fitriyat)
Defines fitriyat as premises needing a medium always present in the mind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify fitriyat premises and explain how they differ from awwaliyat even though they seem immediate.

Core Idea
Innate premises only appear self-evident because the required syllogism is hidden in plain sight.

The second type of certain premises are the fitriyat, often called 'propositions whose syllogisms are with them.' These require a middle term, but that term never leaves the mind. In this lesson, we will unpack this concept and see why fitriyat are still certain but not strictly self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the author's definition: fitriyat are those that need a medium (middle term) that does not disappear from the mind. That means the syllogism is always implicitly present.
2
Grasp the phrase 'propositions whose syllogisms are with them'
This means that whenever you think of the proposition, the reasoning that supports it is also present, even if you don't actively reason. Example: 'Two is half of four' – the idea of division (the middle) is always there.
This is why fitriyat are sometimes mistaken for awwaliyat.
3
Compare with awwaliyat
Awwaliyat need no medium at all; fitriyat need a medium but it is always present. Practice: on the hot bar, classify each of the following: 'Justice is good' (awwali?) vs 'The whole is greater than the part' (awwali) vs 'Every effect has a cause' (fitri?)
The example 'every effect has a cause' is debated; some consider it awwali, others fitri.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider the proposition 'The world is temporally originated.' It requires a syllogism: 'The world is changeable, and everything changeable is temporally originated.' The middle term 'changeability' is always present in the mind when thinking of the world.
This example is from kalam arguments for the existence of a creator.

وَأُصُولُهَا الْأَوَّلِيَّاتُ ، وَهِيَ مَا يَجْزِمُ الْعَقْلُ فِيهَا بِمُجَرَّدِ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ ، نَظْرِيًّا أَوْ بَدِيهِيَّا، وَتَتَفَاوَتُ جَلَاء وَخَفَاءً ، وَبَدَاهَهُ الْبَدِيهِي كَعِلْم الْعِلْمِ مِنْهَا ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ . وَالْفِطَرِيَّاتُ، وَهِيَ مَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ لَا تَغِيبُ عَنِ الذَّهْنِ، وَتُسَمَّى قَضَايَا قِيَاسَاتُهَا مَعَهَا ، وَالْمُشَاهَدَاتُ إِمَّا بِحِسٍ ظَاهِرٍ، وَهِيَ الْحِسِّيَّاتُ ، أَوْ بِحِسٍ بَاطِنٍ، وَهِيَ الْوِجْدَانِيَّاتُ ، وَمِنْهَا الْوَهْمِيَّاتُ فِي الْمَحْسُوسَاتِ، وَمِنْهَا مَا تَجِدُهُ بِنُفُوسِنَا، لَا بِآلَاتِنَا وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْحِسَّ لَا يُفِيدُ إِلَّا حُكْمًا جُزْئِيًّا، وَالْمُنْكِرُونَ لِإِفَادَتِهِ صُمٌ وَعُمْي. وَالْحَدَسِيَّاتُ، وَهِيَ سُنُوحُ الْمَبَادِي الْمُرَتَّبَةِ دَفْعَةً، وَلَا يَجِبُ الْمُشَاهَدَةُ ، فَضْلًا عَنْ تَكْرَارِهَا، كَمَا قِيلَ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَطَالِبَ الْعَقْلِيَّةَ قَدْ تَكُونُ حَدَسِيَّةٌ. وَالتَّجَرُبِيَّاتُ ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَكْرَارِ فِعْلٍ ; حَتَّى يَحْصُلَ الْجَزْمُ. وَقَدْ نَازَعَ بَعْضُهُمْ فِي كَوْنِهَا مِنَ الْيَقِينِيَّاتِ ، كَالْحَدَسِيَّاتِ . وَالْمُتَوَاتِرَاتُ، وَهُوَ إِخْبَارُ جَمَاعَةٍ يُحِيلُ الْعَقْلُ تَوَاطُهُمْ عَلَى الْكَذِبِ، وَتَعْيِينُ الْعَدَدِ لَيْسَ بِشَرْطٍ، بَلِ الضَّابِطَةُ مَبْلَغٌ يُفِيدُ الْيَقِينَ. نَعَمْ، لَا بُدَّ فِيهِ مِنَ الانْتِهَاءِ إِلَى الْحِسَ، وَمُسَاوَاةِ الطَّرَفِ الْوَسَطَ . وَهَذِهِ الثَّلَاثُ الْأَخِيرَةُ لَا تَنْتَهِضُ حُجَّةٌ عَلَى الْغَيْرِ إِلَّا بَعْدَ الْمُشَارَكَةِ. وَحَصَرَ الْمَقَاطِعَ بَعْضُهُمْ فِي الْبَدِيهِيَّاتِ وَالْمُشَاهَدَاتِ ، وَلَهُ وَجْهٌ مَّا .
And their foundations are the self-evident propositions (awwaliyat), which are those that the mind judges immediately upon merely conceiving the two extremes, whether theoretical or intuitive, and they vary in clarity and obscurity. The most obvious of the intuitive is like the knowledge of knowledge itself, and that is the truth. And the innate ones (fitriyat) are those that need a medium that does not disappear from the mind; they are called propositions whose syllogisms are with them. And the observed ones (mushahadat) are either by an external sense, which are the sensory ones (hissiyat), or by an internal sense, which are the introspective ones (wijdaniyat). Among them are the estimative ones (wahmiyat) in the domain of sensibles, and among them are what you find in our souls, not by our instruments. The truth is that sense yields only a particular judgment; those who deny its efficacy are deaf and blind. And the intuitive ones (hadsiiyat) are the occurrence of ordered principles all at once; observation is not necessary, let alone repetition, as it is said; for intellectual demands may be intuitive. And the empirical ones (tajrubiyat) require repetition of an action until certainty is achieved. Some have disputed that they are among the certainties, like the intuitive ones. And the mutawatir ones (mutawatirat) are reports of a group whose collusion on falsehood is rationally impossible; specification of a number is not a condition; rather, the criterion is a magnitude that yields certainty. Indeed, it must end in sense, and the two extreme ends must be equal to the middle. And these last three do not constitute a proof against others except after shared experience. And some have restricted the types to the self-evident and the observed, and that has some validity.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the types of certain premises (usul al-yaqiniyyat) that serve as foundations for demonstration. The author enumerates them sequentially: First, awwaliyat (self-evident propositions): judgments the mind makes immediately upon understanding the subject and predicate, like the principle of non-contradiction or 'the whole is greater than the part'. These vary in clarity; the clearest is 'the knowledge of knowledge itself' (perhaps self-awareness). Second, fitriyat (innate propositions): these require a middle term, but that middle is so obvious it is always present in the mind, like 'two is half of four'—the syllogism is implicitly there. Third, mushahadat (observational propositions) are divided into hissiyat (external senses, e.g., 'the sun is shining') and wijdaniyat (internal senses, e.g., 'I am hungry'). Wahmiyat (estimative judgments) are included within sensibles, like judgments about the motion of a point. The author asserts that sense yields only particular judgments, and he harshly criticizes those who deny its utility. Fourth, hadsiyat (intuitive propositions): principles that occur to the mind suddenly, without repeated observation, like Archimedes' discovery. Fifth, tajrubiyat (empirical propositions): require repeated experiments to reach certainty, e.g., 'fire burns'. Some dispute their certainty. Sixth, mutawatirat (widespread reports): reports from a group too large to conspire on falsehood, no fixed number but a magnitude that gives certainty; they must trace back to sense and have equality of ends. The author notes that these last three (hadsiiyat, tajrubiyat, mutawatirat) do not serve as proof for others unless the other shares the experience. Finally, some scholars restrict certain premises to only the self-evident and observed, and this view has some face. This classification is crucial for understanding which premises yield certainty in demonstration.

What makes fitriyat different from awwaliyat?
A) Fitriyat are based on sense perception.

C) Fitriyat are never certain.
D) Fitriyat require repeated observation.

Key Takeaway
Fitriyat are certain premises that rely on an always-present syllogism, making them distinct from awwaliyat which require no reasoning at all.

?

Observational Premises (Mushahadat)
Defines mushahadat and its subdivisions: hissiyat, wijdaniyat, wahmiyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify observational premises into hissiyat, wijdaniyat, and wahmiyat, and explain the author's claim that sense yields only particular judgments.

Core Idea
Observational premises come from external or internal senses and yield particular, not universal, certainty.

The third category of certain premises is the observational (mushahadat). These include sensory perceptions from the external senses (hissiyat), introspection (wijdaniyat), and estimative judgments about sensibles (wahmiyat). The author emphasizes that sense gives only particular knowledge, but he defends its value against skeptics. Let's explore these subtypes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
The passage splits mushahadat into two: those from external senses (hissiyat) and those from internal senses (wijdaniyat). Wahmiyat are a subset of the latter.
2
Define each subtype
Hissiyat: judgments from the five senses, e.g., 'The sun is shining.' Wijdaniyat: judgments from inner awareness, e.g., 'I am hungry.' Wahmiyat: estimative judgments about sensibles, e.g., 'The point moves along the line.'
Wahmiyat are not based on direct sense but on the estimative faculty (wahm).
3
Analyze the author's defense of sense
The author says sense yields only a particular judgment (not universal). He strongly criticizes those who deny its efficacy, calling them deaf and blind. This affirms the reliability of sense for certain singular propositions.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
In hadith verification, a single reliable witness gives a particular observation (e.g., 'I saw the crescent moon'). This is a hissi judgment that can be used in fiqh if trustworthy.

وَأُصُولُهَا الْأَوَّلِيَّاتُ ، وَهِيَ مَا يَجْزِمُ الْعَقْلُ فِيهَا بِمُجَرَّدِ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ ، نَظْرِيًّا أَوْ بَدِيهِيَّا، وَتَتَفَاوَتُ جَلَاء وَخَفَاءً ، وَبَدَاهَهُ الْبَدِيهِي كَعِلْم الْعِلْمِ مِنْهَا ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ . وَالْفِطَرِيَّاتُ، وَهِيَ مَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ لَا تَغِيبُ عَنِ الذَّهْنِ، وَتُسَمَّى قَضَايَا قِيَاسَاتُهَا مَعَهَا ، وَالْمُشَاهَدَاتُ إِمَّا بِحِسٍ ظَاهِرٍ، وَهِيَ الْحِسِّيَّاتُ ، أَوْ بِحِسٍ بَاطِنٍ، وَهِيَ الْوِجْدَانِيَّاتُ ، وَمِنْهَا الْوَهْمِيَّاتُ فِي الْمَحْسُوسَاتِ، وَمِنْهَا مَا تَجِدُهُ بِنُفُوسِنَا، لَا بِآلَاتِنَا وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْحِسَّ لَا يُفِيدُ إِلَّا حُكْمًا جُزْئِيًّا، وَالْمُنْكِرُونَ لِإِفَادَتِهِ صُمٌ وَعُمْي. وَالْحَدَسِيَّاتُ، وَهِيَ سُنُوحُ الْمَبَادِي الْمُرَتَّبَةِ دَفْعَةً، وَلَا يَجِبُ الْمُشَاهَدَةُ ، فَضْلًا عَنْ تَكْرَارِهَا، كَمَا قِيلَ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَطَالِبَ الْعَقْلِيَّةَ قَدْ تَكُونُ حَدَسِيَّةٌ. وَالتَّجَرُبِيَّاتُ ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَكْرَارِ فِعْلٍ ; حَتَّى يَحْصُلَ الْجَزْمُ. وَقَدْ نَازَعَ بَعْضُهُمْ فِي كَوْنِهَا مِنَ الْيَقِينِيَّاتِ ، كَالْحَدَسِيَّاتِ . وَالْمُتَوَاتِرَاتُ، وَهُوَ إِخْبَارُ جَمَاعَةٍ يُحِيلُ الْعَقْلُ تَوَاطُهُمْ عَلَى الْكَذِبِ، وَتَعْيِينُ الْعَدَدِ لَيْسَ بِشَرْطٍ، بَلِ الضَّابِطَةُ مَبْلَغٌ يُفِيدُ الْيَقِينَ. نَعَمْ، لَا بُدَّ فِيهِ مِنَ الانْتِهَاءِ إِلَى الْحِسَ، وَمُسَاوَاةِ الطَّرَفِ الْوَسَطَ . وَهَذِهِ الثَّلَاثُ الْأَخِيرَةُ لَا تَنْتَهِضُ حُجَّةٌ عَلَى الْغَيْرِ إِلَّا بَعْدَ الْمُشَارَكَةِ. وَحَصَرَ الْمَقَاطِعَ بَعْضُهُمْ فِي الْبَدِيهِيَّاتِ وَالْمُشَاهَدَاتِ ، وَلَهُ وَجْهٌ مَّا .
And their foundations are the self-evident propositions (awwaliyat), which are those that the mind judges immediately upon merely conceiving the two extremes, whether theoretical or intuitive, and they vary in clarity and obscurity. The most obvious of the intuitive is like the knowledge of knowledge itself, and that is the truth. And the innate ones (fitriyat) are those that need a medium that does not disappear from the mind; they are called propositions whose syllogisms are with them. And the observed ones (mushahadat) are either by an external sense, which are the sensory ones (hissiyat), or by an internal sense, which are the introspective ones (wijdaniyat). Among them are the estimative ones (wahmiyat) in the domain of sensibles, and among them are what you find in our souls, not by our instruments. The truth is that sense yields only a particular judgment; those who deny its efficacy are deaf and blind. And the intuitive ones (hadsiiyat) are the occurrence of ordered principles all at once; observation is not necessary, let alone repetition, as it is said; for intellectual demands may be intuitive. And the empirical ones (tajrubiyat) require repetition of an action until certainty is achieved. Some have disputed that they are among the certainties, like the intuitive ones. And the mutawatir ones (mutawatirat) are reports of a group whose collusion on falsehood is rationally impossible; specification of a number is not a condition; rather, the criterion is a magnitude that yields certainty. Indeed, it must end in sense, and the two extreme ends must be equal to the middle. And these last three do not constitute a proof against others except after shared experience. And some have restricted the types to the self-evident and the observed, and that has some validity.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the types of certain premises (usul al-yaqiniyyat) that serve as foundations for demonstration. The author enumerates them sequentially: First, awwaliyat (self-evident propositions): judgments the mind makes immediately upon understanding the subject and predicate, like the principle of non-contradiction or 'the whole is greater than the part'. These vary in clarity; the clearest is 'the knowledge of knowledge itself' (perhaps self-awareness). Second, fitriyat (innate propositions): these require a middle term, but that middle is so obvious it is always present in the mind, like 'two is half of four'—the syllogism is implicitly there. Third, mushahadat (observational propositions) are divided into hissiyat (external senses, e.g., 'the sun is shining') and wijdaniyat (internal senses, e.g., 'I am hungry'). Wahmiyat (estimative judgments) are included within sensibles, like judgments about the motion of a point. The author asserts that sense yields only particular judgments, and he harshly criticizes those who deny its utility. Fourth, hadsiyat (intuitive propositions): principles that occur to the mind suddenly, without repeated observation, like Archimedes' discovery. Fifth, tajrubiyat (empirical propositions): require repeated experiments to reach certainty, e.g., 'fire burns'. Some dispute their certainty. Sixth, mutawatirat (widespread reports): reports from a group too large to conspire on falsehood, no fixed number but a magnitude that gives certainty; they must trace back to sense and have equality of ends. The author notes that these last three (hadsiiyat, tajrubiyat, mutawatirat) do not serve as proof for others unless the other shares the experience. Finally, some scholars restrict certain premises to only the self-evident and observed, and this view has some face. This classification is crucial for understanding which premises yield certainty in demonstration.

Intuitive and Empirical Premises (Hadsiiyat and Tajrubiyat)
Defines hadsiiyat (intuitive) and tajrubiyat (empirical) as separate types of certain premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish intuitive premises (hadsiiyat) from empirical premises (tajrubiyat) and explain the role of repetition in achieving certainty.

Core Idea
Intuition is sudden insight without repetition; empiricism requires repeated observation to yield certainty.

The fourth and fifth types are intuitive (hadsiiyat) and empirical (tajrubiyat) premises. The author contrasts them: intuition delivers principles in a flash without prior observation, while empirical truths need repeated experimentation to become certain. Both are considered certain, but there was debate among scholars about their status. Let's examine them.

Guided Path
1
Understand hadsiiyat (intuitive)
The author says hadsiiyat are the sudden occurrence of ordered principles all at once. Observation is not necessary, nor repetition. Example: Archimedes' discovery of buoyancy or a mathematician seeing a theorem instantly.
Note: The author says 'as it is said', suggesting this is a known view.
2
Understand tajrubiyat (empirical)
Tajrubiyat require repetition of an action (experiment) until certainty is reached. Example: 'Fire burns' after many observations. The author mentions that some scholars dispute whether these are truly certain.
3
Compare the two types
Hadsiiyat do not need observation; tajrubiyat do. Hadsiiyat are a single insight; tajrubiyat build up from multiple instances. Both can yield certainty, but tajrubiyat's certainty is cumulative.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
In medicine (a science studied by Muslims), the knowledge that 'cupping reduces fever' is tajrubi – learned from repeated practice. In contrast, a physicist's sudden insight about a natural law could be hadsi.
Islamic scholars like Ibn Sina used both types in their works.

وَأُصُولُهَا الْأَوَّلِيَّاتُ ، وَهِيَ مَا يَجْزِمُ الْعَقْلُ فِيهَا بِمُجَرَّدِ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ ، نَظْرِيًّا أَوْ بَدِيهِيَّا، وَتَتَفَاوَتُ جَلَاء وَخَفَاءً ، وَبَدَاهَهُ الْبَدِيهِي كَعِلْم الْعِلْمِ مِنْهَا ، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ . وَالْفِطَرِيَّاتُ، وَهِيَ مَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ لَا تَغِيبُ عَنِ الذَّهْنِ، وَتُسَمَّى قَضَايَا قِيَاسَاتُهَا مَعَهَا ، وَالْمُشَاهَدَاتُ إِمَّا بِحِسٍ ظَاهِرٍ، وَهِيَ الْحِسِّيَّاتُ ، أَوْ بِحِسٍ بَاطِنٍ، وَهِيَ الْوِجْدَانِيَّاتُ ، وَمِنْهَا الْوَهْمِيَّاتُ فِي الْمَحْسُوسَاتِ، وَمِنْهَا مَا تَجِدُهُ بِنُفُوسِنَا، لَا بِآلَاتِنَا وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْحِسَّ لَا يُفِيدُ إِلَّا حُكْمًا جُزْئِيًّا، وَالْمُنْكِرُونَ لِإِفَادَتِهِ صُمٌ وَعُمْي. وَالْحَدَسِيَّاتُ، وَهِيَ سُنُوحُ الْمَبَادِي الْمُرَتَّبَةِ دَفْعَةً، وَلَا يَجِبُ الْمُشَاهَدَةُ ، فَضْلًا عَنْ تَكْرَارِهَا، كَمَا قِيلَ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَطَالِبَ الْعَقْلِيَّةَ قَدْ تَكُونُ حَدَسِيَّةٌ. وَالتَّجَرُبِيَّاتُ ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَكْرَارِ فِعْلٍ ; حَتَّى يَحْصُلَ الْجَزْمُ. وَقَدْ نَازَعَ بَعْضُهُمْ فِي كَوْنِهَا مِنَ الْيَقِينِيَّاتِ ، كَالْحَدَسِيَّاتِ . وَالْمُتَوَاتِرَاتُ، وَهُوَ إِخْبَارُ جَمَاعَةٍ يُحِيلُ الْعَقْلُ تَوَاطُهُمْ عَلَى الْكَذِبِ، وَتَعْيِينُ الْعَدَدِ لَيْسَ بِشَرْطٍ، بَلِ الضَّابِطَةُ مَبْلَغٌ يُفِيدُ الْيَقِينَ. نَعَمْ، لَا بُدَّ فِيهِ مِنَ الانْتِهَاءِ إِلَى الْحِسَ، وَمُسَاوَاةِ الطَّرَفِ الْوَسَطَ . وَهَذِهِ الثَّلَاثُ الْأَخِيرَةُ لَا تَنْتَهِضُ حُجَّةٌ عَلَى الْغَيْرِ إِلَّا بَعْدَ الْمُشَارَكَةِ. وَحَصَرَ الْمَقَاطِعَ بَعْضُهُمْ فِي الْبَدِيهِيَّاتِ وَالْمُشَاهَدَاتِ ، وَلَهُ وَجْهٌ مَّا .
And their foundations are the self-evident propositions (awwaliyat), which are those that the mind judges immediately upon merely conceiving the two extremes, whether theoretical or intuitive, and they vary in clarity and obscurity. The most obvious of the intuitive is like the knowledge of knowledge itself, and that is the truth. And the innate ones (fitriyat) are those that need a medium that does not disappear from the mind; they are called propositions whose syllogisms are with them. And the observed ones (mushahadat) are either by an external sense, which are the sensory ones (hissiyat), or by an internal sense, which are the introspective ones (wijdaniyat). Among them are the estimative ones (wahmiyat) in the domain of sensibles, and among them are what you find in our souls, not by our instruments. The truth is that sense yields only a particular judgment; those who deny its efficacy are deaf and blind. And the intuitive ones (hadsiiyat) are the occurrence of ordered principles all at once; observation is not necessary, let alone repetition, as it is said; for intellectual demands may be intuitive. And the empirical ones (tajrubiyat) require repetition of an action until certainty is achieved. Some have disputed that they are among the certainties, like the intuitive ones. And the mutawatir ones (mutawatirat) are reports of a group whose collusion on falsehood is rationally impossible; specification of a number is not a condition; rather, the criterion is a magnitude that yields certainty. Indeed, it must end in sense, and the two extreme ends must be equal to the middle. And these last three do not constitute a proof against others except after shared experience. And some have restricted the types to the self-evident and the observed, and that has some validity.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the types of certain premises (usul al-yaqiniyyat) that serve as foundations for demonstration. The author enumerates them sequentially: First, awwaliyat (self-evident propositions): judgments the mind makes immediately upon understanding the subject and predicate, like the principle of non-contradiction or 'the whole is greater than the part'. These vary in clarity; the clearest is 'the knowledge of knowledge itself' (perhaps self-awareness). Second, fitriyat (innate propositions): these require a middle term, but that middle is so obvious it is always present in the mind, like 'two is half of four'—the syllogism is implicitly there. Third, mushahadat (observational propositions) are divided into hissiyat (external senses, e.g., 'the sun is shining') and wijdaniyat (internal senses, e.g., 'I am hungry'). Wahmiyat (estimative judgments) are included within sensibles, like judgments about the motion of a point. The author asserts that sense yields only particular judgments, and he harshly criticizes those who deny its utility. Fourth, hadsiyat (intuitive propositions): principles that occur to the mind suddenly, without repeated observation, like Archimedes' discovery. Fifth, tajrubiyat (empirical propositions): require repeated experiments to reach certainty, e.g., 'fire burns'. Some dispute their certainty. Sixth, mutawatirat (widespread reports): reports from a group too large to conspire on falsehood, no fixed number but a magnitude that gives certainty; they must trace back to sense and have equality of ends. The author notes that these last three (hadsiiyat, tajrubiyat, mutawatirat) do not serve as proof for others unless the other shares the experience. Finally, some scholars restrict certain premises to only the self-evident and observed, and this view has some face. This classification is crucial for understanding which premises yield certainty in demonstration.

B) Hadsiiyat are sudden insights without observation; tajrubiyat require repeated observation.
C) Hadsiiyat are always certain; tajrubiyat are never certain.
D) Hadsiiyat are based on sensory evidence; tajrubiyat are not.

Key Takeaway
Intuitive premises are sudden intellectual insights; empirical premises are built from repeated observations and both are considered certain, though debated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Propositions

Mutawatirat and Concluding Remarks
Defines mutawatirat and adds final remarks about sharing experience and alternative classifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mutawatirat, explain the conditions for certainty, and discuss the limitation that these premises only constitute proof for those who share the experience.

Core Idea
Mutawatir reports yield certainty when the group is large enough to preclude conspiracy, but they only convince those who accept the report's foundations.

The final type of certain premises is the mutawatir report – information transmitted by a multitude so large that collusion on falsehood is impossible. The author clarifies that no fixed number is required; the criterion is the certainty it generates. He also notes that the last three types (hadsii, tajrubi, mutawatir) do not constitute proof for others unless they share the underlying experience. Finally, he mentions alternative classifications. Let's explore these nuanced points.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of mutawatirat
Mutawatirat are reports from a group whose collusion on falsehood is rationally impossible. The author emphasizes that there is no specific number; what matters is that the report reaches a level that yields certainty.
This is similar to the hadith concept of tawatur.
2
Note the conditions
The author adds two conditions: the report must ultimately trace back to sense (e.g., eyewitnesses), and the two ends (the original witnesses and the final recipients) must be equal to the middle (the chain of transmission must be symmetrical in reliability).
'Equality of ends to middle' ensures each link is as reliable as the first.
3
Understand the epistemic limitation
The author states that the last three types (hadsiiyat, tajrubiyat, mutawatirat) do not constitute proof against others unless the other person shares the experience (shared observation, experiment, or report). That is, one cannot force conviction on someone who has not had the same sensory or testimonial basis.
4
Consider alternative classifications
Some scholars restrict certain premises to only the self-evident and the observed. The author finds this view has some face, indicating that not all logicians accept the full list.
This reminds us that classifications can vary between schools.

And their foundations are the self-evident propositions (awwaliyat), which are those that the mind judges immediately upon merely conceiving the two extremes, whether theoretical or intuitive, and they vary in clarity and obscurity. The most obvious of the intuitive is like the knowledge of knowledge itself, and that is the truth. And the innate ones (fitriyat) are those that need a medium that does not disappear from the mind; they are called propositions whose syllogisms are with them. And the observed ones (mushahadat) are either by an external sense, which are the sensory ones (hissiyat), or by an internal sense, which are the introspective ones (wijdaniyat). Among them are the estimative ones (wahmiyat) in the domain of sensibles, and among them are what you find in our souls, not by our instruments. The truth is that sense yields only a particular judgment; those who deny its efficacy are deaf and blind. And the intuitive ones (hadsiiyat) are the occurrence of ordered principles all at once; observation is not necessary, let alone repetition, as it is said; for intellectual demands may be intuitive. And the empirical ones (tajrubiyat) require repetition of an action until certainty is achieved. Some have disputed that they are among the certainties, like the intuitive ones. And the mutawatir ones (mutawatirat) are reports of a group whose collusion on falsehood is rationally impossible; specification of a number is not a condition; rather, the criterion is a magnitude that yields certainty. Indeed, it must end in sense, and the two extreme ends must be equal to the middle. And these last three do not constitute a proof against others except after shared experience. Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the types of certain premises (usul al-yaqiniyyat) that serve as foundations for demonstration. The author enumerates them sequentially: First, awwaliyat (self-evident propositions): judgments the mind makes immediately upon understanding the subject and predicate, like the principle of non-contradiction or 'the whole is greater than the part'. These vary in clarity; the clearest is 'the knowledge of knowledge itself' (perhaps self-awareness). Second, fitriyat (innate propositions): these require a middle term, but that middle is so obvious it is always present in the mind, like 'two is half of four'—the syllogism is implicitly there. Third, mushahadat (observational propositions) are divided into hissiyat (external senses, e.g., 'the sun is shining') and wijdaniyat (internal senses, e.g., 'I am hungry'). Wahmiyat (estimative judgments) are included within sensibles, like judgments about the motion of a point. The author asserts that sense yields only particular judgments, and he harshly criticizes those who deny its utility. Fourth, hadsiyat (intuitive propositions): principles that occur to the mind suddenly, without repeated observation, like Archimedes' discovery. Fifth, tajrubiyat (empirical propositions): require repeated experiments to reach certainty, e.g., 'fire burns'. Some dispute their certainty. Sixth, mutawatirat (widespread reports): reports from a group too large to conspire on falsehood, no fixed number but a magnitude that gives certainty; they must trace back to sense and have equality of ends. The author notes that these last three (hadsiiyat, tajrubiyat, mutawatirat) do not serve as proof for others unless the other shares the experience. Finally, some scholars restrict certain premises to only the self-evident and observed, and this view has some face. This classification is crucial for understanding which premises yield certainty in demonstration.

Propositions

The Six Certain Propositions in Islamic Logic
Author enumerates and defines types of certain propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between the six types of certain propositions (yaqiniyyat) as defined in the Islamic logical tradition, and explain their respective sources of certainty.

Core Idea
Certain knowledge springs from self-evidence, innate reason, sense, intuition, experiment, and recurrent testimony.

This passage classifies propositions that yield absolute certainty (yaqin). These are the building blocks of all demonstrative knowledge in Islamic sciences. Understanding each type—from the self-evident (badihiyyat) to the recurrent (mutawatirat)—is crucial for evaluating the epistemic strength of premises in kalam, fiqh, and usul al-fiqh. Let's walk through the definitions and see how they differ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching criterion for certainty
The author begins by stating that certain propositions are those the mind judges by mere conception of the two extremes (subject and predicate). This applies to all yaqiniyyat, whether intuitive or theoretical.
This is the common feature: the mind's definitive judgment.
2
Distinguish the source of each type
For each type, identify what makes it certain: for badihiyyat, no middle term is needed; for fitriyyat, a middle term is always present implicitly; for mushahadat, it is sense observation (external or internal); for hadsiyyat, sudden intuition without repetition; for tajribiyyat, repeated experience; for mutawatirat, a consensus of reports that precludes collusion.
Note the subcategories of mushahadat: hissiyyat (external), wijdaniyyat (internal), and wahmiyyat (estimative judgments about sensibles).
3
Note the conditions for using the last three as proof
The author explains that hadsiyyat, tajribiyyat, and mutawatirat cannot be used as proof against someone who does not share the same experience or report. They require 'participation' to be valid evidence.
This is important for jadal (debate) contexts.
4
Consider an alternative classification
The passage ends by noting that some scholars reduce all yaqiniyyat to just badihiyyat and mushahadat, considering the others as subcategories. This reflects a different epistemological approach.
This summarises a scholarly position.

مَا يَجْزِمُ الْعَقْلُ فِيهَا بِمُجَرَّدِ تَصَوُّرِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، نَظْرِيًّا أَوْ بَدِيهِيًّا، وَتَتَفَاوَتُ جَلَاءً وَخَفَاءً، وَبَدَاهَةُ الْبَدِيهِي كَعِلْمِ الْعِلْمِ مِنْهَا، وَهُوَ الْحَقُّ. وَالْفِطَرِيَّاتُ، وَهِيَ مَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى وَاسِطَةٍ لَا تَغِيبُ عَنِ الذَّهْنِ، وَتُسَمَّى قَضَايَا قِيَاسَاتُهَا مَعَهَا، وَالْمُشَاهَدَاتُ إِمَّا بِحِسٍّ ظَاهِرٍ، وَهِيَ الْحِسِّيَّاتُ، أَوْ بِحِسٍّ بَاطِنٍ، وَهِيَ الْوِجْدَانِيَّاتُ، وَمِنْهَا الْوَهْمِيَّاتُ فِي الْمَحْسُوسَاتِ، وَمِنْهَا مَا تَجِدُهُ بِنُفُوسِنَا، لَا بِآلَتِنَا. وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّ الْحِسَّ لَا يُفِيدُ إِلَّا حُكْمًا جُزْئِيًّا، وَالْمُنْكِرُونَ لِإِفَادَتِهِ صُمٌّ وَعُمْيٌ. وَالْحَدَسِيَّاتُ، وَهِيَ سُنُوحُ الْمَبَادِي الْمُرَتَّبَةِ دَفْعَةً، وَلَا يَجِبُ الْمُشَاهَدَةُ، فَضْلًا عَنْ تَكْرَارِهَا، كَمَا قِيلَ؛ فَإِنَّ الْمَطَالِبَ الْعَقْلِيَّةَ قَدْ تَكُونُ حَدَسِيَّةً. وَالتَّجْرُبِيَّاتُ، وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ تَكْرَارِ فِعْلٍ؛ حَتَّى يَحْصُلَ الْجَزْمُ. وَقَدْ نَازَعَ بَعْضُهُمْ فِي كَوْنِهَا مِنَ الْيَقِينِيَّاتِ، كَالْحَدَسِيَّاتِ. وَالْمُتَوَاتِرَاتُ، وَهُوَ إِخْبَارُ جَمَاعَةٍ يُحِيلُ الْعَقْلُ تَوَاطُؤَهُمْ عَلَى الْكَذِبِ، وَتَعْيِينُ الْعَدَدِ لَيْسَ بِشَرْطٍ، بَلِ الضَّابِطَةُ مَبْلَغٌ يُفِيدُ الْيَقِينَ. نَعَمْ، لَا بُدَّ فِيهِ مِنَ الِانْتِهَاءِ إِلَى الْحِسِّ، وَمُسَاوَاةِ الطَّرَفِ الْوَسَطَ. وَهَذِهِ الثَّلَاثُ الْأَخِيرَةُ لَا تَنْتَهِضُ حُجَّةً عَلَى الْغَيْرِ إِلَّا بَعْدَ الْمُشَارَكَةِ. وَحَصَرَ الْمَقَاطِعَ بَعْضُهُمْ فِي الْبَدِيهِيَّاتِ وَالْمُشَاهَدَاتِ، وَلَهُ وَجْهٌ مَّا.
What the mind definitively judges by mere conception of the two extremes, whether theoretical or intuitive, and they vary in clarity and obscurity. The self-evidence of the intuitive is like the knowledge of knowledge itself, and that is the truth. The innate propositions (fitriyyat) are those that need a middle term that is not absent from the mind, and they are called propositions whose syllogisms are with them. The sense-based observations (mushahadat) are either through an external sense, and they are the sensibles (hissiyyat), or through an internal sense, and they are the introspective (wijdaniyyat), and among them are the estimative (wahmiyyat) about sensibles, and among them are what we find in ourselves, not through our instruments. The truth is that the sense only yields a particular judgment, and those who deny its usefulness are deaf and blind. The intuitive (hadsivat) are the sudden occurrence of ordered principles, and observation is not required, let alone its repetition, as it is said; for intellectual demands may be intuitive. The experiential (tajribiyyat) require repetition of an action until certainty obtains. Some have disputed whether they are among the certainties, like the intuitives. The recurrent (mutawatirat) are the report of a group that the mind deems impossible to conspire on falsehood. Determining a specific number is not a condition; rather the criterion is a quantity that yields certainty. Yes, it must trace back to sense and the equality of the extreme to the middle. These last three are not valid as proof against others except after participation. And some restricted the sections to the self-evidents and the observations, and that has some plausibility.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a classification of propositions that yield certainty (yaqiniyyat). The author begins by stating that propositions which the mind judges definitively by mere conception of the subject and predicate are called self-evident (badihiyyat) or theoretical (nazariyyat), varying in clarity. The self-evident is like the knowledge of knowledge itself—an irrefutable example. Then follow the innate propositions (fitriyyat), which require a middle term that never leaves the mind (i.e., always implicitly present), hence called 'propositions whose syllogisms are with them'. Next are observations: external sense yields sensibles (hissiyyat), internal sense yields introspectives (wijdaniyyat), including estimative propositions (wahmiyyat) about sensory matters. A crucial note: sense only gives particular judgments, and deniers are mistaken. Then intuitives (hadsivat): sudden grasp of ordered principles without needing repeated observation. Then experiential (tajribiyyat): require repeated action (like experiments) to produce certainty, though some dispute their certainty status. Then recurrent reports (mutawatirat): reports from a group impossible to collude on falsehood; no fixed number, just sufficient to yield certainty; must trace back to sense and have equality of subject and predicate. The author notes that these last three (hadsivat, tajribiyyat, mutawatirat) cannot be used as proof against others without shared participation. Finally, some scholars limit the sections to self-evidents and observations, which has some justification.

B) Hadsiyyat

Key Takeaway
The six types of certain propositions form a spectrum of epistemic sources: self-evidence, innate reason, sense, intuition, experiment, and recurrent testimony.

Types of Opposition in Islamic Logic: Applying the Conditions
The author explains the division of knowledge and the kind of opposition between necessary and acquired knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which type of opposition applies between two terms by applying the conditions of each type as explained by the author.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires that every subject have one or the other; contrariety allows both to be absent.

In this passage, the author examines the four types of opposition (taqabul) and identifies which one holds between necessary and acquired knowledge. He systematically eliminates contradiction and correlation, showing that only contrariety or privation/possession are possible. This lesson will walk you through his reasoning so you can apply the same analysis to other pairs of opposites in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of opposition
The author lists four types: contradiction (taqābul al-ījāb wa al-salb), correlation (taḍāyuf), contrariety (taḍādd), and privation/possession (al-ʿadam wa al-mulka). Note their definitions and conditions.
Contradiction: one must be true; correlation: mutual interdependence; contrariety: can both be absent; privation/possession: subject naturally suited for possession.
2
Apply the condition of contradiction
Contradiction requires that no subject is free from both opposites. The author says concrete existents (e.g., presential knowledge) are neither necessary nor acquired, so both can be absent. Therefore, the opposition is not contradiction.
3
Apply the condition of correlation
Correlation (taḍāyuf) requires mutual relation (e.g., father-son). Necessary and acquired knowledge do not imply each other in this way. The author dismisses it as 'obvious'.
4
Choose between contrariety and privation/possession
Both require the possibility of the subject of one opposite being characterized by the other. But for necessary knowledge (presential, eternal), this is impossible. Hence, the division cannot apply universally; it must be restricted.
This step leads to the next lesson on the scope of the division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليعلم أن الكسبية التوقف على النظر، والبديهة تقابلها، وليس بينهما التقابل بين تقابل الإيجاب والسلب؛ لأن المتقابلين بالإيجاب والسلب لا يخلو موضوع ما عنهما، وإلا لزم ارتفاع النقيضين، والموجودات العينية [كلها] خالية عنهما. ولا التضايف، وهو ظاهر، بل إنما يتصور التضاد أو العدم والملكة. ولا بد في التضاد من إمكان تعاقبها على موضوع واحد، فكل ما يتصف بأحد الضدين أمكن اتصافه بالآخر، ولا بد في المتقابلين بالعدم والملكة من إمكان اتصاف موضوع العدم بالملكة، وعلى التقديرين فلا بد من إمكان اتصاف الضروري بالكسبية، ومن البين أن الحضوري يمتنع اتصافه بالكسبية، وكذا القديم [من العلم]؛ إذا لو أمكن كونه كسبيا لأمكن حصوله بعد الحركة الفكرية، فلا يكون قديما، هذا خلف، فإذن لا بد من تخصيص المقسم بالتصور والتصديق الحادثين.
And let it be known that acquired [knowledge] depends on reflection, and the necessary [knowledge] is its opposite. And between them there is not the opposition of affirmation and negation; because the two opposites by affirmation and negation, no subject is free from both, otherwise both contradictories would be negated, and all concrete existents are devoid of both. Nor [is it] the opposition of correlation, and that is clear. Rather, only contrariety or privation and possession are conceivable. And in contrariety, there must be the possibility of their succeeding each other upon one subject, so everything that is characterized by one of the two contraries can possibly be characterized by the other. And in the two opposites by privation and possession, there must be the possibility of the subject of privation being characterized by possession. And on both suppositions, there must be the possibility of the necessary [knowledge] being characterized by acquiredness. And it is obvious that presential knowledge is impossible to be characterized by acquiredness, and likewise eternal knowledge; for if it were possible for it to be acquired, it would be possible for it to occur after intellectual motion, so it would not be eternal; this is a contradiction. So then, it is necessary to restrict the division to the contingent [types of] conceptual and assent knowledge.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage clarifies the type of opposition between necessary (badihi) and acquired (kashsbi) knowledge. It begins by stating that acquired knowledge depends on reflection (nazar), while necessary knowledge is its opposite. The author then examines the four types of opposition (taqabul): contradiction (tanāquḍ), correlation (taḍāyuf), contrariety (taḍādd), and privation/possession (ʿadam wa mulk). He argues that the opposition here is not contradiction, because in contradiction, a subject cannot be free of both opposites (e.g., true/false). But there are existents that are neither necessary nor acquired (e.g., presential knowledge? Actually, the text says 'concrete existents are devoid of both'—meaning some existents are not characterized by either necessary or acquired knowledge, so it cannot be contradiction). It is also not correlation, because correlation requires mutuality (e.g., father-son). So it must be either contrariety or privation/possession. In contrariety, both opposites can succeed each other on the same subject (e.g., black/white). In privation/possession, the subject is naturally suited for the possession but currently lacks it (e.g., blindness/sight). Both require the possibility of the subject of one opposite being characterized by the other. However, for necessary knowledge (like presential or eternal knowledge), it is impossible to be acquired. Therefore, the division cannot apply to all knowledge; it must be restricted to contingent (ḥādith) conceptual and assent knowledge. This nuance prevents a false universal classification.

Key Terms
تقابل الإيجاب والسلب
التضاد
العدم والملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the opposition between necessary and acquired knowledge not a contradiction (taqābul al-ījāb wa al-salb)?
A) Because some subjects can have both necessary and acquired knowledge simultaneously.
B) Because some subjects (like presential knowledge) are neither necessary nor acquired.
C) Because necessary knowledge is always affirmative and acquired is negative.
D) Because the two are mutually interdependent.

Key Takeaway
The type of opposition between two terms determines their logical relationship; not all opposites are contradictories, and conditions must be checked carefully.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the terms 'wajib' (obligatory) and 'haram' (forbidden) in Islamic jurisprudence. Using the conditions from the passage, determine which type of opposition best applies. Explain your reasoning.
?
In the context of knowledge, consider 'ilm Allah' (divine knowledge) and 'ilm al-insan' (human knowledge). Can they be classified under the same opposition as necessary and acquired? Why or why not?

The Scope of Knowledge Division: Why Presential and Eternal Knowledge Are Excluded
The author explains the division of knowledge and the kind of opposition between necessary and acquired knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of knowledge fall under the division of necessary and acquired and explain why presential and eternal knowledge are excluded.

Core Idea
Presential and eternal knowledge cannot be acquired, so they are outside the classification of necessary vs. acquired.

After establishing that the opposition between necessary and acquired knowledge is either contrariety or privation/possession, the author realizes that these require the possibility of one opposite becoming the other. However, presential knowledge (knowledge by presence) and eternal knowledge (like divine knowledge) cannot become acquired. This forces a restriction: the division only applies to contingent conceptual and assent knowledge. This lesson will help you understand this crucial epistemological boundary.

Guided Path
1
Recall the condition for contrariety and privation/possession
Both types require that the subject of one opposite can potentially be characterized by the other. For contrariety, the two opposites can succeed each other on the same subject (e.g., black and white on a wall). For privation/possession, the subject of privation is naturally suited for possession (e.g., a blind person can potentially see).
2
Apply the condition to necessary knowledge
The author asks: can necessary knowledge (presential or eternal) be characterized by acquiredness? He argues that presential knowledge (like God's knowledge of creation) is immediate and cannot be result of reflection. Eternal knowledge is uncreated and cannot occur after intellectual motion.
Presential knowledge is knowledge by presence, not mediated by concepts or inference.
3
Analyze the conditional argument for eternal knowledge
The author uses a conditional: if eternal knowledge could be acquired, then it would occur after intellectual motion. But if it occurs after intellectual motion, it would not be eternal (since it would be temporally originated). That contradicts its eternality. Therefore, eternal knowledge cannot be acquired. This is a valid modus tollens argument.
Formally: If P (acquired) then Q (occurs after motion). If Q then R (not eternal). But we have not R (it is eternal). Therefore not P.
4
Conclude the restriction
Since presential and eternal knowledge cannot be acquired, they cannot be part of the division that includes acquired knowledge. Therefore, the division (necessary vs acquired) only applies to contingent (hadith) conceptual and assent knowledge. This prevents an overgeneralization.

The Requests 'Ayy and Hal: Seeking Distinguishers and Assent
Definitions of 'ayy and the two types of 'hal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the request 'ayy (seeking a distinguisher) from the two types of 'hal (simple and composite, seeking assent to existence) and identify what each requests.

Core Idea
'Ayy asks 'which' for a distinguisher; 'hal asks 'whether' for existence.

In logic, every inquiry begins with a request (talab). The first two fundamental requests are 'ayy and 'hal. 'Ayy seeks a distinguishing attribute, answered by a differentia or property. 'Hal seeks assent to existence, either simple (existence itself) or composite (existence with a description). Understanding these prevents confusion in asking and answering questions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of 'ayy
Find the definition of 'ayy: it is for seeking the distinguisher (mumayyiz) that is not said in answer to 'what is it'. Note that it asks for an attribute that distinguishes the thing from others.
2
Note the two types of answer for 'ayy
The answer can be essential (a differentia, proximate or remote) or accidental (a real or relational property). Recognize the conditions for each.
3
Distinguish simple and composite 'hal
Simple 'hal seeks assent to the existence of the thing in itself ('exists?'). Composite 'hal seeks assent to its existence with a description ('exists as such?').
4
Apply the distinction to a concrete example
Think of a question like 'What distinguishes salt from sugar?' (this is 'ayy requiring a differentia). Then ask 'Does salt exist?' (simple 'hal). Then ask 'Is salt soluble?' (composite 'hal).

وَأَيُّ لِطِلِبِ الْمُمَيَّزِ الغير المقول في جواب ما هو في عرف هذه الصناعة، وإن كان في اللغة لمطلق المميز إِمَّا بِالذَّاتِيَّاتِ، فيجاب بالفصل القريب، إن قصد التميز عن المشاركات في الجنس القريب، أو الفصل البعيد إن أريد التميز عن المشاركات في الجنس البعيد، أَوِ الْعَوَارِض، فيجاب بالخاصة الحقيقية أو الإضافية. وَ هَلْ لِطَلَبِ التَّصْدِيقِ بِوُجُودِ الشَّيْءِ فِي نَفْسِهِ، فَتُسَمَّى بَسِيطَةَ، أَوْ بِوُجُودِهِ عَلَى صِفَةٍ فَمُرَكَّبَةٌ، ووهِم صاحب «الأفق المبين» أن ههنا قسما ثالثا، وسماه أبسط وقال به يطلب التصديق بتقرر الماهية المتقدمة على الوجود، وهو مقدم على البسيطة، واعترض عليه بعض الأجلة: هل هذه إما طالبة للتصديق بقوام الشيء، فذلك ممتنع أو غير مفيد، أو لتصوره، فمندرجة في الشارحة. وما قيل: إن مرتبة التقرر مقدمة على مرتبة الوجود، وربما تكون مجهولة، فلا بد أن يعد مطلبا برأسه فسخيف؛ لأن مرتبة التقرر كناية عن نفس الماهية، والتصديق به ممتنع أو غير مفيد والتصور داخل في مطلب «ما». ثم التحقيق أنه قد سلف منا إشارة في أوائل الكتاب، وبرهنا في بعض الرسائل أن مصداق حمل الوجود هو نفس الماهية، فالماهية موجودة حكاية عن نفس تقررها في الواقع، فالتصديق بالتقرر هو بعينه تصديق بالوجود، فعلى هذا ما زعمه قسما ثالثا مندرج في هل البسيط، وتصور مرتبة التقرر من الحيثية مطلب ما الحقيقية وبالجملة ذلك وهم وجزاف محض، ينتظم في سلك سائر الجزافات الصادرة منه، اللهم إلا أن يقال: ربما يكون الحمل الأولى نظريا، فيجوز طلبه بـ هل البسيط، والله أعلم بالصواب. هذه وَ لِمَ لِطَلَبِ الدَّلِيلِ لِمُجَرَّدِ التَّصْدِيقِ، فيجاب بالبرهان مطلقا، إنيا أو لميا، أَوْ لطلب دليل لِلْأَمْر، أي العلة لثبوت المحمول للموضوع بِحَسَبِ
And (any) is for seeking the distinguisher that is not said in answer to 'what is it' in the convention of this art, although in language it is for an absolute distinguisher: either by essential attributes, so it is answered with the proximate differentia if one intends distinction from partners in the proximate genus, or the remote differentia if one intends distinction from partners in the remote genus; or by accidents, so it is answered with the real or relational property. And (hal) is for seeking assent to the existence of the thing in itself, so it is called simple; or to its existence upon a description, so it is composite. And the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' mistakenly thought that here there is a third division, which he called 'absat' and said that by it one seeks assent to the determination (taqarrur) of the quiddity prior to existence, and it is prior to the simple. Some great ones objected to him: this is either seeking assent to the constitution of the thing, which is impossible or useless, or to its conceptualization, so it is subsumed under the explicative (shariha). And what was said: that the rank of determination is prior to the rank of existence, and perhaps it may be unknown, so it must be considered an independent request, is foolish; because the rank of determination is an allusion to the quiddity itself, and assent to it is impossible or useless, and conceptualization falls under the request of 'ma'. Then the verification is that there has preceded from us an indication at the beginning of the book, and we proved in some epistles that the referent of the predication of existence is the quiddity itself, so the quiddity exists as a report of its own determination in reality. Thus assent to determination is identical to assent to existence. On this basis, what he claimed as a third division is subsumed under the simple hal. And conceptualization of the rank of determination from a certain perspective is a request of the real 'ma'. In short, that is a delusion and pure nonsense, fitting into the series of other nonsense emanating from him, unless it is said: perhaps the primary predication may be theoretical, so it is permissible to seek it by the simple hal. And Allah knows best the truth. These (and limā) is for seeking the proof for mere assent, so it is answered with demonstration absolutely, whether it is 'since' or 'why'; or for seeking proof for the matter, i.e., the cause for the affirmation of the predicate to the subject, according to...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn concerning the three types of requests (matālib) in logic: 'ayy', 'hal', and 'limā'. The author explains each type and then engages in a critical refutation of a proposed third division of 'hal'. The passage begins by defining the request 'ayy' (any): it seeks the distinguishing attribute that is not said in answer to 'what is it' (mā huwa). In the convention of logic, it asks for a distinctive characteristic, either essential (by differentia) or accidental (by property). Then it defines 'hal' as the request for assent (tasdiq) to the existence of a thing either in itself (simple hal) or with a description (composite hal). The author then critiques a mistaken claim by the author of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin' who posited a third division called 'absat', which seeks assent to the determination (taqarrur) of the quiddity prior to existence. The refutation proceeds as follows: the objection argues that this either seeks assent to the constitution of the thing (impossible or useless) or to its conceptualization (already covered by the request 'ma'). The claim that the rank of determination is prior and may be unknown is dismissed as foolish, because determination is just the quiddity itself, and assent to it is either impossible or subsumed under conceptualization. The author then provides his own verification: he earlier proved that existence and quiddity are identical in referent, so assent to determination is identical to assent to existence, thus the 'absat' is already included in the simple 'hal'. The passage concludes by noting that the request 'limā' is for seeking the proof (demonstration) for assent, either as a 'why' (limī) or a 'since' (innī) proof, or for seeking the cause of the predication. Throughout, the author adheres to the logical tradition, using precise terminology and dialectical reasoning.

Key Terms
أي
هل بسيطة
هل مركبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the composite 'hal request?
A) The distinguishing attribute not said in answer to 'what is it'
B) Assent to the existence of the thing in itself
C) Assent to the existence of the thing with a description
D) The proof for mere assent

Key Takeaway
'Ayy requests a distinguisher; simple 'hal requests assent to bare existence; composite 'hal requests assent to existence with an attribute.

Refuting the Third Division of Hal and Defining the Request 'Lima
Critique of the 'absat' division and definition of 'lima

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's refutation of the proposed third division of hal ('absat') and define the request 'lima as seeking proof.

Core Idea
The so-called 'absat' request reduces to either simple hal or conceptualization; no third division exists.

After defining the two types of 'hal, the author addresses a mistaken claim by the author of al-Ufuq al-Mubin, who proposed a third division called 'absat' seeking assent to the determination (taqarrur) of the quiddity prior to existence. The author refutes this by showing it collapses into either the simple hal or the request for conceptualization (ma). He then defines 'lima as the request for proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed third division
Find where the author introduces the claim by the author of al-Ufuq al-Mubin: there is a third division 'absat' that seeks assent to the determination (taqarrur) of the quiddity prior to existence.
2
Follow the objection against it
Notice the objection: either this seeks assent to the constitution (qiwam) of the thing (impossible or useless) or to its conceptualization (already covered by the request 'ma').
3
Understand the author's own verification
The author argues that the referent of existence is the quiddity itself, so assent to determination is identical to assent to existence. Thus the 'absat' is subsumed under simple hal.
4
Define the request 'lima
After the critique, the author defines 'lima as seeking proof (dalil) for mere assent, answered by demonstration (burhan) whether inni (that) or limi (why).

The Three Types of Verbal Signification
Author defines the three types of conventional verbal signification: mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam, and identify which type of signification is at work in a given example.

Core Idea
Every word signifies in three ways: the whole, a part, or a necessary accompaniment.

This passage lays the foundation for analyzing how words convey meaning in Islamic logic. The threefold classification of conventional verbal signification is crucial for defining terms and evaluating arguments across all Islamic sciences. We will examine each type and understand their unity for simple expressions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Locate the Arabic terms for the three types: مطابقة (mutabaqa), تضمن (tadammun), and التزام (iltizam). Notice that each is defined by what the word signifies: the entirety, a part, or a necessary concomitant.
2
Understand the basis of convention
All three are instances of conventional signification (دلالة وضعية). They depend on the original act of 'putting' (وضع) the word for a meaning.
3
Analyze the unity for simple words
The author states that for a simple word (لفظ مفرد), the three are united in essence. That is, the word signifies one whole meaning, and that same meaning, when analyzed into parts, yields tadammun. For example, 'man' signifies the whole 'rational animal' by mutabaqa, and by tadammun it signifies 'animal' as a part.
This shows that mutabaqa and tadammun are not separate significations but two perspectives on the same signification.
4
Apply to examples
Take a concrete word like 'roof' (سقف). By mutabaqa it signifies the whole roof; by tadammun it might signify a part like 'covering'; by iltizam it signifies 'wall' because a roof necessarily implies a wall. Practice identifying each type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الألفاظ. فدلالة اللفظ على تمام ما وضع له مطابقة، وعلى جزئه تضمن، وعلى ما يلزم عن معناه التزام. وهي المركبات، بل هما متحدان بالذات؛ فإن اللفظ المفرد يدل على أمر واحد، فذلك الأمر من حيث إنه منحل إلى الأجزاء تضمن.
Chapter on Words. The signification of a word for the entirety of what it is placed for is correspondence; for a part of it is inclusion; and for that which necessarily follows from its meaning is implication. And they are in compounds, but they are united in essence; for the simple word signifies one thing, and that thing, insofar as it is analyzable into parts, is inclusion.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage defines the three conventional types of verbal signification (dalaala wad'iyya). The author first states that signification of a word for its entire meaning is called 'mutabaqa' (correspondence), for a part of its meaning is 'tadammun' (inclusion), and for something necessary that follows from its meaning is 'iltizam' (implication). The passage then clarifies that these are not separate for simple words; for a simple word signifies a single meaning, and that meaning, when analyzed into its conceptual parts (e.g., 'animal' from 'man'), involves tadammun. This classification is fundamental in logic and usul al-fiqh for analyzing how words convey meaning.

Key Terms
دلالة
مطابقة
تضمن
التزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Iltizam
D) None

Key Takeaway
The three types of verbal signification — mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam — are the basic lens for analyzing word meaning in Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh.

Refutation of the Claim That Implication Is Abandoned in the Sciences
Author refutes the claim that implicative signification is abandoned in the sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the dialectical argument that implicative signification (iltizam) is not purely rational but is based on convention (wad').

Core Idea
Implication is conventional, not purely rational, so it is valid in the sciences.

In this passage, the author addresses a claim that implicative signification (iltizam) is abandoned in the sciences because it is purely rational and thus unreliable. He presents an objection based on inclusion (tadamun) and then provides a resolution: implication is actually a type of conventional signification (wad'i), so it is valid in scientific discourse. This lesson will guide you through the dialectical steps of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
Find the position that is being attacked: 'Implication is abandoned in the sciences, not in Arabic discourse, because it is rational.' This is the mudda'i (claim).
2
Analyse the objection
Extract the objection (naqd): the same would apply to inclusion (tadamun) because it is also rational. This creates a reductio ad absurdum: if implication is abandoned, inclusion should also be abandoned, but it is not.
The objection exposes inconsistency in the original claim.
3
Understand the reductio
See how the objection leads to an unwelcome conclusion: either both are abandoned (but they aren't) or the original premise is false. The objection forces a choice.
4
Examine the solution
The author denies that implication is purely rational; instead it is an instance of conventional (wad'i) signification. The positor (wadi') includes the implied meaning by convention.
This resolves the inconsistency: implication is not purely rational, so the objection's basis collapses.
5
Assess the implications
Conclude that implication is valid in all sciences and discourses, as it is established by convention, not reason alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَدْ قِيلَ: الالْتِزَامُ مَهْجُورٌ في الْعُلُوم، لا في المحاورات العربية؛ لأَنَّهُ عَقْلِيٌّ، ولا اعتبار لها، وَنُقِضَ بِالتَّضَمُّن؛ لأنه عقلي أيضا، وبأن في العلوم العقلي غير معتبر في المحاورات أيضا فيلزم أن يكون فيها مهجورا أيضا. والحل: أن كونه عقليا ممنوع، بل من أقسام الوضعية، كيف ولوضع الواضع دخل فيه.
And it was said: Implication is abandoned in the sciences, not in Arabic discourse, because it is rational, and it is not considered [important]. And it was objected by [citing] inclusion, because it [inclusion] is also rational, and [also by the argument that] in the rational sciences it [inclusion] is not considered important in discourse either, and consequently it would be abandoned there as well. The solution: that it is rational is denied; rather, it is one of the types of positional signification; indeed, by the position of the positor it is included.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. A claim is reported: 'Implication (iltizam) is abandoned (mahjur) in the sciences (al-ulum) but not in Arabic discourse, because it is rational (aqli), and no weight is given to it.' The author then presents an objection (naqd): the same would apply to inclusion (tadamun), because inclusion is also rational. Moreover, in the rational sciences, inclusion is not considered valid in discourse either, so it would also be abandoned. This reductio shows the inconsistency of the original claim. The solution (hall) is to deny that implication is purely rational (aqli muthlaq). Instead, it belongs to the categories of positional (wad'iyyah) signification, because it is included by the position of the positor (wad' al-wadi'). In other words, the connection in implication is established by convention (wad'), not pure reason. Therefore, implication is valid in the sciences just as inclusion is.

Key Terms
الالتزام
عقلي
وضعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the core of the solution (al-hall) to the objection that implication is abandoned in the sciences?
A) Implication is purely rational and thus invalid.
B) Implication is abandoned in Arabic discourse too.
C) Implication is not purely rational but includes conventional position.
D) Inclusion is also abandoned.

Key Takeaway
Implication (iltizam) is not abandoned in the sciences because it is a form of conventional signification, not purely rational, and is established by the positor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Signs of Haqiqa and Majaz
Author defines signs of literal and metaphorical meaning with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the signs of literal and metaphorical meaning as defined in this passage and apply them to examples from the Qur'an and fiqh.

Core Idea
Literal meaning is known by immediate understanding without context; metaphor by impossibility or partial usage.

In Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh, words can be used literally or metaphorically. How do we tell which is which? This passage gives the classical signs. Mastering these signs will sharpen your ability to interpret Islamic texts accurately, especially when a word's context might shift its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two signs of haqiqa
The author says the sign of literal meaning is immediate understanding (tabadur) and absence of a diverting contextual indicator (qarina). This means if you hear a word alone and the first meaning that comes to mind is its usual meaning, and there is no clue forcing another interpretation, it is literal.
Tabadur is the key test; absence of qarina is equally necessary.
2
Identify the two signs of majaz
The author lists two signs for metaphorical meaning: (1) if taken literally, the statement becomes impossible (e.g., 'the donkey that speaks'), (2) the word is used for only part of its original meaning (e.g., 'dabba' meaning beast used for donkey).
These signs help detect when a word is used metaphorically.
3
Apply the signs to an example
Take the word 'dabba' (beast). Its literal meaning is any four-legged animal. Using it specifically for a donkey is metaphorical because it applies to a part (donkey) of the whole category (beasts). This matches the second sign of majaz: using the word for part of its meaning.
The example comes directly from the passage.
4
Practice with a Qur'anic example
Consider the word 'miryah' (dispute) in Surah Al-Kahf, where Allah says 'And do not dispute about them except with a clear dispute.' Literally it means 'dispute'. But in context, it means 'discussion'. By the sign of immediate understanding, if no contextual indicator shifts it, it is literal. However, if a qarina (e.g., the command to be gentle) points to a softer meaning, it might be metaphorical.
This step trains you to use the signs on real Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَعَلَامَةُ الْحَقِيقَةِ التَّبَادُرُ (۱) ، أي تبادر معناه المستعمل فيه، وَالْعَرَاء عن الْقَرِينَةِ الصارفة ، وَعَلَامَةُ الْمَجَازِ الْإِطْلَاقُ عَلَى الْمُسْتَحِيلِ، وَاسْتِعْمَالُ اللُّفْظ بَعْض الْمَعْنَى (٢) ، كَالدَّابَّةِ عَلَى الْحِمَارِ ، والتفصيل في كتب الأصول.
And the sign of literal meaning is immediate understanding (1), meaning the immediate understanding of its meaning as used, and the absence of a contextual indicator that diverts. And the sign of metaphorical meaning is application to what is impossible [if taken literally], and using the word for part of the meaning (2), like 'dabba' (beast) for the donkey. The details are in the books of usul.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the signs for distinguishing literal (haqiqa) and metaphorical (majaz) usage. The author states that the primary sign of literal meaning is 'tabadur' — the immediate understanding of the word's meaning when heard without any contextual clue. This is coupled with the absence of a 'qarina sarifa' (a contextual indicator that would divert from the literal meaning). For metaphor, the sign is that the literal interpretation is impossible, or that the word is used for only part of its original meaning. The example given is the word 'dabba' (beast) used specifically for a donkey — a common metaphorical extension. The text notes that full details are found in the works of usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
الحقيقة
المجاز
التبادر
القرينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a sign of literal meaning?
A) Applying the word to something impossible
B) Using the word for part of its meaning
C) Immediate understanding without a contextual indicator
D) The presence of a qarina

Key Takeaway
Literal meaning is recognized by immediate understanding and no contextual diversion; metaphorical meaning by impossibility or partial usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
