Preference Between Naql, Majaz, and Ishtirak
Author establishes preference rules for ambiguous words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the preference rules for naql, majaz, and ishtirak to resolve lexical ambiguity in a Qur'anic or hadith text.

Core Idea
The rarer interpretation is preferred: metaphor is rarest, then transfer, then homonymy.

In usul al-fiqh, legal rulings often hinge on the precise meaning of a word. When a word could be a transferred term (naql), a metaphor (majaz), or a homonym (ishtirak), scholars have established a hierarchy of preference to avoid arbitrary choice. This passage from a classic text gives the rule: the less common interpretation is preferred. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for correct legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three competing interpretations
Recall the definitions: naql is a word moved from its original meaning to a new meaning by common usage (e.g., salat from supplication to prayer); majaz is non-literal usage with a relation (e.g., 'lion' for a brave man); ishtirak is one word with two unrelated meanings (e.g., 'ayn for eye and spring). List them and note their characteristics.
2
Recognize the preference order
The passage states: naql and majaz are both preferred over ishtirak. Additionally, majaz is preferred over naql. So the full order from most preferred to least is: majaz > naql > ishtirak.
The reason given is rarity (qilla): the less common occurrence is more likely intended.
3
Apply the rule to a concrete example
Consider the word 'salat' in the Qur'an. It originally meant 'supplication' but was transferred (naql) to 'ritual prayer'. If a context could allow either meaning (ishtirak between original and transferred), the rule prefers naql over ishtirak, so we take it as prayer. Now consider a word like 'ayn' which could be a homonym (eye/spring) or a metaphor (e.g., 'eye of the needle'). According to the rule, metaphor is preferred over homonymy, so if a metaphorical reading is possible, we prefer it.
These rules are applied in usul al-fiqh to resolve ambiguity in legal texts.
4
Understand the rationale
The basis for the preference is empirical rarity (qilla). Homonymy is considered rarer than naql and majaz, so it is least preferred. Among naql and majaz, majaz is rarer, so it is preferred. This avoids overusing homonymy, which would multiply meanings arbitrarily.
The rule is probabilistic, not absolute; exceptions may exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النَّقْلُ وَالْمَجَازُ أَوْلَى مِنَ الاِشْتِرَاكِ (۳) ؛ لقلته بالنسبة إليهما، فإذا دار على غيره عند اللفظ بين النقل والاشتراك فالنقل أولى ، وكذا بين الاشتراك والمجاز ، فيحمل على المجازية ، وَالْمَجَازُ أَوْلَى مِنَ النَّقْل ؛ لقلته بالنسبة إلى المجاز، فإذا دار اللفظ بينهما ثبت المجازية ، وكذا إذا دار بينهما وبين الاشتراك .
Transfer and metaphor are preferable to homonymy (3) because of their rarity relative to the two. So if the word is ambiguous between transfer and homonymy, then transfer is preferable, and similarly between homonymy and metaphor, so it is taken as metaphorical. And metaphor is preferable to transfer, because of its rarity relative to metaphor, so if the word is ambiguous between the two, metaphor is established, and also if it is ambiguous between the two and homonymy.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage provides rules for preferring one interpretation over another when a word has multiple possible meanings: transfer (naql: a word moved from its original to a new meaning), metaphor (majaz: non-literal usage with a relation), and homonymy (ishtirak: one word with two unrelated meanings). The author states that transfer and metaphor are both preferred over homonymy because homonymy is rarer. Thus, if a word could be either a transfer or a homonymy, we take it as transfer; if between homonymy and metaphor, we take it as metaphor. Additionally, metaphor is preferred over transfer because metaphor is rarer than transfer. So if a word could be either metaphor or transfer, we take it as metaphor. These rules are applied in usul al-fiqh to resolve ambiguities in legal texts.

Key Terms
النقل
الاشتراك
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which is preferred when a word is ambiguous between transfer and metaphor?
A) Transfer is preferred
B) Metaphor is preferred
C) Homonymy is preferred
D) Both are equally likely

Key Takeaway
The preference hierarchy in lexical ambiguity resolution is: metaphor > transfer > homonymy, based on relative rarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Suppose a word in a hadith could be either a transferred term (naql) or a homonym (ishtirak). According to the passage, which interpretation is preferred? Explain the reasoning using the given rule.
?
Find an example in the Qur'an or hadith where a word can be interpreted as either a metaphor (majaz) or a transferred term (naql). Which interpretation does the preference rule favor? Walk through the hierarchy.

Majaz bi al-Dhat and bi al-Taba': Two Types of Metaphorical Usage
Author explains that primary metaphorical usage occurs in nouns, while verbs and particles are metaphorical by followership.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between majaz bi al-dhat and majaz bi al-taba' and classify any Arabic word into the appropriate category.

Core Idea
Metaphor in Arabic originates in nouns; verbs and particles borrow their metaphorical status.

In this passage, the author lays down a foundational principle for analyzing metaphorical language in Arabic: only nouns can be metaphorical in themselves; verbs, derivatives, and particles are only metaphorical because of their dependence on other metaphorical elements. This classification is critical for understanding how metaphors work in the Qur'an and in classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Read the Arabic and locate the two key terms: 'المجاز بالذات' and 'المجاز بالتبع'. The author immediately establishes that only one type applies to a specific word class.
2
Define majaz bi al-dhat
Focus on the phrase 'في الاسم'. Notice that the author specifies 'what is not an adjective' – this includes common nouns (ism jins) and proper nouns (`alam). The metaphor is in the word itself, regardless of its form.
Example: using 'asad' (lion) for a brave man is majaz bi al-dhat.
3
Define majaz bi al-taba'
Examine the explanation for verbs and derivatives: they follow the metaphorical usage of their verbal noun (mabda') and the time (zaman). For particles, they follow a necessary nominal meaning. Thus, these are not primary metaphors.
Example: if the verb 'daraba' is metaphorical, it is because its infinitive 'darb' is used metaphorically.
4
Understand the inductive proof
The author says 'الدليل الاستقراء' – the proof is induction. This means the classification is based on observing countless instances of Arabic speech. Recognize that this is not a logical deduction but an empirical generalization.
Inductive reasoning in rhetoric validates the rule by extensive examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمَجَازُ بِالذَّاتِ إِنَّمَا هُوَ فِي الاِسْمِ (٤)، أي ما ليس بصفة، سواء كان اسم جنس أو علما. وَأَمَّا الْفِعْلُ وَسائِرُ الْمُشْتَقَاتِ وَالأَدَوَاتِ فإنما يوجد بِالتَّبَعِيَّةِ، أما في الفعل وسائر المشتقات فبتبعية المبدأ والزمان، وفي الأداة بتبعية المعنى الاسمي اللازم لمعناه الذي يعبر عنه به، والدليل الاستقراء.
And metaphor by essence is only in the noun (4), meaning what is not an adjective, whether it is a common noun or a proper noun. As for the verb and all derivatives and particles, it only occurs by followership: as for the verb and all derivatives, by followership of the infinitive and the time; and in the particle, by followership of the nominal meaning necessary for its meaning that is expressed by it. The proof is induction.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage classifies metaphorical usage into two categories: by essence (bi al-dhat) and by followership (bi al-taba'). The author states that only nouns can be metaphorical by essence, where the metaphor is in the word itself regardless of its form. This includes both common nouns (like 'asad' for a brave man) and proper nouns (if used metaphorically, e.g., 'Hatim' for a generous person). Verbs and their derivatives (like 'daraba') and particles are metaphorical only by followership: a verb is metaphorical because its infinitive (mabda') and tense (zaman) are metaphorical; a particle is metaphorical because the nominal meaning it depends on (e.g., 'fi' for containment) is metaphorical. The proof for this classification is inductive, based on observing Arabic usage.

Key Terms
المجاز بالذات
المجاز بالتبع
المبدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following can be metaphorical by essence according to the passage?
A) The verb 'daraba' (he hit)
B) The particle 'fi' (in)
C) The noun 'asad' (lion) used for a brave man
D) The derivative 'daribun' (hitting)

Key Takeaway
Only nouns can be metaphorical in themselves; verbs and particles derive their metaphorical status from the nouns they are connected to.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Existence of Synonymy in Arabic
Author argues that synonyms exist in Arabic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify pairs of Arabic synonyms and explain the author's argument for their existence.

Core Idea
Synonymy is real in Arabic – multiple words can share the same meaning.

This passage tackles a foundational linguistic issue: does Arabic truly have synonyms? The author affirms that synonymy (taraduf) is a real phenomenon, giving a clear definition and a practical reason for its existence. As students of logic and usul, understanding synonymy is critical for interpreting revealed texts accurately, since the presence of synonyms affects how we extract rulings and draw inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Locate the author's main assertion about synonymy. Does he affirm or deny its existence? The passage begins by stating it is 'actual in the speech of the Arabs'.
2
Extract the definition
Find the precise definition the author gives for synonymy. He defines it as 'multiplicity of words with unity of meaning'. This is the genus (multiplicity of words) and differentia (unity of meaning).
3
Understand the rationale
The author provides a reason why synonymy exists: 'multiplicity of means to convey what is in the mind' and 'expansion'. This is a pragmatic justification – multiple words give more expressive tools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَتَكْثُرُ اللَّفْظِ مَعَ اتِّحَادِ الْمَعْنَى مُرَادَفَةً، وَذَانِكَ اللَّفْظَانِ مُتَرَادِفَانِ، وَالتَّرَادُفُ وَمَا ذَلِكَ، أَيْ تَكَثُّرُ اللَّفْظِ مَعَ تَوَحُّدِ الْمَعْنَى وَاقِعٌ فِي كَلاَمِ الْعَرَبِ، خِلاَفًا لِشِرْذِمَةٍ قَلِيلَةٍ، وَذَلِكَ لِتَكَثُّرِ الْوَسَائِلِ إِلَى إِفَادَةِ مَا فِي الضَّمِيرِ، وَالتَّوَسُّعِ فِي...
And the multiplicity of words with unity of meaning is synonymy, and those two words are synonyms. And synonymy and what is that? That is, the multiplicity of words with unity of meaning is actual in the speech of the Arabs, contrary to a small minority. And that is due to the multiplicity of means to convey what is in the mind, and for expansion in...
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage affirms the existence of 'tawatur' or 'taraduf' (synonymy) in the Arabic language. The author defines synonymy as multiple words having one meaning. He states that this is a real phenomenon in Arabic, contrary to a small minority who denied it. The reason given is pragmatic: having multiple words for the same meaning gives more means to express what is in the mind and allows for linguistic expansion (tawassu'). The text appears to cut off, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
التّرادف
مرادفة
مترادفان
تكثر اللفظ
اتحاد المعنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Synonymy is a real and functional feature of Arabic, defined as multiple words sharing one meaning, and its existence is supported by the need for expressive variety.

The Perfect Compound and the Nature of Truth in Propositions
Author divides compound expressions and defines the perfect compound as a statement (khabar/qadiyya) that can be true or false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a perfect compound (qadiyya) and determine whether it can be true or false based on its narration (hikaya).

Core Idea
A proposition is a complete sentence that narrates a state of affairs, making it truth-apt.

In logic, not every sentence is a proposition. The author distinguishes the perfect compound (al-murakkab al-tam) as one that can stand alone and is intended as a narration (hikaya) about some matter—either real or hypothetical. This lesson unpacks that definition and shows how truth and falsity apply to categorical, conditional, and disjunctive propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of compound
The passage states that a compound is either perfect (tam) or incomplete (naqis). The condition for perfection is that silence upon it is correct—i.e., it is a complete sentence. Incomplete compounds like 'the son of' are not truth-apt.
This is the foundational cut: a proposition must be a complete sentence.
2
Grasp the dual terminology: khabar and qadiyya
The perfect compound is called both khabar and qadiyya. The commentary by Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq clarifies that 'qadiyya' properly refers to the meaning (madlul), while 'khabar' can refer to the expression (dal). However, in this text they are used interchangeably for the perfect compound.
This distinction helps avoid confusion between sentence and proposition.
3
Understand the function of narration (hikaya)
The perfect compound is a report about a state of affairs (al-amr al-waqi'). For a categorical proposition, the subject is such that the predicate is affirmed or denied. For conditionals, the antecedent is such that the consequent follows necessarily or accidentally (or not). For disjunctives, one alternative excludes or does not exclude the other.
The narration can be about actual reality or a hypothetical world.
4
Recognize the truth condition
Truth is defined as correspondence (mutabaqa) between the narration and the narrated matter; falsehood is lack of correspondence. These properties are inherent in the concept of a proposition, regardless of specific subject matter.
The passage emphasizes that truth and falsity are considered by the very meaning of the proposition, not by external factors.
5
Apply to a real Islamic science example
Consider the kalam proposition: 'The world is temporally originated.' This is a perfect compound (qadiyya). Its truth depends on whether the world actually is temporally originated, matching the author's definition of truth as correspondence.
This example shows how the definition works in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مبحث: تقسم المركب. والمركب إن صح السكوت عليه فتام، خبر وقضية. قال السيد المحقق قدس سره الشريف: إطلاق القضية على المدلول حقيقة، وعلى الدال مجاز، والأظهر أن إطلاق الخبر بالعكس. إن قصد به (أي بالمركب التام تعريف الخبر) الحكاية عن الأمر الواقع الذي هو المحكي عنه، وهو في الحملية كون الموضوع بحيث ينتزع عنه أو يسلب عنه المحمول، وفي المتصلة كون المقدم بحيث لا يفارقه التالي إن وجد، لزوما أو اتفاقا، أو عدم كونه على هذه الحيثية، وفي المنفصلة كونه بحيث ينافيه أو لا ينافيه. ومن ثم (أي من أجل كونه حكاية) يوصف بالصدق (أي المطابقة للمحكي عنه) والكذب ضرورة (أي عدمها)، ومن ثم يحتملهما بالنظر إلى نفس مفهومه، مع قطع النظر عن خصوص المواد، ثم هما يختلفان باختلاف الحكاية، فإن قصد الحكاية عن الأمر الواقعي يجب تحققه في الواقع، وإن كان عن عالم التقدير فيجب تحققه تقديرا.
Section: Division of the compound. The compound: if silence upon it is correct, then it is perfect (tam), a report (khabar) and a proposition (qadiyya). Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (may his secret be sanctified) said: The application of 'proposition' to the meaning (madlul) is real (haqiqa), and to the indicator (dal) is metaphorical (majaz), and the apparent is that the application of 'khabar' is the opposite. [The perfect compound is called khabar] if it is intended (meaning by the perfect compound, the definition of khabar) to narrate (hikaya) about the actual matter (al-amr al-waqi') which is the thing narrated about (mahki 'anhu). In a categorical (hamliyya) [proposition], this consists in the subject being such that the predicate is either affirmed of it or denied of it. In a conditional (muttasila) [proposition], it consists in the antecedent being such that the consequent is inseparable from it if it exists, necessarily or accidentally, or the absence of this condition. In a disjunctive (munfasila) [proposition], it consists in it being such that it either excludes or does not exclude [the other]. Therefore (i.e., because it is a narration), it is described as true (sidq) (i.e., correspondence to the thing narrated about) and false (kidhb) necessarily (i.e., lack of correspondence). Therefore, it is capable of both [truth and falsehood] by consideration of its own concept, regardless of particular matters. Then they (truth and falsehood) differ according to the narration: if the narration is about an actual matter, it must correspond in reality; if it is about a hypothetical world, it must correspond hypothetically.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage defines the perfect compound (al-murakkab al-tam) in logic. The author states that a compound expression is perfect if one can be silent upon it (i.e., it is a complete sentence). It is called a report (khabar) or proposition (qadiyya). The commentary by Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq clarifies that 'qadiyya' is used strictly for the meaning (the propositional content), while 'khabar' may be used for the linguistic expression. The perfect compound, when intended as a narration (hikaya) about some state of affairs (al-amr al-waqi'), is characterized by its truth or falsity. Truth is defined as correspondence (mutabaqa) to the narrated matter, falsehood as lack thereof. The passage then explains how this works for categorical, conditional, and disjunctive propositions. Finally, it notes that truth and falsity are inherent in the concept of a proposition, but their actual value depends on whether the narration is about actual reality or a hypothetical one.

Key Terms
المركب التام (al-murakkab al-tam)
الخبر (al-khabar)
القضية (al-qadiyya)
الصدق (al-sidq)
الكذب (al-kidhb)
الحكاية (al-hikaya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition must a perfect compound fulfill to be considered a report (khabar) or proposition?
A) It must consist of two words.
B) It must be intended as a narration (hikaya) about a real or hypothetical matter.
C) It must be in Arabic.
D) It must be true.

Key Takeaway
The perfect compound is a complete sentence that narrates a state of affairs; its truth is correspondence to that state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following sentence is a perfect compound (qadiyya) according to the passage: 'O Allah, forgive me.' Justify your answer based on the condition of narration (hikaya).
?
Take a categorical proposition from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Explain how its truth or falsity is determined according to the passage's definition of sidq as correspondence.

Resolving Truth-Value Contradictions via Summary and Detail
Author resolves truth-problem by distinguishing summary/detail.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve apparent contradictions in the truth-value of a proposition by applying the distinction between summary and detailed consideration of the relation.

Core Idea
A proposition can be true in one respect and false in another without contradiction.

Sometimes a proposition seems to be both true and false at once, creating an epistemological puzzle. The author of this passage resolves the puzzle by showing that the truth-value depends on how we consider the relation between subject and predicate: summarily (as the thing reported about) or in detail (as the report itself). This distinction, applied in the commentary on al-Dawani, also yields a powerful tool for handling similar problems in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
Locate the apparent contradiction: a proposition with a relation (nisba) seems to be both true and false. In the passage, the relation is considered both as the thing narrated about (mahki 'anha) and as the narration (hikaya) itself.
The problem arises when the same proposition is evaluated without distinguishing between its object and its statement.
2
Understand the Distinction
Recognize the two aspects: summary (ijmal) considers the relation without analysis, as a whole. Detailed (tafsil) considers it as a structured statement where predication (iqa') applies. The passage says the relation is 'considered in summary' as the narrated-about, and 'in detail' as the narration.
Predication requires detailed consideration because it attaches the predicate to the subject in a specific way.
3
Apply the Distinction to Resolve Contradiction
Assign truth-values accordingly: the proposition can be true in detail and false in summary, or vice versa, without contradiction. The author says 'we choose the truth of this proposition in detail and its falsehood in summary, and there is no impossibility.'
Alternatively, if summary cannot bear truth-value (as some hold), then the proposition is simply false in summary.
4
Examine the Gloss and Analogy
Read the gloss critiquing al-Dawani: it points out potential infinite regress or misunderstanding. Then study the analogy: 'Every praise belongs to Allah' – the praise (hamd) is itself a praise, so the narration is the narrated-about. This illustrates the distinction in a concrete Islamic example.
The gloss shows that even logical experts debate these distinctions; the analogy helps cement the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحق أنه بجميع أجزائه مأخوذ في جانب الموضوع، فالنسبة ملحوظة مجملاً، فهي المحكي عنها، ومن حيث تعلق الإيقاع بها ملحوظة تفصيلاً؛ لاستدعاء الإيقاع ذلك، فهي الحكاية، فنختار صدق هذه القضية في التفصيل، وكذبه في الإجمال، ولا استحالة، أو نختار كذبها؛ بناء على أن المجمل لا يتصف بهما، فانحل الإشكال بجميع تقاريره. قال وثبت له المحكي عنه المغاير، فاندفع جواب المحقق الدواني أيضا. في (الحاشية): هذا كما أنه جواب عن السؤال رد لقول المحقق، وفيه نظر ظاهر؛ لأنه إن أراد التوجيه الأول كما يدل عليه قوله من حيث تعلق الإيقاع، فيلزم أن يكون له محكي عنه، حتى يتصور الكذب، وينجر الكلام إليه، حتى يتسلسل. وإن أراد الثاني وإن كان مستبعدا عن العبارة، فمع أن الإجمال عنده متعلق التصديق، فكيف لا يتصف بالصدق والكذب، إن أراد بالإجمال الصورة الواحدانية المنحلة إلى التفصيل، أو الصور المتعددة الملحوظة بلحاظ واحد المنخرطين في سلك المفرد فغير مطابق للسؤال؛ إذ السؤال كان على تقدير كون الإشارة إلى المفصل، والتعبير عنه بمفرد، وإن أراد به الملحوظ بوجه مستقل فهو خبر وقضية، فلا بد من صدقه وكذبه في نفس الأمر، مع لزوم كون الشيء الواحد حكاية ومحكيا عنه، فإذن ظهر أن الحق ما أفاده المحقق الدواني. ونظير ذلك قولنا كل حمد لله تعالى، فإنه حمد من جملة كل حمد لله، فالحكاية نفسها محكي عنها، وقد عرفت جوابه، فَتَأَمَّلْ؛ فإنه جذر أصم، أي شبهة لا تسمع الجواب، وقد سمعت المحقق الدواني أسمع الجواب، هذا، والله أعلم بالصواب.
And the correct view is that it (the judgment) with all its parts is taken on the side of the subject; so the relation is considered in summary, so it is the thing narrated about. And from the aspect of the attachment of the predication to it, it is considered in detail, because the predication requires that. So it is the narration. So we choose the truth of this proposition in detail and its falsehood in summary, and there is no impossibility. Or we choose its falsehood based on the fact that the summary is not characterized by them (truth and falsehood). So the problem is resolved with all its formulations. He said: And there is established for it a different thing narrated about, so the answer of al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani is also repelled. In the gloss: This is just as it is an answer to the question, it is a refutation of the statement of the muhaqqiq, and there is an apparent flaw in it: because if he intends the first explanation, as his saying 'from the aspect of the attachment of the predication' indicates, then it would necessitate that it has a thing narrated about, so that falsehood can be conceived, and the discourse would lead to that, such that it would lead to infinite regress. And if he intends the second explanation, even if it is unlikely from the expression, then since according to him the summary is related to assent (tasdiq), how is it not characterized by truth and falsehood? If he intends by 'summary' the unitary form that resolves into detail, or the multiple forms considered with one consideration that are included in the category of the singular, then it does not correspond to the question; because the question was based on the supposition that the reference is to the detailed and the expression about it is with a singular term. And if he intends by it what is considered as an independent aspect, then it is an informative statement (khabar) and a proposition, so inevitably it must have truth and falsehood in actual fact, along with the necessity of one thing being both narration and narrated about. So it becomes clear that the correct view is what al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani has expressed. And an analogy of that is our saying 'Every praise belongs to Allah the Exalted', for it is a praise from among all praises to Allah, so the narration itself is what is narrated about. And you have known its answer. So reflect; for it is a deaf root, meaning a doubt that does not hear the answer. And al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani has made the answer heard. This, and Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a technical commentary on a problem in logic regarding the truth-conditions of a proposition (qadiyya) when the predicate involves a relation (nisba). The author first states the correct view: the judgment is taken with all its parts on the side of the subject, so the relation is considered in a summary (ijmal) way as the thing narrated about (mahki 'anha), and in detail (tafsil) as the narration (hikaya) itself because predication (iqa') requires it. The resolution: we affirm the proposition is true in detail and false in summary, or false if summary cannot be true/false. Then a marginal note (hashiya) critiques the answer of al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani, arguing it leads to infinite regress or contradiction if he meant one explanation, or if another, it misunderstands the question. Finally, the author gives an analogy: 'Every praise belongs to Allah' — the praise (hamd) is itself a thing praised, so the narration (hikaya) is both narration and narrated about. He calls the problem a 'deaf root' (jadhrm asamm), a stubborn doubt that resists answer, but claims al-Dawani has answered it.

Co-extensiveness and the Derivation of Universals
Refuting the claim that co-extensiveness alone necessitates derivation of shadow.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fundamental existence (المتأصل في الوجود) and shadow existence (الظل) to evaluate whether co-extensiveness between two forms necessitates derivation of a universal.

Core Idea
Co-extensiveness alone does not imply derivation; only the more fundamental existence serves as the source.

This passage presents a critical dialectic on the nature of universals. Some commentators wrongly assumed that if two forms are co-extensive, one must be derived from the other. The author refutes this, establishing an ontological hierarchy: the fundamental existence is the source, and the shadow existence is derived. By working through the objection, response, and verification, you will learn to apply this distinction in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim under attack
Read the first sentence: co-extensiveness (التصادق) is said by some to necessitate derivation (الانتزاع). This is the claim being refuted.
The objector sets up a straw man to reject.
2
Extract the objectors' argument
The objectors argue that the criterion is not union but fundamentality: the more fundamental in existence (المتأصل في الوجود) is the source of derivation. Example: 'human' is not derived from 'writer'.
This establishes an ontological priority principle.
3
Analyze the counter-response and its critique
Some commentators compare the external to imaginary image like 'human' to its individuals. The author criticizes this because a fully determined external cannot be related as a universal without abstraction.
The critique shows that mere analogy fails without considering abstraction.
4
Grasp the author's verification (تحقيق المقام)
The author distinguishes two existential levels: external fundamental (with effects) and sensory shadow (without effects). The universal is the rational shadow that remains after removing mental individuators, applying to many.
This verification refines the concept of derivation and defines the universal.
5
Apply the distinction to Islamic examples
In usul al-fiqh, the effective cause ('illah) must be fundamental in the original case. Co-extensiveness of a property does not validate derivation.
Practice: distinguish fundamental from shadow in rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال بعض محققي شراح هذا الكتاب إن التصادق لا يوجب الانتزاع، ولیس مداره على الاتحاد مطلقا، وكيف يجوز العاقل أن الإنسان منتزع من الكاتب ، بل المتأصل في الوجود يكون منتزعا منه ، وغير المتأصل منتزعا.
وهذا سؤال حق على تقرير المصنف، قال البعض الآخر من الشراح: إن نسبة الصورة الخارجية إلى الصورة الخيالية كنسبة الإنسان إلى أشخاصه، فيكون ظلا منتزعا، كما أنه منتزع وظل ولا يخفى ما فيه؛ فإن المتعين التام لا يمكن تعينه بتعين آخر، حتى يكون نسبتها إليها كنسبة الإنسان إلى أشخاصه، إلا أن يراد بالصورة الخارجية الصورة المجردة تجريدا ما، وهي الهوية التي يمكن وجودها في الحاسة، واكتنافها بالعوارض الخيالية، ووجودها في الخارج واكتنافها بالعوارض الخارجية، كما ذكرنا مشروحا. وتحقيق المقام أن للشيء وجودا خارج المشاعر، وهو يترتب عليه الآثار الخارجية، وهو أصل في الوجود، ووجود فيها لا يترتب عليها تلك الآثار، وهو ظل للأول، ولبعض الموجودات الظلية مناسبة بواحد شخصي، ولذلك يكون إدراكا له ، وهو صورته الحسية، فهي ظل له ، ولبعضها بالكثير، فهو ظل لها، وهو الصورة العقلية، والقسم الأول يسمى جزئيا والثاني كليا، فعلى هذا هما من صفات العلم.
هذا بحسب الجلي من النظر، ثم النظر الدقيق يحكم أن ظل الكثير حقيقة ليس إلا ما يبقى بعد حذف المشخصات الذهنية، موجودا في الذهن؛ إذ هو المنطبق على الكثير، والمنتزع منها المتحد معها، لكن لما لم يكن وجودها إلا في ضمن الشخص الذهني حكم بأنه ظل، فإذن الكلي عبارة عن
Some of the verifiers among the commentators of this book said that co-extensiveness does not necessitate derivation, and its criterion is not union absolutely. How could a rational person accept that 'human' is derived from 'writer'? Rather, the more fundamental in existence is that from which derivation is made, while the less fundamental is that which is derived. And this is a valid objection against the author's formulation. Some other commentators said: The relation of the external image to the imaginary image is like the relation of 'human' to its individuals, so it becomes a derived shadow, just as it is derived and a shadow. But what it contains is not hidden; for the fully determined cannot be determined by another determination such that its relation to it is like the relation of 'human' to its individuals, unless what is meant by the external image is the image abstracted with some abstraction, which is the identity that can exist in the senses and be encompassed by imaginary accidents, and its existence externally encompassed by external accidents, as we have explained in detail. And the verification of the matter is that a thing has an existence outside the senses, upon which external effects depend, and it is fundamental in existence; and an existence in them (the senses) upon which those effects do not depend, and it is a shadow of the first. And some of the shadowy existences have suitability to a particular individual, and therefore it is an apprehension of it, and it is its sensory image, so it is a shadow of it. And some of them have suitability to many, so it is a shadow of them, and it is the rational image. The first division is called particular and the second universal. So on this basis, they are attributes of knowledge. This is according to the apparent consideration. Then the precise consideration judges that the shadow of the many is in reality nothing but what remains after the removal of mental individuators, existing in the mind, for it is what applies to the many, derived from them, united with them. But since its existence is only within the mental individual, it is judged to be a shadow. So the universal is an expression of...
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. Some commentators object to the earlier definition that co-extensiveness (تصادق) between external and mental forms does not automatically necessitate derivation (انتزاع) of one from the other. They argue that the more fundamental existence (المتأصل في الوجود) is the source of derivation, not the less fundamental. For example, the concept 'human' is derived from actual humans, not from 'writer' even if co-extensive in some sense. The author of Bahr Ulum then presents a response from other commentators: they compare the relation of external image to imaginary image to the relation of 'human' to its individuals, implying that the external image is like a universal shadow. However, the author critiques this because a fully determined external individual cannot be related to another determination in that way; instead, we must consider a partially abstracted external image. The author's own verification (تحقيق المقام) distinguishes two types of existence: external fundamental existence (the thing itself) and sensory/shadowy existence (its image). The sensory image of a particular individual is a shadow of that individual, while the rational image (universal) is a shadow of many. Thus particular and universal are attributes of knowledge (صفات العلم). The precise consideration (النظر الدقيق) clarifies that the universal shadow is what remains in the mind after removing individuating mental attributes; it is that which applies to many, derived from them, but its existence is only mental, hence it is a shadow. This refines the earlier definition.

المتأصل في الوجود

Key Takeaway
Co-extensiveness alone does not make one form derived from another; the fundamental existence (المتأصل في الوجود) is the source, and the universal shadow is what remains in the mind after mental individuation.

The Correct Definition of Contradiction (Naqīḍ)
Author criticizes a common definition of contradiction and provides the correct one from al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the correct definition of contradiction (naqīḍ) from the flawed one, and explain why the flawed definition leads to infinite regress.

Core Idea
Contradiction is the act of removal, not the removed thing itself.

In Islamic logic, the precise definition of 'naqīḍ' (contradiction) is essential for correct inference. A common but mistaken definition states that the contradictory of a thing is its removal. The author exposes the flaw in this definition and presents the correct formulation from al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq. Understanding this distinction prevents circular reasoning when dealing with contradictory propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the flawed definition
Find the phrase 'نقيض كل شيء رفعه' (the contradictory of everything is its removal). This is the definition that the author rejects.
2
Understand why it fails
If the contradictory of everything is its removal, then consider 'removal' itself. The contradictory of 'removal' would be 'removal of removal', which leads to an infinite regress. This makes the definition circular and unworkable.
The author says it is 'invalid' (فاسد).
3
Extract the correct definition
The author presents al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq's correct formulation: 'رفع كل شيء نقيضه' (the removal of everything is its contradictory). Here, the operation of removal (rafʿ) is primary, and the contradictory is the negation itself.
This definition avoids infinite regress and aligns with standard logic: the contradictory of P is not-P.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take the proposition 'The world is originated' (الحادث). Its contradictory is not 'the world' itself, but 'The world is not originated' (نفي حدوث العالم). This shows that the contradictory is the removal (negation), not the thing removed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ نَقِيض كُلِّ شَيْءٍ رَفْعُهُ)، وهذا فاسد؛ لما سيحقق من أن نقيض الرفع مرفوعه والصواب في العبارة ما قال السيد المحقق قدس سره: رفع كل شيء نقيضه
Know that the contradictory of everything is its removal; and this is invalid, because it will be verified that the contradictory of removal is its removal? [unclear] And the correct expression is what al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (may his secret be sanctified) said: The removal of everything is its contradictory.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage addresses a crucial point in the theory of contradiction. A common but flawed definition states: 'The contradictory (naqīḍ) of any thing is its removal (raf')'. The author rejects this because it leads to an infinite regress: the contradictory of 'removal' would then be 'removal of removal', which is circular. The correct formulation is the inverse: 'The removal of any thing is its contradictory'. In other words, contradiction is defined as the operation of negating something, not as the thing that is negated. This aligns with standard logic: the contradictory of proposition P is not-P (the removal of its truth). The author credits al-Sayyid al-Muhaqqiq (a famous commentator) for this precision. Understanding this definition is essential for later discussions of contradictory pairs and their logical properties.

Key Terms
نقيض
رفع

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The opposite of that thing
D) The negation of the removal

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a thing is the removal (negation) of that thing, not the thing itself.

Classifying the 'Ma Huwa' Question: Specific vs Shared Essence
Author classifies 'ma huwa' by scope and answer singularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a 'ma huwa' question as targeting a specific or shared essence and determine the correct answer type (species, genus, or complete definition).

Core Idea
The answer to 'what is it?' depends on the essence's specificity and the question's scope.

This lesson unpacks the types of 'ma huwa' (what is it?) questions from classical Islamic logic. Understanding these distinctions is essential for precise definition in Islamic sciences, as the same question can yield different answers depending on the scope of the essence being asked about.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target essence
Determine whether the question is about a specific essence (mukhtassa) that belongs to one species, or a shared essence (mushtaraka) common to multiple species.
2
Check if the question is restricted to one matter or combines multiple
If the question asks about a single thing, note that; if it combines several things, note the combination.
3
Apply the classification rule
If specific and restricted to one matter: answer is species if particular, complete definition if universal. If shared and combines matters: answer is species if agreeing in reality, genus if differing.
4
Practice with an example
Take a sample question like 'ma huwa al-insan?' (what is human?) and walk through the rule: essence specific, answer universal → complete definition.
Use your own examples from fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا مباحث، الأول: أن ما هو سؤال عن تمام الماهية المختصة إن اقتصر فيه على أمر واحد، فيجاب بالنوع إن كان ذلك الأمر جزئيا أو بالحد التام إن كان كليا، وأن ما هو سؤال عن تمام الماهية المشتركة إن جمع في السؤال بين أمور، فيجاب بالنوع إن كانت تلك الأمور متفق الحقيقة، وبالجنس إن كانت مختلف الحقيقة.
And here are discussions. The first: that 'what is it?' is a question about the complete specific essence if the question is restricted to one thing; then the answer is by the species if that thing is particular, or by the complete definition if it is universal; and that 'what is it?' is a question about the complete shared essence if the question combines [multiple] matters; then the answer is by the species if those matters are agreeing in reality, and by the genus if they are differing in reality.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage expands on the question 'ma huwa' (what is it?), showing that not all such questions are answered the same way. The author distinguishes two dimensions: (1) whether the question targets a specific (mukhtassa) essence or a shared (mushtaraka) essence; (2) whether the answer is a single term or multiple. If the question is about a specific essence and restricted to one thing, the answer is the species if that thing is particular (juz'i), i.e., a singular concept, or the complete definition (hadd tam) if the thing is universal (kulli). If the question is about a shared essence and combines multiple things, then if those things are of the same reality, the answer is the species; if they differ in reality, the answer is the genus. This classification is crucial for understanding the scope of definition and genus-species hierarchy.

Key Terms
ما هو
تمام الماهية
مختصة
مشتركة
نوع
حد تام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The species
B) The complete definition
C) The genus
D) The differentia

Key Takeaway
The answer to 'ma huwa' varies by the scope of the essence (specific or shared) and whether the question is singular or composite, determining whether the answer is species, genus, or complete definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Summary and Detailed Judgment
Author defines judgment and divides it into summary and detailed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between summary and detailed judgment and explain why the relation (nisba) is not an independent object of judgment.

Core Idea
Judgment is a bond; summary is immediate disclosure, detailed is the articulated proposition.

In Islamic logic, judgment (hukm) is the cognitive act of affirming or denying something about something. The author splits judgment into two types: a holistic, immediate grasp and a discursive, articulated proposition. Understanding this division clarifies how we move from intuitive knowledge to explicit reasoning. In this lesson, we will dissect the definition of judgment, its two modes, and the subtle role of the relation (nisba).

Guided Path
1
Define judgment as a bond
Find where the author calls judgment a 'bond' ('aqd) composed of subject (mawdu'), predicate (mahmul), and relation (nisba). This three-part structure is the core definition.
The bond is the mental linking of subject and predicate.
2
Distinguish summary and detailed judgment
Summary judgment is an instantaneous, undifferentiated disclosure (like seeing a white wall). Detailed judgment requires multiple explicit parts and is the standard proposition (qadiyya). Identify the example of opening the eyes to see the wall.
Summary does not involve explicit articulation.
3
Analyze the role of the relation (nisba)
Note that the author says the relation enters the object of judgment only incidentally (bi al-tabi'iyya). It is a particle meaning (ma'na harfi) that serves as a mirror for the extremes, not an independent object. Explain why it cannot be the primary object.
The relation is the copula; it points to the state of the terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني الحكم، أي العقد المنعقد من الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة منه إجمالي، وهو انكشاف الاتحاد بين الأمرين دفعة واحدة، كما إذا فتحنا العين ورأينا الجدار الأبيض، فيحصل العقد المنعقد بينهما دفعة، ومنه تفصيلي، وهو العقد المنعقد المنطقي الذي يستدعي صورا متعددة مفصلة، وهو المتعارف بالقضية. والنسبة إنما تدخل في متعلق الحكم والتصديق بالتبعية ; لأنها من المعاني الحرفية التي لا تلاحظ بالاستقلال، إنما هي مرآة لملاحظة حال الطرفين، ومتعلق الحكم يجب أن يكون أمرا مستقلا بالمفهومية. بل إنما يتعلق الحكم حقيقة بمفاد الهيئة التركيبية، وهو الاتحاد مثلا، أي الأمر المجمل المركب من الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة الملحوظ باللحاظ الاستقلالي، أو هذه المفهومات ملاحظة باللحاظ الواحد الاستقلالي، على ما يقتضيه عبارة «الأفق المبين». وإنما حملنا كلامه عليه، وإن كان له
The second division: Judgment, i.e., the bond composed of subject and predicate and relation; from it is summary (ijmali), and it is the disclosure of the union between the two things at once, as when we open the eye and see the white wall, so the bond composed between them occurs at once. And from it is detailed (tafsili), i.e., the logical bond that requires multiple detailed forms, and it is commonly known as the proposition. And the relation only enters into the object of judgment and assent incidentally; because it is among the particle meanings that are not considered independently; rather it is a mirror for considering the state of the two extremes. And the object of judgment must be something independent in concept. Rather, judgment in reality attaches to the meaning of the composite form, which is union for example, i.e., the summary entity composed of subject, predicate, and relation considered with independent consideration, or these concepts are considered by a single independent consideration, as required by the expression of 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin'. And we have interpreted his statement accordingly, although he has
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is definitional and epistemological, introducing the division of judgment (hukm) into two types: summary (ijmali) and detailed (tafsili). The author begins by defining judgment as a bond ('aqd) composed of subject (mawdu'), predicate (mahmul), and relation (nisba). Summary judgment is an immediate, holistic disclosure, exemplified by the instant perception of a white wall upon opening one's eyes. Detailed judgment is the logical bond that requires multiple explicit components, i.e., the standard proposition (qadiyya). The author then discusses the nature of the relation (nisba), explaining that it enters the object of judgment only incidentally (bi al-tabi'iyya), because it is a particle meaning (ma'na harfi) that is not independently considered; it serves as a mirror to reflect the state of the two extremes. The true object of judgment must be something independent in concept. Ultimately, judgment attaches to the meaning of the composite form (hay'a tarkibiyya), which is the union (ittihad) of the components, considered collectively as an independent whole. This interpretation aligns with the work 'al-Ufuq al-Mubin'. The passage guards against misunderstanding the role of the relation, emphasizing that it is not an independent object but a relational aspect.

Key Terms
الحكم الإجمالي
الحكم التفصيلي
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the role of the relation (nisba) in judgment?
A) It is the primary object of judgment.
B) It enters the object of judgment as an independent concept.
C) It enters only incidentally, as a mirror for the state of the two extremes.
D) It is the same as the predicate.

Key Takeaway
Judgment is a bond of subject, predicate, and relation; it is either summary (instantaneous) or detailed (propositional), and the relation is only incidental, not independent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each as summary or detailed judgment: (a) The immediate knowledge of Allah's existence from natural disposition (fitra); (b) The proposition 'The world is created'; (c) The sudden recognition of a friend's face upon seeing them.
?
Explain why the relation 'is' in the proposition 'Zaid is standing' is not an independent object of judgment, using the author's description of it as a particle meaning and a mirror.

Propositions
beginner
5 min
The Three Components of a Proposition
Author states the three necessary components for a complete proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential components of a proposition in any Islamic logical text: subject, predicate, and complete declarative depicting relation.

Core Idea
A proposition is not complete until it has a subject, predicate, and a complete truth-bearing relation.

Every logical argument is built from propositions. But what makes a proposition a proposition? This passage from the matn gives the foundational definition: a proposition is only complete when it contains three specific components. Understanding these components is the first step toward analysing any argument in Islamic logic, from simple theological statements to complex syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components listed
Read the passage carefully. The author explicitly states that a proposition (qadiyya) requires three things: first, the subject (mawdu‘); second, the predicate (mahmul); and third, a relation (nisba) that is complete, declarative, and depicting (tāmma ikhbāriyya ḥākiya).
2
Understand the conditions of the relation
The relation must be 'complete' (tāmma) meaning it forms a full judgment; 'declarative' (ikhbāriyya) meaning it reports a fact, not a question or command; and 'depicting' (ḥākiya) meaning it accurately represents the mind’s judgment about reality.
3
Apply to a simple proposition from Kalam
Take the proposition 'al-‘ālamu ḥādith' (The world is temporally originated). Identify the subject (al-‘ālam), predicate (ḥādith), and the complete declarative depicting relation that says 'is' – i.e., the judgment that origination holds of the world.
This is a classic example from Islamic theology.
4
Distinguish from incomplete relations
Consider a command like 'i‘mal' (Do!) or a question like 'hal al-‘ālamu qadīm?' (Is the world eternal?). These lack a complete declarative depicting relation because they do not assert a truth claim. Contrast them with a full proposition.
This clarifies why the relation must be declarative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الْقَضِيَّةُ)، سواء كانت هَلِيَّات بسيطة أو مركبة (إِنَّمَا تَتِمُّ بِأُمُورٍ ثَلَاثَةِ): أولها الموضوع وثانيها المحمول وثَالِتُهَا نِسْبَةٌ [تَامَّةٌ إِخْبَارِيَّةٌ حَاكِيَةٌ) ، لا أزيد ولا أنقص.
Then the proposition (al-qadiyya), whether it is a simple 'is' proposition or a compound one, is only completed by three things: first, the subject; second, the predicate; and third, a complete declarative depicting relation (nisba tamma ikhbariyya hakiya), without increase or decrease.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage succinctly lists the three essential components of a proposition: the subject (al-mawdu'), the predicate (al-mahmul), and a complete declarative depicting relation (nisba tamma ikhbariyya hakiya). It applies to both simple existential propositions (haliyyat basita) and compound ones. The relation must be 'complete' and 'declarative' and 'depicting' to serve as a proper judgment. This is a foundational definition in Islamic logic.

Key Terms
القضية
النسبة التامة الإخبارية الحاكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Every complete proposition in Islamic logic consists of exactly three elements: subject, predicate, and a complete declarative depicting relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
